Division of the World: Bodies, Substances, and Accidents
Al-Nasafi divides the world and presents Abu Mansur's alternative classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic kalam classification of the world into bodies, substances, and accidents, and the Maturidi refinement into accidents and entities.

Core Idea
Kalam divides the world into three categories, but Maturidi reduces them to two: accidents and entities.

This lesson introduces the fundamental ontological categories in Maturidi theology. Al-Nasafi presents the standard tripartite division, then notes Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's refinement, which reduces the categories to two. This sets the stage for understanding how kalam analyzes creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between the two classifications.
The passage is short but packs two views.
2
Identify the standard division
Note the three terms: bodies (ajsām), substances (jawāhir), and accidents (a‘rāḍ). Understand that 'world' here means all contingent things that are not God.
3
Understand al-Maturidi's adjustment
He says there are only two categories: accidents (a‘rāḍ) and entities (a‘yān). Bodies are included in substances because a body is a substance that occupies space. So bodies are a subset of entities.
The term a‘yān refers to self-subsisting entities.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows that Maturidi kalam is not merely repeating earlier categories; it refines them for clearer ontological analysis. Ask yourself: why would reducing to two categories be useful?
It simplifies the analysis of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينقسم العالم إلى أجسام، وجواهر، وأعراض. إلا أن الشيخ أبا منصور يرى أن الأقسام أعراض، وأعيان؛ لأن الأجسام داخله في الجواهر.
And the world is divided into bodies, substances, and accidents. Except that the Shaykh Abu Mansur holds that the divisions are accidents and entities; because bodies are included in substances.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the standard division of the world into three categories—bodies, substances, and accidents—but then notes that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi differs by reducing the classification to two: accidents and entities. He considers bodies to be a subset of substances (entities), not a separate category. This reflects the Maturidi view that the fundamental constituents are either self-subsisting (entities) or non-self-subsisting (accidents).

Key Terms
العالم
أجسام
جواهر
أعراض
أعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies are a separate ontological category from substances.
Correction: Al-Maturidi includes bodies within substances (entities) because a body is simply a substance with dimensions. So the fundamental division is between entities (self-subsisting) and accidents (non-self-subsisting).

Checkpoint
According to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, how many fundamental categories does the world consist of?
A) Three: bodies, substances, accidents
B) Two: accidents and entities
C) Two: bodies and accidents
D) Three: atoms, bodies, accidents

Key Takeaway
The world consists fundamentally of either self-subsisting entities (a‘yān) or non-self-subsisting accidents (a‘rāḍ), according to Maturidi theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that everything in the world is either a self-subsisting entity or an accident dependent on it. Look at your own daily concerns—your reputation, your wealth, your health. Are you treating any of them as if they are self-subsisting entities when they are actually accidents? What would change if you truly saw them as dependent on Allah? Write down one specific action you will take today to shift your reliance from an accident to the Creator.
?
Abu Mansur simplified the divisions by recognizing that bodies are included in substances. Is there an aspect of your life where you cling to a false distinction—something you think is separate when it is actually part of a larger reality? For example, do you separate your 'religious' life from your 'everyday' life? How will you integrate that division starting this week?
?
The passage shows that scholars refined their understanding of creation over time. What is one belief you hold about the world that you have never examined? Commit to investigating it with the same rigor Maturidi applied—ask: 'Is this truly fundamental, or is it a subset of something deeper?'

Defining Accident, Entity, and the Division of Bodies
Al-Nasafi defines accident and entity, then classifies entities into atom and body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's definitions of accident and entity, and his classification of entities into indivisible atoms and composite bodies.

Core Idea
Accidents depend on entities; entities are either simple atoms or composite bodies.

Al-Nasafi begins by establishing the foundational distinction between what can and cannot subsist by itself. This sets the stage for discussing the nature of substances and bodies, which is essential for later arguments about God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and identify how al-Nasafi defines 'accident' and 'entity'. Notice that accidents cannot subsist by themselves, while entities can.
The term 'accident' here refers to non-substantial attributes, not random events.
2
Examine the discussion with other schools
Al-Nasafi engages with al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila. Understand that he is asserting his own view after considering other positions.
3
Learn the classification of entities
Entities are divided into non-composite (the indivisible atom) and composite (the body). Note the definitions of each.
The indivisible atom is the basic building block in kalam physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبين النسفي أن العرض ما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه أما الأعيان فتقوم بنفسها. ويناقش في هذا الأشعري، وبعض المتكلمين والمعتزلة، ليرجح كلامه. ليبطل إذًا ثم يبين أن الأعيان تنقسم إلى غير مركب وهو الجوهر الفرد. وإلى مركب وهو الجسم، ويحدد معنى كل من الجوهر والجسم، ويناقش تعريفات المتكلمين وهو في مناقشته يقول برأي الماتريدي - إن الله سبحانه وتعالى لا يطلق عليه لفظ الجسم لما أن الجسم اسم لذي الجهات، وذي النهايات، وذي الأبعاد. وهو بذلك يرد على هشام بن الحكم الذي يقول: - لا موجود إلا وهو جسم، والله موجود هو جسم ـــ وعلى الكرامية الذين يقولون: - إن الله تعالى جسم لأنه قائم بالذات
And al-Nasafi clarifies that the accident is what cannot subsist by itself, while entities subsist by themselves. And he discusses this with al-Ash'ari and some mutakallimun and Mu'tazila to give preponderance to his statement. Thus he nullifies. Then he clarifies that entities are divided into non-composite, which is the indivisible atom, and into composite, which is the body. And he defines the meaning of each of substance and body, and he discusses the definitions of the mutakallimun, and in his discussion he states the view of al-Maturidi that Allah, exalted and high, is not called body because body is a name for what possesses directions, ends, and dimensions. And by this he refutes Hisham ibn al-Hakam who says: 'There is no existent except it is a body, and Allah is existent and He is a body'—and also the Karramiyya who say that Allah is a body because He is self-subsistent.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage first defines accident as that which cannot subsist by itself, and entity as that which can. Al-Nasafi then discusses this with Ash'ari and others to argue for his position. He then divides entities into two: the simple indivisible substance (atom) and the composite (body), defining each. He then applies this to the nature of God, stating the Maturidi view that Allah cannot be called a body because the term 'body' implies having directions, ends, and dimensions—attributes that do not apply to God. He uses this to refute Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the Karramiyya, who affirm that God is a body.

Key Terms
العرض
الأعيان
الجوهر الفرد
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is an accident?
A) Something that subsists by itself
B) Something that cannot subsist by itself
C) A composite body
D) An indivisible atom

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defines accident as non-self-subsistent and entity as self-subsistent, then divides entities into indivisible atoms and composite bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that accidents are things that cannot subsist by themselves. Now look at your own life: how many of the things you rely on daily—your health, your wealth, your relationships—are accidents? If they are, what does that mean for your ultimate security? Will you commit today to relying more on the only Self-Subsistent Entity (Allah) and less on these temporary accidents?
?
Your body is a composite (jism) made of atoms that will one day disintegrate. But your soul—if it is a simple substance—may be indivisible. Are you living as though your body will last forever? What one change can you make today to prepare your indivisible substance for its meeting with the Self-Subsistent Creator?

Refuting Corporealism: Why God Is Not a Body
Al-Nasafi refutes corporealism by stating the Maturidi view and refuting two groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school rejects calling God a body, and how al-Nasafi refutes Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the Karramiyya.

Core Idea
God is not a body because a body implies direction, ends, and dimensions.

After establishing the classification of entities, al-Nasafi applies it to the nature of God. He argues that since a body is defined by having directions, ends, and dimensions, and God transcends all such limitations, the term 'body' cannot be applied to Him. He then refutes two groups who affirm God is a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi position
Read the Arabic passage and note that al-Nasafi states the Maturidi view: God is not called a body because body implies directions, ends, and dimensions.
This is a key point of Maturidi theology.
2
Understand the refutation of Hisham ibn al-Hakam
Hisham argued that every existent is a body, therefore God is a body. Al-Nasafi refutes this by implication: God's existence is unique and not subject to bodily limitations.
3
Understand the refutation of the Karramiyya
The Karramiyya argued that God is self-subsistent, and all self-subsistent things are bodies, so God is a body. Al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that self-subsistence does not entail corporeality.
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why it is important for tawhid that God is not comparable to created bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبين النسفي أن العرض ما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه أما الأعيان فتقوم بنفسها. ويناقش في هذا الأشعري، وبعض المتكلمين والمعتزلة، ليرجح كلامه. ليبطل إذًا ثم يبين أن الأعيان تنقسم إلى غير مركب وهو الجوهر الفرد. وإلى مركب وهو الجسم، ويحدد معنى كل من الجوهر والجسم، ويناقش تعريفات المتكلمين وهو في مناقشته يقول برأي الماتريدي - إن الله سبحانه وتعالى لا يطلق عليه لفظ الجسم لما أن الجسم اسم لذي الجهات، وذي النهايات، وذي الأبعاد. وهو بذلك يرد على هشام بن الحكم الذي يقول: - لا موجود إلا وهو جسم، والله موجود هو جسم ـــ وعلى الكرامية الذين يقولون: - إن الله تعالى جسم لأنه قائم بالذات
And al-Nasafi clarifies that the accident is what cannot subsist by itself, while entities subsist by themselves. And he discusses this with al-Ash'ari and some mutakallimun and Mu'tazila to give preponderance to his statement. Thus he nullifies. Then he clarifies that entities are divided into non-composite, which is the indivisible atom, and into composite, which is the body. And he defines the meaning of each of substance and body, and he discusses the definitions of the mutakallimun, and in his discussion he states the view of al-Maturidi that Allah, exalted and high, is not called body because body is a name for what possesses directions, ends, and dimensions. And by this he refutes Hisham ibn al-Hakam who says: 'There is no existent except it is a body, and Allah is existent and He is a body'—and also the Karramiyya who say that Allah is a body because He is self-subsistent.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage first defines accident as that which cannot subsist by itself, and entity as that which can. Al-Nasafi then discusses this with Ash'ari and others to argue for his position. He then divides entities into two: the simple indivisible substance (atom) and the composite (body), defining each. He then applies this to the nature of God, stating the Maturidi view that Allah cannot be called a body because the term 'body' implies having directions, ends, and dimensions—attributes that do not apply to God. He uses this to refute Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the Karramiyya, who affirm that God is a body.

Key Terms
الجسم
الماتريدي
هشام بن الحكم
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming that God is a body simply because He is self-subsistent.
Correction: Self-subsistence does not require being a body; God's self-subsistence is unique and free from bodily limitations.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why is God not a body?
A) Because God is not self-subsistent
B) Because body implies having directions, ends, and dimensions
C) Because God is indivisible
D) Because God is an accident

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school rejects calling God a body because the term 'body' implies spatial limitations incompatible with divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as occupying a space—like being 'above' or 'in heaven' in a physical sense? After reading this, how does the Maturidi position change how you understand Allah's closeness? Will you now actively correct such thoughts when they arise?
?
The Karramiyya argued that because Allah is self-subsistent, He must be like other self-subsistent things (bodies). Where in your own life do you assume that Allah operates like His creation? What specific assumption do you need to let go of after this lesson?
?
Hisham ibn al-Hakam said every existent is a body. Do you ever treat Allah as if He is 'one more thing' among other existents—limited by your own concepts? What would it mean for your worship to truly believe that Allah's existence is incomparable? Commit to one concrete change in how you speak about Allah today.

Al-Nasafi's Critique of Ash'ari's Definition of Accident
Al-Nasafi critiques Ash'ari's definition of accident as composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi considered al-Ash'ari's definition of accident contradictory and how it violated Ash'ari's own principles.

Core Idea
A definition cannot be composed of two distinct descriptions for a simple concept.

Al-Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's definition of accident as something that inheres in a substrate and ceases without the substrate's cessation. While admitting it is valid, he points out a fatal flaw: al-Ash'ari composed the definition from two descriptions, contradicting his own methodological commitment to simplicity. This lesson unpacks that critique and its significance in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the two parts: definition and critique.
The passage is concise; notice the shift from presentation to evaluation.
2
Identify the two components of Ash'ari's definition
Al-Ash'ari defines accident as 'what inheres in something else and ceases without the cessation of its substrate.' Identify the two descriptions: inherence and cessation.
These are the two 'descriptions' al-Nasafi refers to.
3
Understand why it is contradictory
Al-Nasafi says it is composed of two descriptions, which contradicts al-Ash'ari's own principle that definitions of simple entities should be simple.
This is an internal critique—Ash'ari violates his own rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينتقل بعد ذلك إلى الحديث عن العرض، فيقدم تعريفه في اللغة، وعند المتكلمين عارضا تعريفات كثيرة ، ويذكر أن أبا الحسن الأشعري قال ـ إنه ما يعترض على غيره ويبطل من غير بطلان محله ويقول النسفي: إنه حد صحيح، إلا أن الأشعرى ناقض أصله حيث جعله مركبا من وصفين ويرد على من يقول: - إن العرض هو ما لا يستغني في وجوده عن محل - قائلا: - إن هذا لا يستقيم إلا إذا أريد بالوجود الحدوث، أما مطلق الوجود فهو منتقض بصفات الله تعالى، فلا يستقيم القول السابق إلا على أصول المعتزلة -
And after this he moves to discussing the accident, presenting its definition in language and among the mutakallimun, presenting many definitions. And he mentions that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: 'It is what inheres in something else and ceases without the cessation of its substrate.' And al-Nasafi says: 'It is a valid definition, except that al-Ash'ari contradicted his own principle by making it composed of two descriptions.' And he refutes those who say: 'The accident is that which does not dispense with a substrate in its existence,' saying: 'This is only valid if by existence is meant coming-to-be (huduth). As for absolute existence, it is contradicted by the attributes of Allah, so the previous statement is only valid according to the principles of the Mu'tazila.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage examines the definition of accident. Al-Nasafi first presents al-Ash'ari's definition: an accident is what inheres in something else and ceases without the substrate's cessation. Al-Nasafi acknowledges it as valid but points out a contradiction: al-Ash'ari made the definition composed of two descriptions (inherence and cessation), which violates his own principle of avoiding composed definitions for simple concepts. Then al-Nasafi refutes another definition: 'the accident is what does not dispense with a substrate in its existence.' He argues that this definition only works if 'existence' means 'origination' (huduth), because God's attributes also do not dispense with a substrate (God's essence) but are not accidents. Therefore, the definition is only acceptable to the Mu'tazila, who deny God's attributes.

Key Terms
العرض
الأشعري
حد
وصفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's definition of accident is entirely correct and consistent with his own methodology.
Correction: Al-Nasafi points out that al-Ash'ari's definition is composed of two descriptions, which contradicts his own principle of simplicity, making it self-defeating.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the problem with al-Ash'ari's definition of accident?
A) It is too broad
B) It is composed of two descriptions
C) It is not based on language
D) It excludes accidents of the soul

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi exposes an internal contradiction: al-Ash'ari's definition of accident violates his own rule of simplicity by being composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to examine your own theological beliefs for internal contradictions, even if they come from respected scholars? What would it take for you to admit an inconsistency?
?
Al-Nasafi respectfully critiqued al-Ash'ari despite his stature. Do you prioritize truth over personalities in your own learning? Can you point to a specific instance where you did?
?
When you hold a belief that seems valid but later realize it contradicts a core principle you accept, what will you do? Will you ignore it, rationalize it, or seek resolution—and why?

Refuting the Definition of Accident as Dependent on a Substrate
Al-Nasafi refutes the definition of accident as non-self-subsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi rejects the definition of accident as 'what does not dispense with a substrate in its existence' and how it only applies to temporal accidents, not divine attributes.

Core Idea
A definition of accident must distinguish temporal accidents from eternal divine attributes.

Al-Nasafi turns to refute a common definition: the accident is what cannot exist independently of a substrate. He argues that this definition is only valid if 'existence' means 'temporal origination' (huduth), because God's eternal attributes also depend on His essence but are not accidents. Thus, the definition only works for the Mu'tazila, who deny God's attributes. This lesson explores that refutation and its sectarian implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic refutation and its translation. Note the conditional phrasing.
Al-Nasafi uses a refutation structure: 'He refutes those who say... saying...'
2
Understand the key distinction
Al-Nasafi distinguishes between absolute existence (wujud mutlaq) and coming-to-be (huduth). The definition only works if existence means huduth.
This is crucial because divine attributes exist absolutely but are not accidents.
3
See the sectarian implication
The definition aligns with Mu'tazilite theology, which denies eternal attributes. Al-Nasafi uses this to show the definition is not universally valid.
The Mu'tazila hold that God's attributes are identical to His essence, so they do not need a substrate in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينتقل بعد ذلك إلى الحديث عن العرض، فيقدم تعريفه في اللغة، وعند المتكلمين عارضا تعريفات كثيرة ، ويذكر أن أبا الحسن الأشعري قال ـ إنه ما يعترض على غيره ويبطل من غير بطلان محله ويقول النسفي: إنه حد صحيح، إلا أن الأشعرى ناقض أصله حيث جعله مركبا من وصفين ويرد على من يقول: - إن العرض هو ما لا يستغني في وجوده عن محل - قائلا: - إن هذا لا يستقيم إلا إذا أريد بالوجود الحدوث، أما مطلق الوجود فهو منتقض بصفات الله تعالى، فلا يستقيم القول السابق إلا على أصول المعتزلة -
And after this he moves to discussing the accident, presenting its definition in language and among the mutakallimun, presenting many definitions. And he mentions that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: 'It is what inheres in something else and ceases without the cessation of its substrate.' And al-Nasafi says: 'It is a valid definition, except that al-Ash'ari contradicted his own principle by making it composed of two descriptions.' And he refutes those who say: 'The accident is that which does not dispense with a substrate in its existence,' saying: 'This is only valid if by existence is meant coming-to-be (huduth). As for absolute existence, it is contradicted by the attributes of Allah, so the previous statement is only valid according to the principles of the Mu'tazila.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage examines the definition of accident. Al-Nasafi first presents al-Ash'ari's definition: an accident is what inheres in something else and ceases without the substrate's cessation. Al-Nasafi acknowledges it as valid but points out a contradiction: al-Ash'ari made the definition composed of two descriptions (inherence and cessation), which violates his own principle of avoiding composed definitions for simple concepts. Then al-Nasafi refutes another definition: 'the accident is what does not dispense with a substrate in its existence.' He argues that this definition only works if 'existence' means 'origination' (huduth), because God's attributes also do not dispense with a substrate (God's essence) but are not accidents. Therefore, the definition is only acceptable to the Mu'tazila, who deny God's attributes.

Key Terms
العرض
الوجود
الحدوث
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition 'accident is what needs a substrate for its existence' is universally valid for all things.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues this definition only works if existence means temporal origination, because God's attributes also need a substrate but are not accidents.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi say the definition of accident as 'non-self-subsistent' is only valid according to the Mu'tazila?
A) Because the Mu'tazila deny divine attributes
B) Because they define existence as temporal origination
C) Because they reject the concept of accidents
D) Because they follow linguistic definitions

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi exposes that the definition of accident as dependent on a substrate only holds for temporal beings, thus it is biased towards Mu'tazilite theology that denies eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a definition that seems straightforward, do you ever pause to check whether it carries hidden assumptions? In your own understanding of Islam, are there beliefs you hold that you have not examined for unspoken biases?
?
This refutation shows that a definition can be loaded with theological commitments. Think about a concept you believe strongly: what presuppositions are you assuming about it that might not be shared by someone from a different school?
?
Al-Nasafi forces you to see that even a technical definition like 'accident' is not neutral. Where in your own life have you uncritically accepted a definition or label that actually shapes your worldview without your awareness? What will you do to uncover those assumptions?

Refutation of Infinite Divisibility and the Dirariyya-Najjariyya View
Al-Nasafi refutes infinite divisibility and presents the Dirariyya-Najjariyya view on bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key discussions in kalam: the refutation of infinite divisibility and the view that bodies are aggregated accidents, along with al-Maturidi's stance.

Core Idea
Kalam rejects infinite divisibility and considers bodies as aggregates of accidents.

Al-Nasafi presents two important topics. First, he refutes the philosophers and al-Nazzam who claimed matter is infinitely divisible. Second, he introduces the Dirariyya and Najjariyya view that bodies are composed of accidents, noting that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi inclined toward this view. This passage shows the diversity of opinions within kalam on the nature of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two main ideas: refutation of infinite divisibility and the accident-aggregate view.
2
Identify the two topics
Notice that the passage has two parts: first a refutation, then a presentation of a sectarian view. Mark where one ends and the other begins.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Nasafi refutes the philosophers and al-Nazzam who argued for infinite divisibility. Consider why this refutation matters for kalam's atomism.
The standard kalam position affirms indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard).
4
Examine the Dirariyya-Najjariyya view
They held that bodies are nothing but aggregates of accidents, not substances. This is a minority view.
5
Reflect on al-Maturidi's inclination
Al-Nasafi reports that Abu Mansur inclined to this view but did not adopt it as a formal school. Think about what this tells us about scholarly openness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد هذا ينتقل إلى مناقشة الفلاسفة، والنظام في قولهم – بالجزء الذي يتجزأ إلى ما لا نهاية ، ويبطل مزاعمهم بطريقة موضوعية. ويعرض كلام الضرارية، والنجارية - أن الأجسام أعراض مجتمعة إلا أنه لا ينتهي إلى رأي بل يقول - إن أبا منصور مال إلى هذا وإن لم يعتقده مذهبا.
And after this he moves to discussing the philosophers and al-Nazzam regarding their statement about the part that is divisible infinitely, and he refutes their claims in an objective manner. And he presents the statement of the Dirariyya and Najjariyya that bodies are aggregated accidents, but he does not settle on an opinion; rather he says that Abu Mansur inclined to this although he did not adopt it as a school.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage covers two topics. First, al-Nasafi refutes the philosophers and al-Nazzam who argued that matter is infinitely divisible. He refutes them objectively, defending the view that there is an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). Second, he presents the view of the Dirariyya and Najjariyya sects that bodies are nothing but aggregates of accidents. Al-Nasafi does not explicitly endorse this view but notes that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi inclined toward it, though he did not formally adopt it as a doctrine.

Correction: The Dirariyya and Najjariyya held that bodies are accident aggregates, and al-Maturidi himself inclined to this view, showing diversity within kalam.

Al-Nasafi refutes infinite divisibility and presents a minority view on bodies, showing the breadth of theological debate and al-Maturidi's open-mindedness.

Defining 'Al-Ghayrayn' - The Two Others
Al-Nasafi defines 'two others' as existents whose nonexistence is possible for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's precise definition of 'two others' and why this definition is crucial for the debate on divine attributes.

Core Idea
Two things are 'others' only if each can possibly exist without the other.

Al-Nasafi opens by stating that he could avoid defining the term 'the two others,' but he engages voluntarily to clarify the matter. The Mu'tazila have claimed that God's attributes, if real, would be 'others' to His essence, which is impossible. To refute them, al-Nasafi must first establish what 'otherness' truly means. In this lesson, we will examine his definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the passage where al-Nasafi explains that he is not obliged to define the two others, but does so voluntarily.
Notice his tone: he is refuting a claim, not starting from scratch.
2
Identify the definition
Find the sentence where al-Nasafi defines al-ghayrayn. Write it down.
The definition is: 'the two existents such that the existence of the non-existence of the other is possible.'
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this definition prevents divine attributes from being considered 'others' to God's essence. Attributes are eternal and cannot cease to exist.
This is the key move: otherness requires possibility of non-existence, which is absent for God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رده عليهم يقول كعادته - التي أحسب أنه لم يلتزم بها منهجيا حينما اشترط على نفسه الإيجاز : لا حاجة بنا إلى الاشتغال بتحديد الغيرين، لأن الخصوم هم الذين يريدون نفي الصفات بعلة أنها لو كانت ثابتة لكانت أغيارا للذات وإثبات غير الله تعالى في الأزل محال، ويقول : إنه لن يشتغل بمنع ذلك إلا على طريقة التبرع، فيحدد الغيرين أنهما الموجودان اللذان يصح وجود عدم الآخر، وأن شيئا مما ذكره الخصوم لا يصلح أن يكون حدا، لأنهما كانا غيرين، لأنهما عرضان لثبوت المغايرة بين الباري، وبين العالم، ولا لأنهما جسمان، لثبوت التغاير بين الأعراض والأجسام، وبين الأجسام والأجسام، وما كانا غيرين، لقيام مغايرة بينهما لجريان التغاير بين الأعراض، وإن كان يستحيل قيام المعنى بها، ولأن المغايرة لو كانت معنى لكانت هي أيضا غير الأجسام وما وراءها من أعراض فتقوم بها مغايرة أخرى ثم كذلك إلى ما لا نهاية، وكل هذه الأقسام ممتنعة، كما ذكر النسفي قبل ذلك.
And in his refutation of them he says, as is his habit—which I think he did not adhere to methodologically when he obliged himself to be brief: 'We have no need to occupy ourselves with defining the two others (al-ghayrayn), because the opponents are the ones who want to negate the attributes with the reason that if they were established they would be others (aghyār) for the essence, and affirming other than God most high in pre-eternity is impossible.' And he says: 'He will not occupy himself with preventing that except as a voluntary favor.' So he defines the two others: they are two existents such that the existence of the non-existence of the other is possible. And none of what the opponents mentioned is valid as a definition, because they were two others because they are accidents for the establishment of otherness between the Creator and the world, nor because they are bodies for the establishment of difference between accidents and bodies and between bodies and bodies. And they were two others because of the existence of otherness between them due to the running of difference among accidents, even though it is impossible for the meaning to subsist in them. And because if otherness were a meaning, it would itself be other than bodies and what is beyond them of accidents, and another otherness would subsist in it, and then on to infinity. And all these divisions are impossible, as al-Nasafi mentioned before that.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation of the concept of 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya) as used by the Mu'tazila to argue that attributes are other than God's essence. Al-Nasafi begins by noting that he could ignore this topic entirely, but he engages voluntarily. He defines 'two others' (al-ghayrayn) as two existents such that it is possible for the non-existence of one to occur while the other exists. This definition shows that attributes cannot be other than God because they are eternal and cannot cease to exist. He then refutes the Mu'tazila's attempts to define otherness: (1) they cannot say that two things are other because they are accidents, since accidents themselves differ; (2) nor because they are bodies, since there is difference between bodies; (3) nor because otherness is a meaning (ma'nā) subsisting in them, because that would create an infinite regress—if otherness itself were a meaning, it would itself be other than bodies and accidents, requiring another otherness, ad infinitum. Thus all proposed definitions fail.

Key Terms
غير
الغيرين
أغيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two distinct things are automatically 'others'.
Correction: Al-Nasafi defines 'others' specifically as existents whose non-existence is possible for each, which excludes eternal existents like God's attributes.

Checkpoint
What is al-Nasafi's definition of 'two others' (al-ghayrayn)?
A) Two things that differ in their essences.
B) Two existents such that the existence of the non-existence of the other is possible.
C) Two things that are separate in space.
D) Two things that are caused by different agents.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defines 'two others' as two existents each of which can possibly not exist; this definition prevents divine attributes from being considered other than God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes like mercy or power, do you ever subconsciously imagine them as separate entities added to His essence? How might al-Nasafi's definition of 'otherness' challenge that tendency and purify your understanding of tawhid?
?
The Mu'tazila saw affirming attributes as compromising God's oneness. In your own life, do you sometimes treat God's attributes as if they were independent qualities, forgetting that they are one with His essence? What changes when you truly internalize that His mercy is not a separate thing but His very being?
?
Al-Nasafi defines 'two others' as existents that could cease to exist. What created things in your life do you cling to as if they are eternal? How would your heart change if you saw everything except God as a possible 'non-existent'?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Definitions of Otherness
Al-Nasafi refutes three Mu'tazilite attempts to define otherness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's refutation of three attempted definitions of otherness proposed by the Mu'tazila, including the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
Otherness cannot be defined by accident, body, or a subsisting meaning — all lead to absurdity.

After defining 'two others' correctly, al-Nasafi turns to refute the definitions that the Mu'tazila implicitly rely on. They might think otherness is simply being accidents (since accidents differ), or being bodies (since bodies differ), or that otherness is a meaning (ma'nā) that subsists in things. Al-Nasafi shows that each of these attempts fails, and the last leads to an endless chain. In this lesson, we will follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
List the opponents' definitions
Read the passage and identify the three definitions that the Mu'tazila propose for otherness.
They are: (1) being accidents, (2) being bodies, (3) a subsisting meaning.
2
First refutation: accidents
Understand why otherness cannot be defined by being accidents: accidents themselves differ, so otherness is not about accidentality.
3
Second refutation: bodies
Understand why otherness cannot be defined by being bodies: difference exists between bodies and between bodies and accidents, so this is not unique.
4
Third refutation: a meaning
Follow the infinite regress argument: if otherness were a meaning, it itself would be other, requiring another otherness, and so on ad infinitum.
This is the decisive refutation.
5
Conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that all these definitions are impossible. Reflect on how this protects the doctrine of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And none of what the opponents mentioned is valid as a definition, because they were two others because they are accidents for the establishment of otherness between the Creator and the world, nor because they are bodies for the establishment of difference between accidents and bodies and between bodies and bodies. And they were two others because of the existence of otherness between them due to the running of difference among accidents, even though it is impossible for the meaning to subsist in them. And because if otherness were a meaning, it would itself be other than bodies and what is beyond them of accidents, and another otherness would subsist in it, and then on to infinity. And all these divisions are impossible, as al-Nasafi mentioned before that.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation of the concept of 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya) as used by the Mu'tazila to argue that attributes are other than God's essence. Al-Nasafi begins by noting that he could ignore this topic entirely, but he engages voluntarily. He defines 'two others' (al-ghayrayn) as two existents such that it is possible for the non-existence of one to occur while the other exists. ?

The Definition of Takwin and Its Synonyms
Author defines takwin and states he follows his predecessors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of takwin and its synonyms as used by Imam Nasafi, and why he affirms it based on his predecessors.

Core Idea
Six terms, one meaning: bringing the non-existent into existence.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi establishes the foundational definition of takwin, a key term in Maturidi kalam. He lists six Arabic terms and equates them, showing they all refer to the same act of divine creation. He then grounds his position in the authority of earlier scholars. Let's dive into the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the six terms listed and their common definition.
The terms appear in a specific order; try to memorize them.
2
Identify the six terms and their meaning
List the six terms and note that the author explicitly states they are synonymous. Their shared meaning is 'bringing the non-existent out of non-existence into existence.'
This is a technical definition for theological discussion.
3
Understand the author's position
Observe that the author affirms takwin in emulation of his predecessors. This shows his adherence to the established scholarly tradition.
It signals that his view is not novel but follows earlier authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرفنا أولا أن التكوين والتخليق والخلق والإيجاد والإحداث، والاختراع أسماء مترادفة، يراد بها كلها إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود، ثم يبين أنه قال بالتكوين اقتداء باختيار سلفه.
He first makes us know that takwīn (bringing into existence), takhlīq (creating), khalq (creation), ījād (causing existence), iḥdāth (origination), and ikhtirā‘ (invention) are synonymous terms, all intended by them to mean bringing the non-existent out of non-existence into existence. Then he explains that he said [i.e., affirmed] takwīn in emulation of the choice of his predecessors.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a statement of position. The author begins by listing six Arabic terms — takwīn, takhlīq, khalq, ījād, iḥdāth, and ikhtirā‘ — and declares that they are synonymous (asmā’ mutarādifa). Their single shared meaning is ‘bringing the non-existent out of non-existence into existence’ (ikhrāj al-ma‘dūm min al-‘adam ilā al-wujūd). This is a precise technical definition that fixes the term. Then the author states that he affirms takwīn (qāla bi-l-takwīn) in emulation of the choice of his predecessors (iqtidā’an bi-ikhtiyār salafihī). This immediately signals that his position is not an innovation but a continuation of earlier authoritative figures.

التخليق (takhlīq)
الخلق (khalq)
الإيجاد (ījād)
الإحداث (iḥdāth)
الاختراع (ikhtirā‘)
إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود (ikhrāj al-ma‘dūm min al-‘adam ilā al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these terms have different theological nuances, such as takhlīq referring to creation from nothing vs. ījād referring to causing existence from pre-existing matter.
Correction: The author explicitly equates them as synonymous for this discussion, all meaning bringing the non-existent into existence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The six terms are synonymous and all mean bringing the non-existent into existence; Nasafi affirms takwin following his predecessors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Division from Definition in Logic
Distinguishes between classification and definition, criticizes confusing them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conceptual difference between division (taqsīm) and definition (taḥdīd), and why confusing them indicates a superficial understanding of logical methods.

Core Idea
Division and definition move in opposite directions: one from particulars to universals, the other from universals to particulars.

In this brief but powerful passage, Imam al-Nasafi offers a crisp logical insight: division and definition are not interchangeable tools. He explains their distinct purposes and the technical terms used by logicians for each. This lesson will help you see why mixing them up undermines sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then a distinction, then a critique.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two logical processes
Find where each process is described: division (taqsīm) for knowing universals from particulars, definition (taḥdīd) for knowing particulars from universals.
These are opposite directions of reasoning.
3
Learn the technical names
Note that division corresponds to induction (istiqrāʾ) and definition corresponds to proof (burhān) in Aristotelian logic.
These terms recur in later kalam discussions.
4
Understand the criticism
The author criticizes anyone who treats these two as the same subject, calling them deficient in knowledge of realities.
This is a warning against methodological confusion.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Ask yourself: why does it matter to keep these distinct? How does this apply to the study of theology?
Precision in method leads to sound conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على بطلان الحدود المقسمة، أن التقسيم وضع لمعرفة الكليات بواسطة الجزئيات، ويسمى هذا عند أهل المنطق استقراء، والتحديد وضع لمعرفة الجزئيات بوساطة الكليات، ويسمى هذا برهانا، فمن جعل الأمرين بابا واحدا فهو قليل الحظ من العلم بالحقائق.
And what indicates the invalidity of the divided definitions is that division is set for knowing universals by means of particulars, and this is called by the people of logic induction (istiqrāʾ), and definition is set for knowing particulars by means of universals, and this is called proof (burhān). So whoever makes the two matters one door is one of little share in knowledge of realities.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a separate proof for the invalidity of dividing definitions. It distinguishes between two logical processes: division (taqsīm) and definition (taḥdīd). Division is used to know universals from particulars, known as induction (istiqrāʾ). Definition is used to know particulars from universals, known as proof (burhān). The author argues that conflating these two distinct methods shows ignorance of true knowledge. Thus, definitions that are actually divisions are invalid.

Key Terms
التقسيم
التحديد
الكلية
الجزئية
الاستقراء
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think division and definition are interchangeable or part of the same logical operation.
Correction: As the passage clarifies, they move in opposite directions: division goes from particulars to universals (induction), definition goes from universals to particulars (proof). Mixing them shows a lack of understanding.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of division (taqsīm)?
A) To know particulars by means of universals.
B) To know universals by means of particulars.
C) To prove the existence of God.
D) To define the science of kalam.

Key Takeaway
Division and definition are opposite logical movements, and confusing them reveals a superficial grasp of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mechanically combined two distinct ideas just because they seemed similar, without examining their underlying direction? This lesson warns that such confusion shows 'little share in knowledge of realities.' Where in your own study of Islam have you conflated the inductive and deductive, and how might that have weakened your understanding?
?
The author differentiates between induction (from particulars to universals) and proof (from universals to particulars). In your daily learning, do you actively check which direction your reasoning takes? When you learn a new aqeedah concept, are you careful to distinguish whether it is derived from observed particulars or applied from universal principles?
?
Consider your own thought habits: Are you more comfortable with examples (particulars) or rules (universals)? How might an imbalance affect the way you process theological proofs? What will you do this week to consciously practice both inductive and deductive reasoning in your study of Islam?

Two Proofs that Knowledge is Not Belief
Two proofs and a supplementary point against defining knowledge as belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments establishing that knowledge is not identical to belief, using the impossibility of belief for Allah.

Core Idea
Knowledge and belief are distinct because God is Knowing but cannot have belief.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents two concise proofs that knowledge is not the same as belief. He then adds a third point revealing the motive behind those who define knowledge as belief: to deny God's knowledge. By following his reasoning, we see how theological definitions are tested against what we know about Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the structure of two proofs and a third point become clear.
The passage uses 'أحدهما' (the first) and 'والآخر' (the second) to mark the proofs.
2
Identify the two proofs
Notice the first proof: if knowledge were belief, then since Allah cannot have belief, He could not be Knowing—which is false. The second proof: since Allah is proven Knowing, if knowledge were belief, He would have belief—also false.
Both proofs rely on the impossibility of belief for Allah.
3
Understand the third point
Read the final sentence: opponents define knowledge as belief specifically to deny Allah's knowledge, because belief is impossible for Him. Their own definition backfires when Allah's knowledge is established.
This reveals the motive behind a wrong definition.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the opponents' definition is invalid because it leads to contradiction with necessary truths about Allah.
The term 'تحديد' means definition or delimitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الله تعالى لما استحال أن يوصف بكونه معتقدا، استحال أن يوصف بكونه عالما، وهذا محال. والآخر: أنه تعالى لما كان عالما بدليل الشرع والعقل كان معتقدا، وهو أيضا فاسد، وثبت أنه عالم وليس بمعتقد، فدل أن العلم ليس هو الاعتقاد. والآخر: أنهم إنما يجعلون العلم اعتقادا ليتمكنوا من دفع علم الله تعالى، لاستحالة اتصافه تعالى بالاعتقاد، وحيث ثبت بالدلائل الموجبة أن الله تعالى له علم، على ما نبين في مسألة الصفات، ويستحيل أن يكون ذلك العلم اعتقادا، كان ذلك دليلا على بطلان هذا التحديد.
After this we state the discussion from two aspects: One: that since it is impossible for Allah to be described as having belief (i'tiqad), it is impossible for Him to be described as knowing, and this is absurd. And the other: that since He is knowing by the evidence of revelation and reason, He would be having belief, which is also corrupt; and it is established that He is knowing and not having belief, so it indicates that knowledge is not belief. And the other [aspect]: that they only make knowledge belief in order to be able to deny Allah's knowledge, due to the impossibility of His being described with belief; and since it is established by necessary evidences that Allah has knowledge, as we will clarify in the question of attributes, and it is impossible for that knowledge to be belief, that becomes a proof of the invalidity of this definition.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who define knowledge as belief (i'tiqad). The author presents two main proofs and a supplementary point. The first proof: If God cannot be described as having belief, then if knowledge were belief, He could not be described as knowing, which is absurd. The second proof: Since God is known to be knowing by both revelation and reason, if knowledge were belief, God would have belief, which is false. Therefore knowledge is not belief. Then a third point: The opponents define knowledge as belief specifically to deny God's knowledge, because belief is impossible for God. But since God's knowledge is proven, and it cannot be belief, their definition is invalid.

Correction: The author shows they are distinct because Allah is Knowing but cannot be described as having belief (i'tiqad).

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and belief are distinct; the impossibility of belief in Allah proves that knowledge cannot be equated with belief.

Distinguishing 'Ilm from Ma'rifa: Refuting al-Baqillani
Refutation of al-Baqillani and distinction between 'ilm and ma'rifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical Sunni theologians distinguish between 'ilm (knowledge) and ma'rifa (recognition), and why al-Baqillani's definition of 'ilm as 'knowing the known' is rejected.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not mere recognition—Allah is 'alim, not 'arif.

This passage tackles a key debate in Islamic theology: the precise definition of knowledge. Al-Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian, defined 'ilm as 'knowing the known as it is,' equating it with ma'rifa. The author refutes this by showing that Allah is described as 'alim but never as 'arif, and then clarifies the nuanced relationship between the two terms, using linguistic evidence and scholarly consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of al-Baqillani
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author directly challenges al-Baqillani's definition by pointing to the divine names: Allah is 'alim, not 'arif.
This argument relies on consensus and linguistic usage.
2
Examine the objection from the Karramiyya
The Karramiyya sect objected that Allah can be called 'arif just as He is 'alim. See how the author dismisses this by citing consensus and defining ma'rifa as an acquired action.
3
Understand the distinction through poetry and analogy
The author supports the distinction with pre-Islamic poetry showing ma'rifa as recognition after doubt, and draws an analogy to qasd (intention) and irada (will). Reflect on how this clarifies the relationship.
The passage ends with an incomplete explanation; consider what might follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر القاضي أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني من جملة الأشعرية أن العلم هو معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به وهو أيضا فاسد، لأن العلم لو كان معرفة، لكان العالم عارفا، والله تعالى يوصف بأنه عالم، ولا يوصف بأنه عارف. ولا يلتفت إلى قول بعض الكرامية: إن الله تعالى يوصف بأنه عارف كما يوصف بأنه عالم، لاتحاد العلم والمعرفة، لما أن هذا خلاف إجماع المسلمين، ولأن المعرفة اسم للعمل المستحدث، لا لمطلق العلم، يقال: عرفت فلانا، أي استحدثت به علما، كذا ذكره بعض الناس. وقيل: هي الانكشاف عن شيء بعد لبس وتوهم. قال زهير: وقفت بها من بعد عشرين حجة فلأياً عرفت الدار بعد توهم. وقال عنترة: هل غادر الشعراء من متردم أم هل عرفت الدار بعد توهم؟ فعلى هذا نزلت المعرفة من العلم منزلة القصد من الإرادة. وقال بعض أهل العلم بأن العلم اسم شامل على ما يتعلق بالمعنى في الجملة، وما يتعلق به في التفصيل والمعرفة اسم لما يتعلق بالمعنى في التفصيل، بيانه أنك تقول:
Al-Qadi Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani, from among the Asha'irah, mentioned that knowledge is knowing the known as it is, and it is also invalid, because if knowledge were knowing, then the knower would be a knower (عارف), but Allah the Exalted is described as knowing (عالم), and He is not described as a knower (عارف). And no attention is paid to the saying of some Karramiyya: 'Allah the Exalted is described as a knower (عارف) just as He is described as knowing (عالم), because knowledge and recognition are one,' because this is contrary to the consensus of the Muslims, and because recognition is a name for acquired action, not for absolute knowledge. It is said: 'I recognized so-and-so,' meaning I acquired knowledge of him. Thus some people mentioned it. And it is said: It is uncovering of something after confusion and assumption. Zuhayr said: 'I stopped at it after twenty years / scarcely did I recognize the abode after assumption.' And 'Antarah said: 'Have the poets left any patch to be sewn? / Or have you recognized the abode after assumption?' On this basis, recognition (ma'rifa) is placed in relation to knowledge as intention (qasd) is placed in relation to will (irada). And some scholars said that knowledge is a comprehensive name for what pertains to meaning in general, and what pertains to it in detail, and recognition is a name for what pertains to meaning in detail. Its explanation: you say:
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes a definition of knowledge ('ilm) by al-Baqillani and then distinguishes 'ilm from ma'rifa, also refuting the Karramiyya. 

The author states that al-Baqillani defines knowledge as 'knowing the known as it is' (ma'rifat al-ma'lum 'ala ma huwa bihi). This definition is deemed invalid because if knowledge were identical to ma'rifa (here translating as 'knowing'), then the one who knows would be called 'arif (knower/recognizer). However, Allah is described as 'alim (knowing) and never as 'arif in the Qur'an and Islamic tradition, indicating a distinction.

The Karramiyya object that Allah can be called 'arif just as He is 'alim, claiming that 'ilm and ma'rifa are the same. The author dismisses this as contrary to Muslim consensus. He then explains that ma'rifa is a term for an acquired action (al-'amal al-mustahdath), not for absolute knowledge. For example, saying 'I recognized so-and-so' implies acquiring new knowledge of him. Others define ma'rifa as 'uncovering after confusion and doubt', citing lines from pre-Islamic poets Zuhayr and 'Antarah to illustrate this nuance.

On this basis, the relationship between ma'rifa and 'ilm is compared to that between qasd (intention) and irada (will): just as intention is a specific instance of will, ma'rifa is a specific instance of 'ilm. Another scholar adds that 'ilm is a comprehensive name covering both general and detailed understanding, while ma'rifa is specifically for detailed understanding. The author then begins to illustrate this with an example ('you say:') but the text breaks off.

المعرفة
العالم
العارف

الباقلاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonyms and can be used interchangeably in reference to Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that 'ilm and ma'rifa are distinct: Allah is described only as 'alim, not 'arif, and ma'rifa involves acquisition and sometimes prior uncertainty.

Checkpoint

B) Because if 'ilm were ma'rifa, then the knower would be called 'arif, but Allah is called 'alim, not 'arif.
C) Because al-Baqillani was not a reliable scholar.
D) Because the definition is too vague.

Key Takeaway
Theologically, 'ilm and ma'rifa are not synonymous; Allah is 'alim not 'arif, and ma'rifa is a specific acquired knowledge.

Distinguishing 'ilm from ma'rifa and the Rule for Divine Names
The author distinguishes 'ilm from ma'rifa and explains why Allah is not called 'arif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and conceptual distinction between 'ilm and ma'rifa, and why divine names are restricted to those with revelatory warrant.

Core Idea
Not all seemingly synonymous terms are permissible for Allah; revelation sets the limits.

This passage by Imam al-Nasafi explores a subtle linguistic difference between two Arabic words for knowledge: 'ilm and ma'rifa. Through a grammatical analysis, he shows that they behave differently with predicates, which then affects whether Allah can be called 'arif. The lesson reveals the important theological principle of tawqif (restriction to revealed terms).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without trying to analyze. Note the verbs used and the examples given.
Focus on the contrast between 'alima Zaydan and 'arafa Zaydan.
2
Understand the grammatical distinction
Notice how both verbs can take a direct object (Zayd), but when a second element (like 'standing') is added, 'alima treats it as part of a predicate while 'arafa treats it as a state. Recognize the implications for definitions.
The distinction invalidates defining one term by the other.
3
Apply the distinction to divine names
See that logically, since Allah's knowledge is detailed, He could be called 'arif. But the author states that permission is withheld because of tawqif – the requirement that divine names come from revelation.
This shows the priority of revelation over pure logic in naming Allah.
4
Reflect on the principle of tawqif
Consider why the scholars insisted on tawqif: it protects Allah from names He did not choose for Himself, even if they seem semantically appropriate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم زيدا، كما تقول : أعرف زيدا، فيتعلقان بذات زيد منفصلا عن خبر يتعلق به. وتقول : علمت زيدا عالما، فيتعلق العلم بمعنى الجملة المركبة من المبتدأ والخبر ولا يقال: عرفت زيدا قائما، إلا إذا أردت بقولك قايما الحال لا الخبر ، فلو تحققت هذه العبارة في الفرق لبطل تحديد أحدهما بالآخر، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يجوز وصف الله تعالى بأنه عارف لتعلق علمه بالمعلومات على التفصيل، إلا أن الامتناع عنه لعدم ورود التوقيف.
I know Zayd, just as you say: I recognize Zayd, so both relate to the essence of Zayd separately from a predicate connected to it. And you say: I knew Zayd to be knowledgeable, so knowledge attaches to the meaning of the compound sentence consisting of subject and predicate, and one does not say: I recognized Zayd standing, unless you mean by your statement 'standing' the state, not the predicate. So if this expression regarding the difference were realized, the definition of one by the other would be invalid. And on this basis, it should be permissible to describe Allah the Exalted as 'arif because His knowledge relates to known things in detail, except that it is avoided due to lack of revelatory warrant.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage makes a grammatical distinction between the verbs 'alima (to know) and 'arafa (to recognize). The author shows that when you say 'I know Zayd' (علمت زيدا) or 'I recognize Zayd' (عرفت زيدا), both relate directly to the essence of Zayd, without a predicate. But when you say 'I knew Zayd to be knowledgeable' (علمت زيدا عالما), knowledge attaches to the compound sentence (subject + predicate). In contrast, 'I recognized Zayd standing' (عرفت زيدا قائما) is only said if 'standing' is a state, not a predicate. If this distinction were fully understood, defining one term by the other would be invalid. The author then applies this to divine attributes: logically, since Allah's knowledge relates to all known things in detail, He could be called 'arif (knower), but this is avoided because the term 'arif has no revelatory warrant (tawqif). So the rule is to restrict divine names to what is revealed, even if a term is logically applicable.

Key Terms
علم
معرفة
عارف
توقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ilm and ma'rifa are completely interchangeable in Arabic, and that any adjective that seems fitting for Allah can be used freely.
Correction: The passage shows a grammatical distinction between the two words, and asserts that divine names are restricted to what is revealed, even if the term seems logically appropriate.

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible to describe Allah as 'arif despite the logical basis?
A) Because 'arif implies deficiency
B) Because there is no revelatory warrant (tawqif)
C) Because 'ilm and ma'rifa are identical
D) Because 'arif only applies to humans

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of calling Allah 'arif illustrates the principle that divine names are not derived from human logic but from revelation (tawqif).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of Ibn Furak's Definition of Knowledge
The author refutes Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge as a perfected-action attribute is too narrow and fails to account for theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge leads to action – knowledge of God is theoretical.

Sanusi here examines and refutes a specific definition of knowledge proposed by Ibn Furak. He shows that knowledge of God and impossibilities does not result in any action, thus the definition is not universal. This lesson helps clarify the nature of knowledge in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Furak's definition
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how Ibn Furak defines knowledge as an attribute that enables perfected action.
The definition is clear but narrow.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author says the definition does not apply universally (لا يطرد). Find this phrase and understand its significance.
The critique is the core of the refutation.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author provides a counterexample: knowledge of God, His attributes, and impossibilities. This knowledge does not lead to any action.
This shows the definition fails to cover all knowledge.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since such knowledge has no effect on action, Ibn Furak's definition is too narrow. Reflect on why this matters for defining knowledge.
Theological knowledge is often theoretical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر أبو بكر بن فورك أن العلم صفة يتأتي بها من القادر أحكام الفعل وإتقانه. وهذا التحديد لا يطرد، فإنا علمنا بالقديم تعالى وبصفاته وبالمحالات، ولا يتأتي بهذا العلم إحكام الفعل وإتقانه، فإذا لا تأثير لهذا النوع من العلم في إحكام الفعل وإتقانه.
And Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned that knowledge is an attribute by which the capable one (al-qadir) achieves the perfection and proficiency of the action. But this definition does not apply universally, for we know about the Eternal Exalted, His attributes, and impossibilities, and by this knowledge the perfection and proficiency of action is not achieved. So this type of knowledge has no effect on the perfection and proficiency of action.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge. Ibn Furak held that knowledge is an attribute (sifa) through which a capable agent (al-qadir) brings about the flawless execution and mastery of an action. The author points out that this definition is not universal (la yattarid): we have knowledge of God (al-qadim ta'ala), His attributes, and impossible things (al-muhalat), yet this knowledge does not result in any action, let alone a perfected one. Therefore, such theoretical knowledge has no effect on action, showing that Ibn Furak's definition is too narrow and fails to cover all instances of knowledge.

Why Knowledge Cannot Be Defined as Idrak (Perception)
The author rejects the definition of knowledge as idrak due to ambiguity and encompassment implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining knowledge as idrak is invalid because the term is ambiguous and one of its meanings—encompassment—cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Idrak is ambiguous and one meaning implies encompassing limits, which is impossible for Allah.

The Ash'ari definition of knowledge as 'perception (idrak) of the known object as it is' may seem plausible, but the author shows it fails because the word 'idrak' is ambiguous. It can mean different things, and one of its meanings—encompassing the boundaries of a thing—is theologically problematic for God. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the author's clear refutation style.
The passage is dense but focused.
2
Identify the multiple meanings of idrak
The author lists four distinct meanings of the word idrak. List them and notice how each is different.
This shows the term is mushtarak (ambiguous).
3
Understand why ambiguity invalidates a definition
For a definition to be precise, the term used must be unambiguous. Since idrak is ambiguous, it cannot serve as a definition of knowledge.
4
Consider the theological implication
One meaning—encompassment (ihata)—cannot apply to Allah because He has no limits. Therefore, Allah is known (yu'lam) but not perceived (yudrak).
This connects to the later discussion on ru'ya.
5
Review the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the definition is invalid on both linguistic and theological grounds.
Note the reference to the upcoming topic of vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض الأشعرية قال: إنه درك المعلوم على ما هو به، وهو فاسد أيضا لأن لفظة الدرك مشتركة يقال : أدركه إذا أحاط به، وأدركه إذا لحق به، وأدركه إذا فهمه، وأدركت الثمار إذا نضجت، وكذا الله تعالى يُعلم ولا يُدرك إذ الإدراك عبارة عن الإحاطة بحدود الشيء ونهايته على ما نبين حقيقة هذا في مسألة الرؤية إن شاء الله تعالى.
And some Ash'aris said: It is the perception (idrak) of the known object as it is. And this is also invalid because the word 'idrak' is ambiguous: one says 'adrakahu' meaning he encompassed it, and 'adrakahu' meaning he caught up with it, and 'adrakahu' meaning he understood it, and 'adrakat al-thimar' meaning they ripened. Likewise, Allah the Exalted is known but not perceived, for 'idrak' signifies encompassing the limits and end of a thing, as we will clarify the reality of this in the issue of vision (ru'ya), God willing.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage critiques another Ash'ari definition that identifies knowledge (ilm) with 'idrak al-ma'lum' (perception of the known object as it is). The author argues that the word 'idrak' is ambiguous (mushtaraka) with multiple meanings: encompassing (ihata), catching up (luhuq), understanding (fahm), and ripening (nuduj). Because of this ambiguity, using it in a definition fails to specify the intended meaning. Moreover, one of its meanings—encompassing the limits and end of a thing—cannot apply to God's knowledge, since God is not encompassed. Hence, Allah is known (yu'lam) but not perceived (yudrak). The author promises to discuss this further in the topic of seeing God (ru'ya). Thus, the definition is invalid due to both ambiguity and theological inapplicability.

إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'idrak' is a neutral, unambiguous term for perception, and that defining knowledge as perception is unproblematic.
Correction: The author shows that 'idrak' is ambiguous, with four distinct meanings, and one of them—encompassment—is theologically inappropriate for God, thus invalidating the definition.

Checkpoint

A) Because it implies God does not know
B) Because idrak has multiple meanings and one of them implies encompassing limits which does not apply to God
C) Because the Ash'aris themselves rejected it
D) Because it is not based on revelation

Key Takeaway
The word idrak is unsuitable for defining knowledge because it is ambiguous and one of its meanings—encompassment—is theologically incompatible with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a word about Allah that unknowingly implies a limitation? Identify one such phrase you commonly use and commit to replacing it with precise language from today's lesson.
?
The author insists that Allah is known but not perceived. Do you truly accept that your mind cannot fully encompass Him, or do you secretly think you have Him figured out? What specific belief about God do you need to repent from today?
?
The ambiguity of 'idrak' shows the danger of loose theological language. What casual statement about Allah's attributes have you made that might diminish His majesty? Write down one concrete change you will make in your speech starting now.

Refutation of al-Isfarayini's Definition of Knowledge as Tabayyun
The author refutes al-Isfarayini's definition of knowledge as tabayyun due to ambiguity and inapplicability to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as tabayyun is rejected on theological and logical grounds.

Core Idea
A definition of knowledge must apply to God and be unambiguous.

In this passage, the author critiques a specific definition of knowledge proposed by Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. He presents two clear objections: one from divine attributes and one from the ambiguity of the term tabayyun. This lesson unpacks each objection and shows why they invalidate the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
Read the passage and locate the definition: knowledge is 'tabayyun al-ma'lum' (clarification of the known). Note that it is attributed to al-Isfarayini.
The definition is mentioned at the beginning.
2
Examine the first objection: divine names
The author points out that God is called 'alim (knowing) but never mutabayyin (clarified). Reflect on why this is a serious issue for a definition intended to cover all instances of knowledge.
This shows the definition does not apply to divine knowledge.
3
Examine the second objection: ambiguity
The term tabayyun has multiple meanings (e.g., 'I knew' vs. 'it became clear'). This ambiguity undermines the purpose of a definition, which is to remove confusion.
Consider how ambiguity defeats the goal of a clear definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله قال في حده إنه تبين المعلوم على ما هو به وقيل: هو اختيار أبي إسحاق الأسفراييني كذا حكي عنه بعض أصحابه من ديارنا وهو فاسد لأن الله تعالى يقال له عالم ولا يقال له متبين ولأن التبين مشترك يقال تبينت الأمر وتبين لي ومن خاصيته اللفظية المشتركة بقاء الالتباس عند سماعها إلى أن يعين المراد بها بالدليل وهذا يضاد الغرض من التحديد وهو الإعلام بحقيقة المحدود على ما مر.
And some of our shaykhs (may God have mercy on them) said in its definition: It is the clarification (tabayyun) of the known object as it is. And it was said: This is the choice of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, as narrated from him by some of his companions from our region. And it is invalid, because Allah the Exalted is called 'alim (knowing) and not called 'mutabayyin (clarified), and because 'tabayyun' is ambiguous: one says 'tabayyantu al-amr' meaning I knew it, and 'tabayyana li' meaning it became clear to me. And one of its properties as an ambiguous verbal form is that confusion remains upon hearing it until the intended meaning is specified by evidence. And this contradicts the purpose of definition, which is to inform of the reality of the defined term, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage critiques the definition of knowledge as 'tabayyun al-ma'lum' (clarification of the known object as it is), attributed to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. The author gives two objections. First, God is described as 'alim (knowing) but never as mutabayyin (clarified) in revelation, so the definition does not correspond to the divine attribute. Second, the word 'tabayyun' itself is ambiguous: it can mean both 'I knew the matter' and 'it became clear to me'. This ambiguity means that upon hearing the term, confusion persists until the intended sense is clarified by evidence. This defeats the very purpose of a definition (hadd), which is to provide clear and immediate understanding of the defined thing (as stated earlier). Thus, the definition fails both on theological and logical grounds.

Correction: The author shows that 'tabayyun' has multiple meanings (e.g., 'I knew' vs. 'it became clear'), making it ambiguous.
