Refutation of al-Asfarayini's Definition of Knowledge
Al-Nasafi presents and refutes al-Asfarayini's definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as 'clarification' is rejected by al-Nasafi and what makes a valid definition in kalam.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be unambiguous and applicable to God.

Al-Nasafi examines a proposed definition of knowledge from al-Asfarayini. He finds it flawed because it uses ambiguous language and cannot describe Allah. This lesson unpacks his critique and shows the standards for theological definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the main argument.
Pay attention to the key terms 'تبين' and 'مشترك'.
2
Identify the definition and its attribution
Find where the definition is stated and who it is attributed to. Notice that it is presented as a choice of al-Asfarayini.
3
Examine the first criticism: divine applicability
Al-Nasafi argues that the term 'clarification' cannot be used for Allah, while the term 'knowing' can. This shows the definition's incompatibility with divine attributes.
Why is it important that definitions apply to God in kalam?
4
Examine the second criticism: ambiguity
The word 'تبين' is ambiguous (مشترك) with multiple meanings. This leads to confusion until evidence specifies the intended meaning.
5
Connect to the purpose of definition
Al-Nasafi concludes that an ambiguous definition contradicts the very purpose of a definition, which is to clearly convey the reality of the defined term.
This ties the argument together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله - قال في حده إنه تبين المعلوم على ما هو به أهل وقيل: هو اختيار أبي إسحاق الأسفراييني كذا حكي عنه بعض أصحابه، من ديارنا، وهو فاسد، لأن الله تعالى يقال له: عالم ولا يقال له: متبين، ولأن التبين مشترك، يقال: تبينت الأمر أي علمت وتبين لي أي ظهر ومن خاصيته اللفظية المشتركة بقاء الالتباس عند سماعها، إلى أن يعين المراد بها بالدليل، وهذا يضاد الغرض من التحديد، وهو الإعلام بحقيقة المحدود على ما مر.
And some of our teachers (may Allah have mercy on them) said in its definition that it is the clarification of the known object as it actually is. It was said: this is the choice of Abu Ishaq al-Asfarayini, as narrated by some of his companions from our region. And it is invalid, because Allah Most High is called 'knowing' and is not called 'one who clarifies'. And because 'clarification' is ambiguous: one says 'I clarified the matter' (i.e., I knew it) and 'it became clear to me' (i.e., it appeared). And one of its characteristics as a shared linguistic term is that confusion remains upon hearing it, until the intended meaning is specified by evidence. And this contradicts the purpose of definition, which is to inform of the reality of the defined term, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents a definition of knowledge proposed by some Maturidi teachers, attributed to Abu Ishaq al-Asfarayini, and then refutes it. The definition states that knowledge is 'the clarification of the known object as it actually is' (تبين المعلوم على ما هو به). Al-Nasafi then offers three criticisms.

First, the word 'clarification' (متبين) cannot be applied to Allah, because Allah is described as 'knowing' (عالم) but not as 'one who clarifies' (متبين). Since the definition must apply to the divine knower, this is a fatal flaw.

Second, the term 'clarification' (تبين) is ambiguous (مشترك) in Arabic: it can mean either the act of coming to know something or the act of something becoming apparent. Because of this ambiguity, the definition fails to provide a clear, single meaning.

Third, because of its ambiguity, the term leaves confusion (التباس) upon hearing it until the intended sense is specified by evidence. But the entire purpose of a definition (حد) is to immediately and clearly convey the reality of the defined term. An ambiguous term undermines that purpose.

Thus, al-Nasafi concludes that this definition is invalid (فاسد) because it does not fulfill the essential requirements of a proper definition in theology.

Key Terms
علم
حد
تبين
مشترك
التباس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any linguistic definition is acceptable for technical theological terms.
Correction: This passage shows that definitions must be unambiguous and applicable to God; common linguistic usage is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi reject the definition of knowledge as 'clarification of the known'?
A) Because it is not found in the Quran.
B) Because it is ambiguous and cannot apply to Allah.
C) Because it contradicts the Ash'ari school.
D) Because it is too brief.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi rejects the definition because it is ambiguous and cannot be predicated of Allah, violating the purpose of a clear definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know Allah,' do you have a clear, unambiguous understanding of what knowledge means? How would your definition of knowledge hold up if tested by al-Nasafi's criteria of being applicable to Allah and free from ambiguity?
?
If you were to define your own core beliefs, would they pass the test of being free from confusion? Are there areas of your faith where you rely on vague terms rather than seeking precise understanding, and what is the cost of that ambiguity?
?
Consider the phrase 'Allah knows everything.' What does that actually mean to you? Are you willing to examine your own conception of divine knowledge with the same rigor al-Nasafi applied to al-Asfarayini's definition, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions?

Al-Ash'ari's Definition of Knowledge and the Circularity Objection
Al-Nasafi relates al-Ash'ari's definition and the circularity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge, the circularity objection raised against it, and the initial response that distinguishes absolute knowledge from true knowledge.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge by the knower and vice versa creates a circularity that requires a careful distinction.

Al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge is a foundational topic in kalam. However, it sparked a serious objection: if knowledge is defined by the knower, and the knower by knowledge, the definitions seem circular. In this lesson, we explore the definition, the objection, and the beginnings of a response that differentiates between absolute and true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three formulations of al-Ash'ari's definition.
Pay attention to the opponents' objection and the analogy.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the circularity objection and the analogy of Zaid and Amr. Why is this considered a vicious circle?
The objection claims that neither knowledge nor knower are properly defined.
3
See the beginning of the response
Note the distinction between absolute (vague) knowledge and true (precise) knowledge. How might this distinction help break the circularity?
The response is incomplete; it continues in a later passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحكي عن الأشعري أن العلم ما أوجب للعالم – أي حقق له بأنه عالم. وبعض أصحابه عبر عن هذا المعنى فقال: العلم صفة تشتق لمن قام به الوصف بأنه عالم، وتارة يقولون: العلم هو الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما، وهذه اللفظة هي اختيار أكثر أصحابه وهو لفظ يحيط بجميع المحدود ويمنع ما وراءه، من الدخول تحته، والخصوم يقولون: هذا منكم تعريف الشيء بما يتعرف هو به، فإنكم إذا سؤلتم عن العلم عرفتموه بالعالم فقلتم: ما أوجب العالم، أو ذكرتم إحدى العبارتين الباقيتين، وهذا تعريف العلم بالعالم، ثم إذا سؤلتم عن العالم، قلتم: العالم من قام به العلم، أو من له العلم، فقد عرفتم العلم بالعالم، والعالم بالعلم، والشيء متى عرف بما يعرف هو به بقي كل واحد منهما مجهولا، كمن قال: جاءني زيد، فقيل له ومن زيد فقال ابن عمرو فقيل له ومن عمرو هذا فقال: أبو زيد، بقي كل واحد منهما مجهولا، ولا تحصل المعرفة بأحدهما. والخصوم يصولون على الأشعرية بهذا الاعتراض، وما يمكن أن يدفع به هذا الإشكال، ويتخلص عن هذه الاستحالة بأن يقال: إن هذا الاعتراض صدر عن الجهل بما وضع له الحد، وبالوقوف على ما وضع له التحديد، يظهر بطلانه، وبيان هذا: أن العلم بأمر ما قد يكون علما به مطلقا مبهما من غير وقوف من علمه على الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز المعلوم عن غيره مما يناسبه أو يقاربه، وقد يكون علما حقيقيا، وقف من علمه على الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز المعلوم عما يناسبه أو يقاربه، كعلم عامة الخلق أن زيدا آدمي، وهو علم ثابت على طريق التيقن، لا ارتياب للعالم فيه، ولا اضطراب لقلبه، ولا اعتراض عليه للشكوك، ولا اعتراء للخواطر الموجبة للتردي، ثم التيقن هو مع هذا علم بمهم مطلق لا وقوف للعامي على الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز الآدمي عما يناسبه في القوة الحيوانية، ويقاربه في اشتراكه إياه في العقل
And what is transmitted from al-Ash'ari is that knowledge is what necessitates for the knower—that is, makes it true that he is knowing. Some of his companions expressed this meaning by saying: knowledge is an attribute from which is derived the description of the one in whom it subsists as knowing. And sometimes they say: knowledge is the attribute such that whoever possesses it is knowing. This wording is the choice of most of his companions, and it is a wording that encompasses all that is defined and prevents what is outside it from entering under it. The opponents say: This is from you a definition of a thing by that by which it is itself known. For when you are asked about knowledge, you define it by the knower, saying 'what necessitates the knower' or one of the other two phrases. This is defining knowledge by the knower. Then when you are asked about the knower, you say 'the one in whom knowledge subsists' or 'the one who has knowledge'. So you have defined knowledge by the knower and the knower by knowledge. Whenever a thing is defined by that by which it is itself known, each of them remains unknown, like one who says 'Zaid came to me' and is asked 'Who is Zaid?' and he replies 'the son of Amr.' Then he is asked 'Who is this Amr?' and he replies 'the father of Zaid.' Each of them remains unknown, and no knowledge of either is obtained. The opponents press hard upon the Ash'aris with this objection. And it is possible to repel this difficulty and extricate oneself from this impossibility by saying: This objection arises from ignorance of the purpose for which the definition was posited. By understanding the purpose for which the definition was posited, its falsity becomes clear. The explanation of this is that knowledge of a matter may be absolute and vague knowledge of it, without the knower grasping the specific reality by which the known object is distinguished from other things that resemble or approximate it. And it may be true knowledge, where the knower has grasped the specific reality by which the known object is distinguished from what resembles or approximates it—like the common people's knowledge that Zaid is human. This is certain knowledge, with no doubt for the knower, no agitation of his heart, no objection from doubts, and no intrusion of thoughts that cause confusion. Yet this certainty is, along with this, an absolute, vague knowledge; the common person does not grasp the specific reality by which the human is distinguished from what resembles it in animal force and approximates it in sharing reason.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage first presents al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge. Al-Ash'ari is reported to have said that knowledge is 'what necessitates the knower' (ما أوجب للعالم)—i.e., it makes it true that one is knowing. Some of his followers expressed this as 'an attribute from which is derived the description of being knowing' (صفة تشتق لمن قام به الوصف بأنه عالم). Another phrasing is that knowledge is 'the attribute that, whoever possesses it, is knowing' (الوصف الذي من قام به كان عالما). Al-Nasafi notes that the last wording is preferred by most Ash'aris because it is comprehensive and excludes extraneous things.

Then the opponents raise a circularity objection: The Ash'aris define knowledge by referring to the knower (عالم), and then define the knower by knowledge. This is like saying 'Zaid is the son of Amr' and 'Amr is the father of Zaid'—neither is properly known. So the definition is viciously circular.

Al-Nasafi then records the beginning of a response, which attempts to break the charge by distinguishing between two types of knowledge. The first is absolute, vague knowledge (علم مطلق مبهم)—a general, undifferentiated awareness. The second is true knowledge (علم حقيقي)—a precise awareness that grasps the essential distinguishing features of a thing. The example given is that common people know that Zaid is human with certainty, but they do not know the specific reality that distinguishes humans from other animals that share reason. The response seems to argue that the definitions in question are not intended to give true, precise knowledge but rather absolute, vague knowledge, so the charge of circularity misses the mark. However, the passage cuts off before the response is complete. The response is clearly continuing on the next page.

Key Terms

عالم
حد
تعريف
دور
يقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any definition that uses a related term is automatically viciously circular.
Correction: The Ash'ari response argues that if the definition provides absolute, vague knowledge rather than precise essential knowledge, it can break the circularity.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection the opponents raise against al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge?
A) It is too vague.
B) It is circular, defining knowledge by the knower and the knower by knowledge.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It applies only to God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge is subject to a circularity objection, but a distinction between absolute and true knowledge is proposed as a rebuttal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of Allah's names and attributes, do you rely on absolute, vague knowledge that gives you certainty without a full grasp of their true reality, or have you sought deeper essential knowledge? How does this affect the firmness of your faith?
?

The Unique Effect of Knowledge
Nasafi proves the reality of knowledge by its unique effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the reality of knowledge is discovered by examining its unique effect, distinguishing it from physical attributes.

Core Idea
Knowledge's only effect is making its possessor knowledgeable — nothing else.

How do we know what knowledge truly is? Theologians examine its effects. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi shows that knowledge does not cause any physical change; its sole effect is that the one who has it becomes knowledgeable. This method of discerning reality by effects is key to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the list of physical attributes and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the contrast between physical attributes and knowledge's effect.
2
Identify the negative argument
Notice how the author argues that knowledge does not cause movement, stillness, color, etc. This eliminates physical effects.
3
Identify the positive argument
Knowledge's only effect is making the subject knowledgeable. This is the key point.
4
Understand the example of Zayd and his father
The example shows that we cannot know an unknown essence through another, supporting the idea that we know knowledge by its own effect.
5
Summarize the method
Explain that things are known by their own states and attributes, not by their subjects or relations.
This method is used throughout kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السكون والاجتماع والافتراق، والطول والقصر، وكذا في الطعوم والروائح كلها، فظهر أنه ما كان عالما إلا لقيام العلم به، وكان هذا حقيقة العالم وكذا العلم تأملنا فيه فعلمنا أنه لا يوجب كون من قام به متحركا ولا ساكنا، ولا مجتمعا ولا مفترقا، ولا أسود ولا أبيض، فعلمنا أن حقيقته أنه يوجب كون من قام به عالما، إذ لا أثر له إلا هذا، يظهر بالتأمل في أحوال ما هو معلوم في نفسه الحقيقة التي بها يمتاز عن غيره، فإذا عرفنا حقيقة كل واحد منهما بالتأمل في أحواله وأوصافه، لا بصاحبه بعدما ثبت علمنا بكل واحد منهما على الإطلاق بخلاف ما أورد في المثال فإن زيدا وأباه كل واحدا منهما مجهول الذات، فلا يحصل معرفة كل واحد منهما بصاحبه، والله الموفق.
Stillness, togetherness, separation, length, shortness, and likewise in tastes and smells all – so it became apparent that it was not knowledgeable except by the standing of knowledge in it, and this was the reality of the knowledgeable one, and likewise knowledge: we reflected on it and knew that it does not necessitate that the one in whom it subsists be moving nor still, nor together nor separate, nor black nor white. So we knew that its reality is that it necessitates that the one in whom it subsists be knowledgeable, for it has no effect except this. It becomes apparent by reflection on the states of what is known in itself: the reality by which it is distinguished from other than it. So when we know the reality of each of them by reflection on its states and attributes, not by its subject – after our knowledge of each of them has been established absolutely – contrary to what was adduced in the example, for Zayd and his father, each of them, is unknown in essence, so knowledge of each of them cannot be obtained by the other. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the distinct reality of knowledge by examining its effects. The author begins by listing physical attributes (stillness, togetherness, separation, length, shortness, tastes, smells) and notes that knowledge does not cause any of these. When we reflect on knowledge itself, we see that it does not make its subject (the one in whom it subsists) moving, still, together, separate, black, or white. The only effect of knowledge is that the subject becomes knowledgeable. Thus, the reality of knowledge is known by its own proper effect, not by any physical change. The author then explains that we can know the reality of each thing (e.g., the knower and knowledge) by reflecting on their states and attributes, not by reference to their subject (i.e., the individual in which they inhere). This is unlike the example of Zayd and his father: if both are unknown in essence, knowing one does not give knowledge of the other. The lesson ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
علم
حقيقة
أثر
معلوم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can be known by looking at the person who has it, just as we know Zayd by his father.
Correction: Knowledge is known by its own effects, not by the subject it inheres in, because the subject's essence may be unknown.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only effect of knowledge?
A) It causes movement.
B) It causes the subject to be knowledgeable.
C) It causes color change.
D) It causes separation.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge's reality is known by its sole effect: making its subject knowledgeable, regardless of physical attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your only true distinction is what you know, not how you look or move, have you invested in knowledge that makes you truly knowledgeable in the sight of Allah?
?
This passage says knowledge's only effect is making you knowledgeable. Does your life reflect that you value knowledge for itself, or do you seek it for worldly status?
?
The example of Zayd and his father shows that we cannot know one by the other if both are unknown. Who is the ultimate Source of knowledge that makes you truly know? Have you sought knowledge from its proper source?

The Maturidi Correction of the World's Divisions
Nasafi presents the common threefold division and al-Maturidi's correction to a twofold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Maturidi rejected the common threefold division of the world and proposed a twofold division instead.

Core Idea
The world is accidents and substances, not three parts.

The mutakallimun commonly divided the world into substances, bodies, and accidents. But al-Maturidi noticed an overlap: bodies are composed of substances. In this passage, he corrects the classification to a simpler and more logical twofold division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the classification.
2
Identify the threefold division
Note that the majority mutakallimun held three categories: jawāhir, ajsām, and aʿrāḍ.
3
Understand the overlap
See that bodies are made of substances, causing redundancy.
4
Learn Maturidi's correction
He reduces the division to accidents (aʿrāḍ) and substances (aʿyān).
5
Define 'self-subsistent'
Understand the meaning of 'qā'im bi nafsihi' as a basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أقسامه فعامة المتكلمين يزعمون أنها أقسام ثلاثة جواهر، وأجسام وأعراض. والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي لم يرض بهذه القسمة لما فيها من عيب التداخل، فإن الأجسام هي جواهر، لأنها مركبة منها، فاختار بأن قال: العالم قسمان: أعراض وأعيان يعني بالأعراض ما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه. وبالأعيان ما يقوم بنفسه ونعني بقولنا: ما يقوم بنفسه أن يصح وجوده من غير محل يقوم به لا ما سبق إلى وهم الأشعري. الرد على الأشعري وبرغوث في تعريفهم للعين إن القائم بنفسه ما يستغني في وجوده عن غيره. ولهذا أنكر كون الجواهر قائمة بأنفسها وقال: لا قائم بالنفس إلا الله تعالى. وإذا كان مرادنا من هذا اللفظ ما ذكرنا فلا معنى لإنكاره، لأن كل جوهر متركب كان أو غير متركب يصح وجوده في غير محل يقوم به، ولهذا قام جميع العالم لا في محل خلافا لما يقوله محمد بن عيسى الملقب ببرغوث أن ذلك لا يجوز. ولهذا زعم أنه ليس يجوز أن يخلق الله تعالى أقل من جزءين ليكون أحدهما مكانا للآخر، وهذا مع ما فيه من تعجيز الله، فاسد، إذ لو كان أحدهما مكانا للآخر ولم يكن الآخر مكانا إليه فهذا الذي هو المكان موجود لا في مكان، وهذا عين ما أباه، ولو كان كل واحد منهما مكانا للآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متمكنا فيما هو متمكن فيه ومكانا لما مکانه هو محال، والله الموفق.
As for its divisions, the generality of the mutakallimūn claim that they are three divisions: jawāhir (substances), ajsām (bodies), and aʿrāḍ (accidents). But Shaykh Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī was not satisfied with this division because of the defect of overlap it contains—for bodies are jawāhir (substances), since they are composed of them. So he chose to say: the world is two divisions: aʿrāḍ (accidents) and aʿyān (substances). By aʿrāḍ he means what cannot subsist by itself; by aʿyān he means what subsists by itself. And by our saying 'what subsists by itself' we mean that its existence is valid from being without a locus (maḥall) subsisting in it, not as occurred to the imagination of al-Ashʿarī. The refutation of al-Ashʿarī and Barghūth regarding their definition of substance (al-ʿayn): Indeed, what subsists by itself is that which is independent in its existence from other than it. For this reason he (al-Ashʿarī) denied that jawāhir (substances) are self-subsistent, and said: no one subsists by Himself except Allah Most High. And if what we mean by this expression is what we have mentioned, then there is no sense in his denial, because every jawhar (substance), whether composite or not, its existence is valid without a locus subsisting in it. Thus the entire world subsists without a locus, contrary to what Muḥammad ibn ʿĪsā, nicknamed Barghūth, says—that this is not permissible. For this reason he claimed that it is not permissible for Allah Most High to create less than two parts (juzʾayn) so that one of them might be a place (makān) for the other. This, along with what it contains of rendering Allah incapable, is corrupt. For if one of them were a place for the other and the other were not a place for it, then this one which is the place exists without a place—and that is exactly what he rejects. And if each of them were a place for the other, then each of them would be located in that which it is located in, and a place for what is its place—that is impossible. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a refutation. It first presents the common threefold division of the world into substances (jawāhir), bodies (ajsām), and accidents (aʿrāḍ). Then it notes al-Māturīdī's correction: bodies are composed of substances, causing overlap, so he reduces the division to accidents (aʿrāḍ) and substances (aʿyān). The term 'substance' (ʿayn) is defined as what subsists by itself, meaning it can exist without a locus—this is a response to al-Ashʿarī, who thought only Allah subsists by Himself. The passage then refutes Barghūth's view that Allah cannot create less than two parts because one needs a place. The refutation argues that if one part is a place for the other without reciprocity, then the place itself exists without a place, contradicting Barghūth's own principle. If they are mutual places, each would be simultaneously located in and a place for the other, which is absurd. Thus, substances can exist without a locus, supporting the Māturīdī definition.

Key Terms
جواهر
أجسام
أعراض
أعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The threefold division of the world is the universally accepted and logically airtight classification.
Correction: Al-Maturidi demonstrated an overlap between bodies and substances, making the threefold division redundant; a twofold division is more accurate.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Maturidi reject the threefold division of the world?
A) Because bodies and substances overlap, as bodies are composed of substances
B) Because accidents are not real
C) Because there are actually four divisions
D) Because he agreed with al-Ashʿarī

Key Takeaway
The world is fundamentally composed of two categories: accidents and substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you habitually think of physical objects (bodies) as the only true 'realities'? How does al-Māturīdī's correction—that bodies are merely composed of substances, not a distinct category—shift your understanding of what is ultimately real?
?
Your existence depends entirely on Allah—you are not self-subsisting. In light of this lesson, what 'accidents' in your life (wealth, status, health) do you mistakenly treat as though they subsist on their own? Will you recalibrate your attachments starting today?
?
If the world is only accidents and substances, then every event in your life is an accident in a substance, all sustained by Allah. Does your daily anxiety reflect that belief? What would change if you truly internalized that nothing can subsist without Him—not even your next breath?

Defining Self-Subsistence and Refuting Ash'ari and Barghuth
Nasafi defines self-subsistence and refutes al-Ash'ari and Barghuth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of 'self-subsistence' and how it refutes the objections of al-Ash'ari and Barghuth.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence means existing without a locus, not independence from Allah.

After defining self-subsistence as existing without a locus, al-Maturidi counters two opposing views: al-Ash'ari who thought only Allah is self-subsistent, and Barghuth who claimed substances cannot exist without a spatial location. This passage clarifies the Maturidi position and exposes the logical flaws in these objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation.
2
Grasp the definition
Note the precise meaning of 'qā'im bi nafsihi' as existence without a locus.
3
Understand al-Ash'ari's error
He thought self-subsistence means independence from anything, but the text clarifies it is independence from a locus.
4
Analyze Barghuth's argument
Barghuth required at least two parts for place, but the refutation shows this leads to contradiction.
5
See the conclusion
Substances are self-subsistent, and the world exists without a locus, contrary to Barghuth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أقسامه فعامة المتكلمين يزعمون أنها أقسام ثلاثة جواهر، وأجسام وأعراض. والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي لم يرض بهذه القسمة لما فيها من عيب التداخل، فإن الأجسام هي جواهر، لأنها مركبة منها، فاختار بأن قال: العالم قسمان: أعراض وأعيان يعني بالأعراض ما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه. وبالأعيان ما يقوم بنفسه ونعني بقولنا: ما يقوم بنفسه أن يصح وجوده من غير محل يقوم به لا ما سبق إلى وهم الأشعري. الرد على الأشعري وبرغوث في تعريفهم للعين إن القائم بنفسه ما يستغني في وجوده عن غيره. ولهذا أنكر كون الجواهر قائمة بأنفسها وقال: لا قائم بالنفس إلا الله تعالى. وإذا كان مرادنا من هذا اللفظ ما ذكرنا فلا معنى لإنكاره، لأن كل جوهر متركب كان أو غير متركب يصح وجوده في غير محل يقوم به، ولهذا قام جميع العالم لا في محل خلافا لما يقوله محمد بن عيسى الملقب ببرغوث أن ذلك لا يجوز. ولهذا زعم أنه ليس يجوز أن يخلق الله تعالى أقل من جزءين ليكون أحدهما مكانا للآخر، وهذا مع ما فيه من تعجيز الله، فاسد، إذ لو كان أحدهما مكانا للآخر ولم يكن الآخر مكانا إليه فهذا الذي هو المكان موجود لا في مكان، وهذا عين ما أباه، ولو كان كل واحد منهما مكانا للآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متمكنا فيما هو متمكن فيه ومكانا لما مکانه هو محال، والله الموفق.

Substances and Bodies: The Division of A'yan
Classification of a'yan into jawhar and jism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun divide a'yan into jawhar (non-composite) and jism (composite), and the logical relationship between them.

Core Idea
Every body is a substance, but not every substance is a body.

In the previous lesson, we saw that the world is divided into accidents (a'rad) and entities (a'yan). Now we delve deeper into the category of a'yan. The mutakallimun classify a'yan into non-composite entities called jawahir (substances) and composite entities called ajsam (bodies). Understanding this classification is crucial before defining each term. The passage also clarifies that every body is composed of substances, establishing a hierarchical relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the main ideas.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice the terms 'ghayr al-murakkab' (non-composite) and 'al-murakkab' (composite). These are the two subdivisions of a'yan.
The mutakallimun call the non-composite 'jawhar' and the composite 'jism'.
3
Understand the implication
The passage states 'every jism is a jawhar'. Think about why this is true: a body is composed of simpler substances.
This implies a hierarchical relation, not mutual exclusivity.
4
Prepare for definitions
The author says that once this division is known, they will proceed to define each category. This sets the stage for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ثبوت هذه القسمة الأولية نقول: تنقسم الأعيان إلى غير المتركبة وهي المسماة في عرف المتكلمين جواهر، والمتركبة وهي المسماة في عرفهم أجساما، وبهذا يعرف أن كل جسم جوهر، فإذا عرف هذا التقسيم الجامع لجميع أجزاء المقسوم نشتغل ببيان تحديد كل قسم من هذه الأقسام.
Then, after the establishment of this primary division, we say: The a'yan (entities) are divided into the non-composite, which in the terminology of the mutakallimun are called jawahir, and the composite, which in their terminology are called ajsam (bodies). And by this it is known that every body (jism) is a jawhar (substance). So when this comprehensive division of all parts of the divided is known, we occupy ourselves with clarifying the definition of each of these divisions.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsim). After establishing the initial division of the world into a'rad and a'yan, the author now subdivides a'yan into two categories: non-composite entities, which are called jawahir (singular: jawhar, substances) in the terminology of the mutakallimun, and composite entities, which are called ajsam (singular: jism, bodies). The author notes that from this it follows that every jism is a jawhar (since a body is composed of jawahir). The purpose of this comprehensive division is to then proceed to define each category. Note that this classification avoids the earlier overlap problem because now jawahir are a subset of a'yan, not a separate class alongside a'rad.

Key Terms
عين
جوهر
جسم
متركب
غير متركب
متكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That jism and jawhar are mutually exclusive categories.
Correction: The passage states that every jism is a jawhar, meaning bodies are composed of substances; they are not separate.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between a body (jism) and a substance (jawhar)?
A) They are completely distinct categories
B) Every jism is a jawhar
C) Every jawhar is a jism
D) They are synonyms

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun divide a'yan into non-composite substances (jawhar) and composite bodies (jism), and every body is composed of substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every body you see, including your own, is actually a composite of jawahir created directly by Allah. Does this truth change how you perceive the world around you? Will you pause today to recognize the unseen reality behind every physical thing and thank Allah for His continuous creation?

The Twofold Definition of Jawhar: Linguistic and Technical
Begins the definition of jawhar: linguistic and technical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual definition of jawhar: its linguistic sense as 'origin' and its technical sense as 'self-subsistent'.

Core Idea
Jawhar is both an origin and a self-subsistent entity.

The term jawhar (substance) is foundational in kalam. This passage begins by grounding its technical usage in common Arabic speech. Before we can discuss atoms and bodies, we need to know what the word means both in everyday language and in the technical vocabulary of theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noticing how it starts with the linguistic meaning before moving to the technical definition.
The passage is cut off; we will only cover what is present.
2
Identify the two definitions
Observe that the author gives a linguistic definition (jawhar = 'origin') with examples, then begins a technical definition (jawhar = 'self-subsistent').
The technical definition is incomplete but its direction is clear.
3
Connect language to terminology
Notice how the technical term is not arbitrary but builds on the linguistic sense of 'origin' or 'essence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الجوهر: أما الجوهر فهو في اللغة عبارة عن الأصل، يقال: لفلان جوهر شريف أي أصل شريف وهذا ثوب جوهري: أي جيد الأصل. وأما حده فهو: أنه القائم
Definition of the substance (al-jawhar): As for al-jawhar, in the language it is an expression for the origin (al-asl). It is said: 'So-and-so has a noble jawhar', meaning a noble origin; and 'this is a jawhari garment', meaning good in origin. And as for its definition (hadd), it is: that it is the self-subsistent (al-qa'im)...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage introduces the definition of jawhar (substance) in two ways: linguistically and terminologically. Linguistically, jawhar means 'origin' (asl), as in 'noble origin' or 'good quality of fabric'. Technically, the author begins to define it as 'that which subsists by itself' (al-qa'im bi-nafsih), but the definition is cut off. The text continues on the next page. This lesson captures the start of the definition, emphasizing that the technical term is grounded in common usage.

Mathematicians' Definition of Body as Three Dimensions
Nasafi explains the mathematicians' definition of body as having three dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mathematicians define body as having three dimensions and how they derive it from points, lines, and surfaces.

Core Idea
Body, according to mathematicians, is three-dimensional: length, width, and depth.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla introduces the geometrical definition of body (jism) as held by mathematicians (ahl al-ḥisāb). Unlike the mutakallimūn's metaphysical definition, mathematicians define body purely in terms of extension. Nasafi explains the progression from point to line to surface to body, laying the foundation for later discussions on the nature of physical substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi systematically builds up the definition from the simplest element.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the pattern is clear.
2
Identify the key terms
List the Arabic terms for point, line, surface, and the three dimensions. Understand their definitions as given in the passage.
These terms are foundational for later discussions in kalam.
3
Trace the progression
Follow the logical progression: indivisible point → line (by combining two points) → surface (by combining two lines) → body (by combining a surface with another dimension).
4
Compare with mutakallimun definition
Contrast this geometrical definition with the metaphysical definition of body used by theologians, which you may have learned earlier.
This distinction is important to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجسم فعلى رأي الحساب هو ما له الأبعاد الثلاثة، ويعنون بالأبعاد الثلاثة الطول والعرض والعمق، وهم يسمون الجوهر الواحد الذي لا يتجزأ فعلا نقطة. ويقولون: إنه إذا تركب بآخر مثله حدث هناك طول ويسمونه خطا، ويقولون في تحديد الخط هو المجتمع طولا فقط ثم إذا تركب من الجانب الأخر يسمى سطحا، ويقولون: السطح ما له طول وعرض، ثم إذا قبل تركيبا آخر من الجانب
As for body, according to the opinion of the mathematicians (al-hussab), it is that which has three dimensions, and by three dimensions they mean length, width, and depth. They call the single substance (al-jawhar al-wahid) that is actually indivisible a point (nuqta). They say: when it is composed with another like it, length occurs there, and they call it a line (khatt). They say in defining the line: it is that which extends in length only. Then when it is composed from the other side, it is called a surface (sath), and they say: the surface is that which has length and width. Then when it accepts another composition from the side
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a definition. Nasafi presents the geometrical definition of body (jism) according to mathematicians (ahl al-hisab). They define body as having three dimensions: length, width, and depth. They begin with the indivisible substance (jawhar wahid), which they call a point (nuqta). When two points are combined, a line (khatt) is produced, defined as having length only. When two lines combine on another side, a surface (sath) is produced, having length and width. Finally, when a surface is combined further, a body results, having all three dimensions. The text cuts off at this point, but the pattern is clear.

Correction: The mathematicians define body solely in terms of extension (three dimensions), whereas theologians consider additional attributes like indivisibility and substance.

Mathematical Definition of Body and Its Three Dimensions
Nasafi gives the mathematicians' definition of body as having three dimensions and explains the derivation from point to line to surface to body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mathematicians' definition of body as something possessing three dimensions and the derivation from point to line to surface to body.

Core Idea
Body is defined by three dimensions, derived from indivisible points.

Nasafi begins by presenting the definition of body (jism) according to mathematicians (al-hussab). This definition is not merely abstract; it is built from a theory of indivisible substances called points, which combine step-by-step to produce length, width, and depth. Understanding this derivation is essential for later theological discussions about the nature of Allah, as the Mu'tazila and early Maturidis accepted this definition but theologians had to consider whether it applies to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three dimensions
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice that the mathematicians define body by three specific dimensions: length, width, and depth. Write them down.
These dimensions correspond to the three spatial axes.
2
Trace the derivation from point to body
Follow the sequence: point (indivisible substance) → line (from two points) → surface (from line plus another point creating width) → body (adding depth). Describe each step in your own words.
The mathematicians use a constructive approach, building up dimensions from indivisible units.
3
Note the minimum number of parts required
The passage states that a body requires at least eight points. Consider why that number is necessary (likely a cube with two points per dimension).
This shows that body is not merely abstract but has a minimum composition.
4
Recognize the scholarly support mentioned
Nasafi notes that early Maturidis and all Mu'tazila accepted this definition. Reflect on why this consensus might matter for later theological arguments.
This sets the stage for kalam discussions on whether Allah can be called a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجسم فعلى رأي الحساب هو ما له الأبعاد الثلاثة، ويعنون بالأبعاد الثلاثة الطول والعرض والعمق، وهم يسمون الجوهر الواحد الذي لا يتجزأ فعلا نقطة. ويقولون: إنه إذا تركب بآخر مثله حدث هناك طول ويسمونه خطا، ويقولون في تحديد الخط: هو المجتمع طولا فقط. ثم إذا تركب من الجانب الآخر يسمى سطحا، ويقولون: السطح ما له طول وعرض. ثم إذا قبل تركيبا آخر من الجانب الأسفل أو من الجانب الأعلى فحصل له عمق وسمك الآن يسمى جسما. ويقولون: لا يحصل الجسم بأقل من ثمانية أجزاء كل جزء نقطة. وساعدهم أوائل أصحابنا والمعتزلة بأسرهم على هذا، وقالوا: الجسم ما له طول وعرض وعمق.
As for body, according to the opinion of the mathematicians (al-hussab), it is what has the three dimensions; and by the three dimensions they mean length, width, and depth. And they call the single substance that is actually indivisible a point. And they say: when it is combined with another like it, there occurs length, and they call it a line. And they say in defining the line: it is that which extends in length only. Then when it is combined from another side, it is called a surface, and they say: the surface is what has length and width. Then when it receives another combination from the lower side or from the upper side, depth and thickness occur, and now it is called a body. And they say: body does not come about with fewer than eight parts, each part being a point. And the early ones among our companions and all the Mu'tazila supported them in this, and they said: body is what has length, width, and depth.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines body (jism) according to the mathematicians (al-hussab) as that which possesses three dimensions: length, width, and depth. They begin with the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-wahid) called a point (nuqta). Two points combine to produce length, forming a line (khatt). Adding a second dimension yields a surface (sath) with length and width. Adding a third dimension from above or below gives depth (umq) or thickness (sammak), resulting in a body. They state that at least eight points are necessary to form a body, likely a cube with two points per dimension. Nasafi notes that early Maturidi scholars and all Mu'tazila accepted this definition. This sets the stage for the theological discussion on whether such a definition can apply to Allah.

Al-Ash'ari's Argument on the Meaning of 'Body'
Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's reasoning that 'body' cannot be a name for the sum of three compositional aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's logical argument that the term 'body' cannot refer to the sum of the three dimensions (length, width, depth).

Core Idea
The comparative 'ajsam' proves that 'body' is not defined by three dimensions.

Al-Ash'ari, a major theologian, used a clever linguistic-logical argument to clarify what 'body' really means. He argued that if 'body' were simply the combination of length, width, and depth, then saying 'this is more bodily' would require an increase in all three. But we use 'ajsam' even when only one dimension increases, so 'body' must mean something else. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: al-Ash'ari states his claim, then gives the reasoning using the comparative 'ajsam.'
The passage is concise but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'الأوجه الثلاثة' (the three aspects) and 'أجسم' (more bodily). Understand that 'ajsam' is the comparative form of 'jism.'
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the logical implication
If 'body' named the sum of three dimensions, then 'more bodily' would require an increase in all three. But al-Ash'ari argues we use 'ajsam' when only one dimension increases. Therefore, 'body' cannot be that sum.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الأشعري فإنه اشتغل بالبحث عن ذلك، فزعم أنه لا يصلح أن يكون اسما لما له التركيب من الأوجه الثلاثة التي يحصل بها الطول والعرض والعمق لا محالة. وهذا لأنه لو كان اسما لمجموع الأوجه الثلاثة من التركيب لكان الوصف منا بالتفضيل لأحد الجسمين على الآخر بقولنا: هذا أجسم من ذلك، واقعا على زيادة أجزاء هذا في الطول والعرض والعمق جميعا على ذلك، ولن يجوز إطلاق هذا التفضيل على ذلك عند وجود زيادة تركيب من جهة بعد من الأبعاد الثلاثة، إذ التفضيل يوجب زيادة في الجنس الذي يدل عليه مطلق اللفظ بدون صيغة التفضيل.
As for al-Ash'ari, he occupied himself with investigation of that, and claimed that it is not suitable for it to be a name for that which has composition from the three aspects by which length, width, and depth necessarily come about. And this is because if it were a name for the sum of the three aspects of composition, then our description of preference of one body over another by our saying 'this is more bodily (ajsam) than that' would be actual upon an increase in the parts of this one in length, width, and depth all together over that one. And it would not be permissible to apply this preference to that when there exists an increase in composition from only one of the three dimensions, since preference requires increase in the genus indicated by the absolute word without the comparative form.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's argument that the term 'body' (jism) cannot be a name for the sum of three compositional aspects (the three dimensions). His reasoning uses the comparative form 'ajsam' (more bodily). If 'body' denoted the sum of all three dimensions, then saying 'this is more bodily than that' would require an increase in all three dimensions simultaneously. However, we observe that we use 'ajsam' even when only one dimension increases (e.g., length). Therefore, 'body' cannot be a name for that sum; rather, it must refer to something else, likely composition itself. Al-Ash'ari thus argues that the term does not entail a specific combination of dimensions but a general notion of composition, which he then uses to argue against applying it to Allah.

Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's argument is merely a linguistic observation without logical force.
Correction: The argument is a logical demonstration: it shows that defining 'body' as the sum of three dimensions leads to a contradiction with the ordinary use of the comparative, thus proving that definition is false.

Key Takeaway

Al-Ash'ari's Linguistic Argument for Defining Body
Al-Ash'ari critiques the definition of body and refines it via linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari used linguistic analysis to define 'body' and why his followers' proposed term 'mu'allaf' was rejected on linguistic grounds.

Core Idea
Linguistic precision reveals that 'body' implies no 'bodi-fier', so definitions must avoid implying a creator.

Al-Ash'ari delved into the definition of body, using the comparative form 'ajsam' to argue that the word refers to absolute composition from the three dimensions. However, some of his followers introduced the term 'mu'allaf' (composed), which linguistically implies a composer (mu'allif). The verifying scholars rejected this, leading al-Ash'ari to adopt 'mujtami' (combined) instead. This passage demonstrates the intersection of linguistic precision and theological caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the English translation. Note that the argument is dense and uses linguistic examples.
The passage contains two main sections: a comparative argument about 'ajsam' and a critique of 'mu'allaf'.
2
Understand the comparative argument
Al-Ash'ari argues that if 'body' meant a composite of all three dimensions, then the comparative 'ajsam' would require an increase in all three dimensions. But language uses 'ajsam' when only one dimension increases, proving that 'body' means absolute composition.
This is a classic example of istidlāl (inference) from linguistic usage.
3
Notice the critique of 'mu'allaf'
The passage explains that 'mu'allaf' (composed) linguistically indicates a composer (mu'allif), whereas 'jism' does not imply a 'mujassim'. Therefore, defining body as 'mu'allaf' would inadvertently imply a creator of the body, which is theologically problematic.
This shows the Ash'ari commitment to avoiding any term that might suggest a human-like composition of God's creation.
4
Reflect on the final definition
Al-Ash'ari settles on 'mujtami' (the combined) or 'mu'talif' or 'murakkab' (composite) because these words do not linguistically imply a combiner or composer. This preserves the rational indication (dala 'aqliyya) that bodies have a Creator without embedding it in the definition itself.
The lesson highlights the importance of choosing words carefully in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الأشعري فإنه اشتغل بالبحث عن ذلك، فزعم أنه لا يصلح أن يكون اسما لما له التركيب من الأوجه الثلاثة التي يحصل بها الطول والعرض والعمق لا محالة، وهذا لأنه لو كان اسما لمجموع الأوجه الثلاثة من التركيب لكان الوصف منا بالتفضيل لأحد الجسمين على الآخر بقولنا : هذا أجسم من ذلك، واقعا على زيادة أجزاء هذا في الطول والعرض والعمق جميعا على ذلك، ولن يجوز إطلاق هذا التفضيل على ذلك عند وجود زيادة تركيب من جهة بعد من الأبعاد الثلاثة، إذ التفضيل يوجب زيادة في الجنس الذي يدل عليه مطلق اللفظ بدون صيغة التفضيل، وإذا لم يكن كذلك، بل استعمل أهل اللغة لفظة التفضيل عند وجود زيادة في أحد هذه الأبعاد الثلاثة دون الباقين منها دل أن اللفظ ما وضع اسما إلا لما له مطلق التركيب ولا حاجة إلى ثبوت التركيب من الأوجه الثلاثة كله لاستحقاق إطلاق هذا الاسم عليه. ثم بعد ثبوت هذا المعنى، زعم بعض أصحابه أن الجسم اسم للمؤلف ولم يرض المحققون بهذه العبارة فإنها بصيغتها موضوعة في اللغة على وجه يدل على أن له مؤلفا يقال : ألفه فهو مؤلف وذلك مؤلف، وليس في لفظة الجسم ما يدل على أن له مجسما، بل الدلالة على أن له مجسما عقلية لا لغوية، والدلالة على أن للمؤلف مؤلفا لغوية فلم يصلح تحديد الجسم بأنه مؤلف أو مركب مجموع فحدده الأشعري بأنه المجتمع ، وربما قيل : المؤتلف أو المتركب لأن هذه الألفاظ لا تدل بصيغتها من حيث اللغة على أن للمسمى به جامعا أو مؤلفا أو مركبا كما أن لفظة الجسم لا تدل من حيث اللغة على أن له مجسما
As for al-Ash'ari, he engaged in research about that, and claimed that it is not suitable to be a name for that which has composition from the three aspects by which length, width, and depth are necessarily obtained. And this is because if it were a name for the totality of the three aspects of composition, then our description by way of comparing one body to another by our saying 'this is more bodily (ajsam) than that' would be upon an increase of all three dimensions together, and it would not be permissible to apply this comparison when there is an increase in one of the three dimensions only, since comparison necessitates an increase in the genus which the absolute word indicates without the comparative form. And if it is not so, but rather the people of language use the comparative word when there is an increase in one of these three dimensions without the others, that indicates that the word is not placed as a name except for that which has absolute composition, and there is no need for the composition from all three aspects to deserve the application of this name. Then after establishing this meaning, some of his followers claimed that body is a name for the composed (mu'allaf), and the verifying scholars were not satisfied with this expression because its linguistic form is placed in the language in a way that indicates that it has a composer (mu'allif) — it is said: 'he composed it (allafahu), so it is composed (mu'allaf), and that is composed (mu'allaf).' And there is not in the word 'body' anything that indicates that it has a 'bodi-fier' (mujassim). Rather, the indication that it has a 'bodi-fier' is rational, not linguistic, while the indication that the composed has a composer is linguistic. So it is not suitable to define body as 'composed' or 'composite aggregate'. So al-Ash'ari defined it as 'the combined' (al-mujtami'), and sometimes it is said: 'the composed' (al-mu'talif) or 'the composite' (al-murakkab), because these words do not, by their form from a linguistic perspective, indicate that the thing named has a combiner (jami') or composer (mu'allif) or compounder (murakkib), just as the word 'body' does not, from a linguistic perspective, indicate that it has a 'bodi-fier'.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage discusses al-Ash'ari's engagement with the definition of body and the linguistic critique of his followers' proposed definition. Al-Ash'ari argued that body cannot be a name for something composed in the three aspects (length, width, depth) because if it were, the comparative form 'ajsam' would require an increase in all three dimensions together, but language uses it even when only one dimension increases, proving that the word refers to absolute composition. After establishing this, some of his followers defined body as 'composed' (mu'allaf), but the verifying scholars rejected this because the word 'mu'allaf' linguistically implies a composer (mu'allif), whereas 'jism' does not imply a 'mujassim' linguistically—only rationally. So al-Ash'ari redefined it as 'the combined' (mujtami') or 'the composite' (murakkab), as these do not imply a combiner. The passage highlights the importance of linguistic precision in theological definitions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ash'ari's definition is purely linguistic and has no theological implications.
Correction: In fact, the choice of definition is driven by the theological need to avoid any term that might imply a created body has a composer in the same way a composed object has a maker, which would compromise the unique divine creative act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's definition of body as 'the combined' (al-mujtami') avoids implying a creator in the definition itself, preserving the theological distinction between rational inference and linguistic implication.

Al-Ash'ari rejected the term 'mu'allaf' because it functionally embedded a theological claim into a definition. Are there terms in your own vocabulary about Allah that come with hidden implications you have never examined? Will you now inspect them?

The Logical Objection to Ash'ari's Definition of Body
Objection against Ash'ari's definition using atom combination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem of defining body as composed atoms, and why al-Ash'ari's definition leads to absurd consequences according to this objection.

Core Idea
If each atom acquires composition, then every atom becomes a body, making nonsense of the definition.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a powerful logical objection against the Ash'ari definition of body as a combination of atoms. The argument shows that if composition is an accident that must inhere in each atom, then each atom individually qualifies as a body, leading to an absurd regress. This critique is aimed at exposing the internal inconsistency in al-Ash'ari's atomistic framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow. Notice the compact style of argument.
Pay attention to the role of the principle that a single accident cannot subsist in two loci.
2
Identify the key premise
The premise is that when two atoms combine, the accidents of combination must occur in each atom individually because a single accident cannot inhere in two loci.
This is a fundamental rule in kalam physics.
3
Trace the reasoning to the conclusion
From the premise, each atom becomes a composed entity (mu'talif). In Ash'ari's system, being composed is what defines a body, so each atom would be a body.
This leads to the absurd result that even a single atom is a body.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is a powerful critique. It shows internal inconsistency without relying on external assumptions.
The author also dismisses the later Mu'tazila position as impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأورد على أصحابه أن الجوهر إذا تركب بجوهر قام بكل واحد منهما تركب واجتماع وائتلاف على حدة لأن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بمحلين، ومن يجوز ذلك من متأخري المعتزلة فقد ارتكب محالا على ما نبين بعد هذا، وإذا كان قام بكل جوهر ائتلاف كان كل جوهر مؤتلفا وهما جوهران فكان مؤلفين، فينبغي أن يكونا جسمين ويكون كل جوهر على حياله جسما، فإذا جوهر واحد كان جسما
And he objected to his followers that if an atom (jawhar) combines with an atom, combination (tarkib), togetherness (ijtima'), and composition (i'tilaf) occur in each one of them individually, because a single accident does not subsist in two loci. And whoever among the later Mu'tazila permits that has committed an impossibility, as we shall explain after this. And when composition occurs in each atom, each atom is composed (mu'talif), and they are two atoms, so they are two composed entities. Then they should be two bodies, and each atom individually would be a body. Then one atom would be a body.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against al-Ash'ari's definition of body as combined or composite. The argument is: if an atom (jawhar) combines with another atom, then each atom individually acquires the accidents of combination (tarkib), togetherness (ijtima'), and composition (i'tilaf), because a single accident cannot subsist in two loci. Therefore, each atom would be composed, and so each would be a body. This leads to the absurd conclusion that even a single atom would be a body. The objection also notes that later Mu'tazila who allow a single accident to subsist in two loca would be committing an impossibility. The purpose is to show the difficulty of defining body in terms of composition without falling into contradiction.

Key Terms
جوهر
تركب
اجتماع
ائتلاف
عرض واحد
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is about physical impossibility of atoms combining.
Correction: The objection is purely logical: it targets the definition of body as combination. It shows that under Ash'ari's own principles, the definition leads to an absurd conclusion, regardless of physical reality.

Checkpoint
What is the intended conclusion of the objection about two atoms combining?
A) That atoms cannot combine.
B) That each atom would be a body, leading to absurdity.
C) That bodies are eternal.
D) That accidents can spread across multiple atoms.

Key Takeaway
The objection reveals that defining body as composed atoms leads to the absurd result that every atom is itself a body, rendering the definition internally inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this logical objection, do you recognize any areas in your own theological beliefs where you have accepted definitions without fully considering their implications? What might be at stake if you got those definitions wrong?
?
The author insists on the impossibility of a single accident subsisting in two loci. In your spiritual life, are there 'accidents'—actions or habits—that you try to serve two masters? How might this principle of 'one accident, one locus' challenge you to focus your devotion?
?
If a flawed definition can lead to absurd conclusions in theology, what does that tell you about the importance of precise language in matters of faith? Are you willing to invest time in understanding technical terms to protect your belief from internal contradictions?

The Linguistic Definition of Jism
Al-Nasafi argues against defining jism as 'composed' on linguistic grounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifiers rejected defining jism as 'mu'allaf' (composed) based on the linguistic implication of a composer.

Core Idea
A word's linguistic form matters: 'jism' does not imply a composer, but 'mu'allaf' does.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi examines the definition of jism. He first clarifies that the term applies to anything with absolute composition, without requiring three dimensions. Then he reports that some of Al-Ash'ari's companions defined jism as 'mu'allaf' (composed), but the verifiers objected because the word 'mu'allaf' linguistically implies a composer, whereas 'jism' does not. This lesson unpacks that linguistic argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the translation. Notice the key terms: tafdil, tarkib, mu'allaf, mujassim.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the role of tafdil
Al-Nasafi uses the linguistic usage of 'tafdil' (superiority) to argue that jism applies to anything with absolute composition, not requiring all three dimensions.
3
Grasp the rejection of mu'allaf
The verifiers reject 'mu'allaf' because its form indicates a composer (mu'allif), whereas 'jism' has no such implication. The indication of a composer for jism is rational, not linguistic.
Al-Ash'ari preferred 'mujtama'' (collected) which avoids this issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا لم يكن كذلك، بل استعمل أهل اللغة لفظة التفضيل عند وجود زيادة في أحد هذه الأبعاد الثلاثة دون الباقين منها دل أن اللفظ ما وضع اسما إلا لما له مطلق التركيب ولا حاجة إلى ثبوت التركيب من الأوجه الثلاثة كله لاستحقاق إطلاق هذا الاسم عليه. ثم بعد ثبوت هذا المعنى، زعم بعض أصحابه أن الجسم اسم للمؤلف ولم يرض المحققون بهذه العبارة فإنها بصيغتها موضوعة في اللغة على وجه يدل على أن له مؤلفا يقال: ألفه فهو مؤلف وذلك مؤلف، وليس في لفظة الجسم ما يدل على أن له مجسما، بل الدلالة على أن له مجسما عقلية لا لغوية، والدلالة على أن للمؤلف مؤلفا لغوية فلم يصلح تحديد الجسم بأنه مؤلف أو مركب مجموع فحدده الأشعري بأنه المجتمع، وربما قيل: المؤتلف أو المتركب لأن هذه الألفاظ لا تدل بصيغتها من حيث اللغة على أن للمسمى به جامعا أو مؤلفا أو مركبا كما أن لفظة الجسم لا تدل من حيث اللغة على أن له مجسما فأورد على أصحابه أن الجوهر إذا تركب بجوهر قام بكل واحد منهما تركب واجتماع وائتلاف على حدة لأن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بمحلين، ومن يجوز ذلك من متأخري المعتزلة فقد ارتكب محالا على ما نبين بعد هذا، وإذا كان قام بكل جوهر ائتلاف كان كل جوهر مؤتلفا وهما جوهران فكان مؤلفين، فينبغي أن يكونا جسمين ويكون كل جوهر على حياله جسما، فإذا جوهر واحد كان جسما وأنتم تأبون هذا، فتفرق أصحابه في ذلك فمنهم من التزم هذا وقال: نعم كل جوهر جسم ولكن بشريطة قيام التأليف به إذ هو اسم للمتألف وليس من شرطه كثرة الأجزاء إذ هو ليس باسم للمتكثر، وعند الانفكاك عن صاحبه لا يسمى جسما، لا لانفراده بل لتعريه عن صفة الائتلاف القائمة به ومنهم من قال: إن كل جوهر وإن قام به الائتلاف لا يسمى جسما بل المؤتلفان بمجموعهما جسم واحد، ولم يرض في تحديد الجسم القول بأنه المؤتلف، بل قال: الجسم هو المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم لما فيه من الجواهر المتركبة بحيث لا تحصى كثرة، والله الموفق.
And if it is not so, but the people of language used the word of superiority (tafdil) when there is an increase in one of these three dimensions without the other two, it indicates that the word is not made a name except for that which has absolute composition (tarkib) and there is no need for the establishment of composition in all three aspects to deserve the application of this name to it. Then after the establishment of this meaning, some of his companions claimed that 'jism' (body) is a name for the composed (mu'allaf) but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) were not satisfied with this expression, because it is placed in the language in a way that indicates that it has a composer (mu'allif); it is said: he composed it, so he is a composer, and that is composed. And in the word 'jism' there is nothing indicating that it has a composer (mujassim); rather the indication that it has a composer is rational ('aqliyya), not linguistic (lughawiyya), while the indication that the composed has a composer is linguistic. So it is not valid to define 'jism' as composed or compound composite (murakkab majmu'). Al-Ash'ari defined it as the collected (mujtama'), and it is sometimes said: the combined (mu'talaf) or the compound (mutarakkab), because these words do not indicate by their form from the perspective of language that the named thing has a gatherer (jami'), composer, or compounder, just as the word 'jism' does not indicate linguistically that it has a composer (mujassim). Then an objection was raised against his companions: when a substance (jawhar) is composed of another substance, composition, aggregation, and combination occur in each one separately, because the single accident ('arad) cannot subsist in two loci. And whoever permits that among the later Mu'tazilites has committed an impossibility, as we will explain later. And if combination subsists in each substance, then each substance is combined, and they are two substances, so they are two combined things; therefore they should be two bodies, and each substance individually should be a body. Thus a single substance should be a body, but you refuse this. So his companions differed in this: among them were those who adhered to this and said: yes, every substance is a body but with the condition of combination subsisting in it, for it is a name for the combined (muta'allaf) and numerous parts are not required for it, for it is not a name for the multiplied (mutakaththir). And when separated from its companion, it is not called a body, not because of its isolation but because of being divested of the attribute of combination subsisting in it. And among them were those who said: every substance, even if combination subsists in it, is not called a body; rather the two combined things together are one body. And he was not satisfied with defining 'jism' as the combined (mu'talaf), but said: 'jism' is two combined things and upwards. And when this is known, it is known that among the parts of the world are bodies, since they contain numerous composed substances beyond count. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the definition of jism (body) and whether a single jawhar (substance) can be called a body. The author begins by noting that the linguistic usage of the word 'tafdil' (superiority) shows that a thing can be called 'body' if it has absolute composition, without requiring all three dimensions. Then he reports the claim of some of al-Ash'ari's companions that 'jism' means 'composed' (mu'allaf). The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) reject this because the word 'composed' linguistically implies a composer, whereas 'jism' does not. The indication that a body has a composer is rational, not linguistic. Al-Ash'ari himself defined it as 'the collected' (mujtama') or 'the combined' (mu'talaf), which similarly do not imply a composer linguistically. An objection is then raised: if two substances combine (i.e., form a body), then each substance individually undergoes combination (since the accident of combination cannot subsist in two loci), so each should be a body. Thus even a single substance if it has combination would be a body, which is rejected. The companions then split: some accept that a single substance with combination is a body but only when the combination subsists; when it separates, it ceases to be a body. Others say two substances together form one body, not each. The author concludes that some parts of the world are bodies because they contain countless composed substances.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
تأليف
مؤلف
مجسم

Checkpoint
Why did the verifiers reject defining jism as 'mu'allaf'?
A) Because 'mu'allaf' implies a composer (mu'allif) linguistically, while jism does not.
B) Because jism requires three dimensions, but mu'allaf does not.
C) Because mu'allaf implies numerous parts, while jism can be a single substance.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites objected to it.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'jism' must avoid linguistic implication of a creator; hence 'mu'allaf' is rejected in favor of 'mujtama'' or 'mu'talaf'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the word 'body' (jism) in your daily life, do you ever pause to consider what you are implicitly affirming about its existence and origin? How does this lesson change the way you think about the words you use to describe the world?
?
The author argues that the word 'mu'allaf' gives a linguistic claim for a composer. Are there any words or phrases you habitually use that might carry a similar implicit claim about God's attributes without your realizing it? How would you examine them after this lesson?
?
This lesson separates rational deduction from linguistic implication. In your own faith, are there beliefs you hold because of linguistic tradition rather than rational proof? How can you ensure your 'ilm is built on the right foundations?

The Objection: Single Substance as Body
Al-Nasafi presents an objection from the opponents of the view that a single substance is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if combination occurs in each substance individually, then each substance alone should be a body.

Core Idea
A single accident cannot be in two substances, so combination must be in each, making each a body.

After establishing the definition, Al-Nasafi presents a critical objection: if two substances combine, the accident of combination ('i'tilaf) must subsist in each separately (since a single accident cannot subsist in two loci). Therefore, each substance becomes 'mu'talaf' (combined) and thus a body. This leads to the conclusion that a single substance with combination is a body, which some reject. This lesson examines that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Focus on the reasoning: when two substances combine, composition occurs in each one individually. Understand why the accident of combination cannot be shared between two substances.
The key principle: one accident cannot subsist in two loci.
2
Trace the logical steps
Follow: if each substance has combination, each is 'mu'talaf'. If 'mu'talaf' means body, then each is a body. Thus a single substance is a body, which is the objection.
3
Consider the counter-view
The objection is directed at those who deny that a single substance is a body. It forces them to explain how two substances can form one body without each being a body.
This sets up the differing opinions in the next lesson.
