Two Positions on the Single Substance
Al-Nasafi records the disagreement among Ash'ari's followers and states his own view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions among Al-Ash'ari's companions regarding whether a single substance with combination is a body.

Core Idea
Some say every substance with combination is a body; others say only two combined substances together form one body.

Faced with the objection, Al-Ash'ari's companions split into two camps. One camp accepted that a single substance with combination is a body, but only when the combination is present. The other camp insisted that a body must consist of at least two substances. Al-Nasafi himself preferred the latter view and defined jism as 'two combined things and upwards'. This lesson explores both positions and the final definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two opinions
Identify the first opinion: every substance is a body conditionally. Note the condition: combination must subsist.
This opinion distinguishes between having combination and being a body only when combined.
2
Identify the second opinion
The second opinion: a single substance is not a body even with combination; only two substances together form one body.
This opinion defines body as 'two combined things and upwards'.
3
Understand Al-Nasafi's preference
Al-Nasafi endorses the second opinion: 'jism is two combined things and upwards.' He then concludes that many parts of the world are bodies due to countless composed substances.
This definition avoids the problem of a single substance being a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا لم يكن كذلك، بل استعمل أهل اللغة لفظة التفضيل عند وجود زيادة في أحد هذه الأبعاد الثلاثة دون الباقين منها دل أن اللفظ ما وضع اسما إلا لما له مطلق التركيب ولا حاجة إلى ثبوت التركيب من الأوجه الثلاثة كله لاستحقاق إطلاق هذا الاسم عليه. ثم بعد ثبوت هذا المعنى، زعم بعض أصحابه أن الجسم اسم للمؤلف ولم يرض المحققون بهذه العبارة فإنها بصيغتها موضوعة في اللغة على وجه يدل على أن له مؤلفا يقال: ألفه فهو مؤلف وذلك مؤلف، وليس في لفظة الجسم ما يدل على أن له مجسما، بل الدلالة على أن له مجسما عقلية لا لغوية، والدلالة على أن للمؤلف مؤلفا لغوية فلم يصلح تحديد الجسم بأنه مؤلف أو مركب مجموع فحدده الأشعري بأنه المجتمع، وربما قيل: المؤتلف أو المتركب لأن هذه الألفاظ لا تدل بصيغتها من حيث اللغة على أن للمسمى به جامعا أو مؤلفا أو مركبا كما أن لفظة الجسم لا تدل من حيث اللغة على أن له مجسما فأورد على أصحابه أن الجوهر إذا تركب بجوهر قام بكل واحد منهما تركب واجتماع وائتلاف على حدة لأن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بمحلين، ومن يجوز ذلك من متأخري المعتزلة فقد ارتكب محالا على ما نبين بعد هذا، وإذا كان قام بكل جوهر ائتلاف كان كل جوهر مؤتلفا وهما جوهران فكان مؤلفين، فينبغي أن يكونا جسمين ويكون كل جوهر على حياله جسما، فإذا جوهر واحد كان جسما وأنتم تأبون هذا، فتفرق أصحابه في ذلك فمنهم من التزم هذا وقال: نعم كل جوهر جسم ولكن بشريطة قيام التأليف به إذ هو اسم للمتألف وليس من شرطه كثرة الأجزاء إذ هو ليس باسم للمتكثر، وعند الانفكاك عن صاحبه لا يسمى جسما، لا لانفراده بل لتعريه عن صفة الائتلاف القائمة به ومنهم من قال: إن كل جوهر وإن قام به الائتلاف لا يسمى جسما بل المؤتلفان بمجموعهما جسم واحد، ولم يرض في تحديد الجسم القول بأنه المؤتلف، بل قال: الجسم هو المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم لما فيه من الجواهر المتركبة بحيث لا تحصى كثرة، والله الموفق.
And if it is not so, but the people of language used the word of superiority (tafdil) when there is an increase in one of these three dimensions without the other two, it indicates that the word is not made a name except for that which has absolute composition (tarkib) and there is no need for the establishment of composition in all three aspects to deserve the application of this name to it. Then after the establishment of this meaning, some of his companions claimed that 'jism' (body) is a name for the composed (mu'allaf) but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) were not satisfied with this expression, because it is placed in the language in a way that indicates that it has a composer (mu'allif); it is said: he composed it, so he is a composer, and that is composed. And in the word 'jism' there is nothing indicating that it has a composer (mujassim); rather the indication that it has a composer is rational ('aqliyya), not linguistic (lughawiyya), while the indication that the composed has a composer is linguistic. So it is not valid to define 'jism' as composed or compound composite (murakkab majmu'). Al-Ash'ari defined it as the collected (mujtama'), and it is sometimes said: the combined (mu'talaf) or the compound (mutarakkab), because these words do not indicate by their form from the perspective of language that the named thing has a gatherer (jami'), composer, or compounder, just as the word 'jism' does not indicate linguistically that it has a composer (mujassim). Then an objection was raised against his companions: when a substance (jawhar) is composed of another substance, composition, aggregation, and combination occur in each one separately, because the single accident ('arad) cannot subsist in two loci. And whoever permits that among the later Mu'tazilites has committed an impossibility, as we will explain later. And if combination subsists in each substance, then each substance is combined, and they are two substances, so they are two combined things; therefore they should be two bodies, and each substance individually should be a body. Thus a single substance should be a body, but you refuse this. So his companions differed in this: among them were those who adhered to this and said: yes, every substance is a body but with the condition of combination subsisting in it, for it is a name for the combined (muta'allaf) and numerous parts are not required for it, for it is not a name for the multiplied (mutakaththir). And when separated from its companion, it is not called a body, not because of its isolation but because of being divested of the attribute of combination subsisting in it. And among them were those who said: every substance, even if combination subsists in it, is not called a body; rather the two combined things together are one body. And he was not satisfied with defining 'jism' as the combined (mu'talaf), but said: 'jism' is two combined things and upwards. And when this is known, it is known that among the parts of the world are bodies, since they contain numerous composed substances beyond count. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the definition of jism (body) and whether a single jawhar (substance) can be called a body. The author begins by noting that the linguistic usage of the word 'tafdil' (superiority) shows that a thing can be called 'body' if it has absolute composition, without requiring all three dimensions. Then he reports the claim of some of al-Ash'ari's companions that 'jism' means 'composed' (mu'allaf). The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) reject this because the word 'composed' linguistically implies a composer, whereas 'jism' does not. The indication that a body has a composer is rational, not linguistic. Al-Ash'ari himself defined it as 'the collected' (mujtama') or 'the combined' (mu'talaf), which similarly do not imply a composer linguistically. An objection is then raised: if two substances combine (i.e., form a body), then each substance individually undergoes combination (since the accident of combination cannot subsist in two loci), so each should be a body. Thus even a single substance if it has combination would be a body, which is rejected. The companions then split: some accept that a single substance with combination is a body but only when the combination subsists; when it separates, it ceases to be a body. Others say two substances together form one body, not each. The author concludes that some parts of the world are bodies because they contain countless composed substances.

Key Terms
متألف
متكثر
مؤتلفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, how many substances are required to form a body?
A) At least one substance with combination.
B) At least two combined substances.
C) Any number of substances.
D) Exactly three dimensions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defines jism as two combined things and upwards, rejecting the view that a single substance can be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Muslims scholars debated what makes something a 'body'. Do you ever think about the definitions of the things you hold most sacred? What is the danger of using loose or careless language when speaking about Allah and His creation?
?
Al-Nasafi preferred one definition over another. Are you someone who seeks out the most precise understanding of your beliefs, or do you settle for convenient labels? What step can you take this week to refine your understanding of a core Islamic concept?
?
The two positions both rely on the accident of 'combination'. In your own life, what 'accidents' do you rely on to define who you are — status, wealth, relationships — that are not essential to your true identity before Allah? How can you detach from these and focus on what is essential?

Defining the Accident: Linguistic and Technical Meanings
Al-Nasafi defines 'arad linguistically and as a technical term among mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'arad and its various technical definitions among the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
An accident is a non-substantial property that cannot persist.

Al-Nasafi begins by clarifying a key term in kalam: 'arad (accident). First, he explains its everyday linguistic sense—something impermanent. Then he shows how theologians refined that sense into a precise technical term. This passage also reveals the diversity of definitions among early mutakallimun, all circling the same core idea.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice that the passage starts with 'wa amma' (as for), indicating a new topic.
2
Distinguish linguistic vs. technical meaning
Al-Nasafi first gives the linguistic meaning: something impermanent. Then he gives the technical usage among theologians: attributes of originated things.
The Quranic example of a cloud illustrates the linguistic sense.
3
Identify the list of technical definitions
After stating the general technical meaning, Al-Nasafi lists several specific definitions from different theologians. Find each one and note how they all emphasize non-persistence.
There are four definitions introduced by 'qila' (it was said) and then al-Ash'ari's definition.
4
Reflect on the common theme
Despite the different wordings, all definitions agree that an accident cannot endure or exist independently. This is the core idea.
Think about how this contrasts with substance (jawhar) which persists.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Attempt the multiple-choice question to check if you can identify which statement is not a definition from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العرض فهو في موضع اللغة اسم لما لا دوام له ولا ثبات يقال: عرض لفلان كذا وفلان في عرض شغل أو مرض، ولهذا سمى السحاب عارضا على ما قال تعالى خبرا عن قوم عاد: هَذَا عَارِضٌ مُمطِرُنا (الأحقاف 24) وهو في عرف المتكلمين اسم للصفات الثابتة للمحدثات زائدة على ذواتها كالألوان والأكوان والطعوم والروائح و أشباه ذلك، فاختلفت عبارات المتكلمين في تحديده، فقال بعضهم : العرض ما يستحيل بقاؤه . وقيل : ما يوجد بالجوهر. وقيل : ما لا يبقى زمانين. وقيل: هو ما يستحيل وجوده إلا بين عدمين. وذكر الأشعري في بعض كتبه : أنه ما يعترض على غيره ويبطل من غير بطلان
As for 'arad (accident), in the language it is a name for what has no permanence (dawam) nor stability (thabat). It is said: an accident befell so-and-so, and so-and-so is in an accidental state of occupation or illness. For this reason the cloud was called 'arid (passing), as Allah said, telling of the people of 'Ad: 'This is a passing cloud giving us rain' (al-Ahqaf 24). And in the technical usage of the mutakallimun (theologians), it is a name for the attributes (sifat) subsisting in originated things (muhdathat), superadded to their essences (dhawat), such as colors, modes (akwan), tastes, smells, and the like. The expressions of the mutakallimun differed in defining it: some said: 'arad is that whose persistence (baqa') is impossible. And it was said: that which exists in a substance (jawhar). And it was said: that which does not last for two moments (zamanayn). And it was said: it is that whose existence is impossible except between two non-existences. And al-Ash'ari mentioned in some of his books that it is that which occurs to another (ya'taridu 'ala ghayrihi) and perishes without the other perishing.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'arad (accident) first from a linguistic perspective and then as a technical term of the mutakallimun. Linguistically, 'arad refers to something without permanence or stability, e.g., a passing affair. The author gives the example of a cloud (al-'arid) from the Quran. Technically, the mutakallimun use 'arad for the attributes that come to exist in created things (muhdathat) and are additional to their essences, like colors, modes (akwan), tastes, and smells. Theologians differed in their precise definitions. The author lists several definitions: (1) that whose persistence is impossible (i.e., it perishes quickly), (2) that which exists in a substance (i.e., it cannot exist independently), (3) that which does not remain for two moments (i.e., it is instantly renewed), (4) that whose existence is only possible between two non-existences (i.e., it is created and then annihilated), and finally al-Ash'ari's definition: it is an attribute that 'occurs to' another entity and perishes without that entity perishing. These definitions all capture the idea that an accident is a non-substantial, non-enduring property.

Key Terms
عرض ('arad - accident)
جوهر (jawhar - substance)
صفات (sifat - attributes)
محدثات (muhdathat - originated things)
بقاء (baqa' - persistence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse the linguistic meaning of 'arad (accidental event) with the technical kalam meaning, thinking they are the same.
Correction: The linguistic meaning is broad (anything impermanent), while the technical meaning is specific: a non-substantial attribute that inheres in a substance and perishes quickly.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a definition of 'arad mentioned by the author?
A) That whose persistence is impossible.
B) That which exists in a substance.
C) That which exists independently of a substance.
D) That which does not last for two moments.

Key Takeaway
The term 'arad (accident) in kalam refers to a non-enduring attribute that exists in a substance, and theologians offered multiple definitions emphasizing its impermanence and dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a quality you possess that you consider permanent—like your intelligence or beauty. According to this lesson, such qualities are accidents that do not persist. How does knowing that they are constantly renewed by Allah change how you view them? Are you willing to lose them in an instant and trust His wisdom?
?
The passage mentions that the cloud is called 'arid because it passes. In your life, what 'clouds' have you been clinging to that are merely passing? What specific step can you take today to shift your hope from these temporary accidents to the eternal substance of Allah's mercy?

The First View: Every Atom as a Body with Composition
Nasafi states the first view on atom and body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first position among the mutakallimun that every atom is a body provided composition subsists in it.

Core Idea
For some scholars, any atom with composition is already a body.

The passage begins by noting a disagreement among Nasafi's followers. One group holds that every atom (jawhar) qualifies as a body (jism) as long as the attribute of composition is present. This view challenges the common assumption that a body must have multiple parts. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the condition 'with the condition of composition subsisting in it'.
The passage is a single continuous stance.
2
Identify key terms
Locate the terms jawhar, jism, ta'lif, i'tilaf. These are central to kalam ontology.
3
Understand the isolation case
Why is the atom not called a body when separated? The passage says it is not because of isolation but because it loses composition.
This emphasizes the primacy of composition over multiplicity of parts.
4
Reflect on the definition
Consider how this view differs from the intuitive idea that a body must have many parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنتم تأبون هذا، فتفرق أصحابه في ذلك فمنهم من التزم هذا وقال: نعم كل جوهر جسم ولكن بشريطة قيام التأليف به إذ هو اسم للمتألف وليس من شرطه كثرة الأجزاء إذ هو ليس باسم للمتكثر، وعند الانفكاك عن صاحبه لا يسمى جسما، لا لانفراده بل لتعريه عن صفة الائتلاف القائمة به ومنهم من قال: إن كل جوهر وإن قام به الائتلاف لا يسمى جسما بل المؤتلفان بمجموعهما جسم واحد، ولم يرض في تحديد الجسم القول بأنه المؤتلف، بل قال: الجسم هو المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم لما فيه من الجواهر المركبة بحيث لا تحصى كثرة، والله الموفق.
And you reject this, so his followers differed in that: among them is one who adhered to this and said: Yes, every atom is a body, but with the condition of composition (ta'lif) subsisting in it, for it is a name for the composed thing, and multiplicity of parts is not a condition for it, for it is not a name for the multi-part. And when it is separated from its companion, it is not called a body, not because of its isolation but because it is devoid of the attribute of composition subsisting in it. And among them is one who said: Not every atom, even if composition subsists in it, is called a body; rather the two composed things together are one body. And he was not satisfied in defining body with the statement that it is the composed thing, but said: The body is two composed things and upwards. And when this is known, it is known that some parts of the world are a body of two composed things and upwards. And when this is known, it is known that some parts of the world are a body because of the composite atoms it contains such that their multiplicity cannot be counted. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents two differing views among the mutakallimun regarding whether every atom (jawhar) can be called a body (jism). The author begins by noting the disagreement. The first view holds that every atom is a body, provided that composition (ta'lif) is actually subsisting in it. This is because 'body' is a name for the composed entity; it does not require a multiplicity of parts, only the presence of composition. When the atom is separated from its companion and becomes isolated, it is no longer called a body—not because it is alone, but because it has lost the attribute of composition. The second view rejects this: even if composition subsists in an atom, it is not called a body. Instead, only the aggregate of at least two composed atoms constitutes a single body. This second group defines body as 'two composed things and upwards' (al-mu'talifan fa-sa'idan). The author then draws two conclusions from this definition: first, that some parts of the world are a body consisting of at least two composed things; second, that some parts of the world are a body because of the countless composite atoms within them. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
جسم (jism)
تأليف (ta'lif)
ائتلاف (i'tilaf)
متألف (mu'talif)
متكثر (mutakaththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body must have many parts (kathrat al-ajza').
Correction: The first view holds that composition alone suffices; multiplicity of parts is not a condition.

Checkpoint
According to the first view, when is an atom not called a body?
A) When it is isolated from its companion
B) When it is devoid of composition
C) When it has many parts
D) When it is composed with another atom

Key Takeaway
The first view defines body by the presence of composition, not by the number of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as an atom's identity as a body depends on its internal composition, what internal principles compose your character? Are you just a collection of parts, or is there a unifying composition that makes you a true servant of Allah? What would you change starting today?
?
The passage says an atom is not called a body when alone because it lacks composition. In your solitude, do you lack the 'composition' of faith and virtue? How can you maintain your identity before Allah even when no one sees you?
?
This lesson shows that a thing's name can depend on a subtle condition, not on obvious quantity. What in your life have you been defining by quantity (e.g., how many prayers, how much charity) when Allah may be looking at the quality of composition (e.g., sincerity, consistency)? Ask yourself honestly: Is your worship composed with the right intention, or is it just a heap of actions?

The Second View: Body as Two Composed Things Upwards
Nasafi presents the second view and its conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second position that a body requires at least two composed atoms and its implications for the composition of the world.

Core Idea
A body is not a single atom, but the union of two or more composed atoms.

The second group of mutakallimun disagrees with the first. They maintain that even if an atom has composition, it is not yet a body. Instead, a body is formed by at least two composed atoms together. Nasafi then draws two conclusions from this definition about the parts of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second view
Notice the phrase 'al-mu'talifan fa-sa'idan' (two composed things and upwards).
2
Define body according to this group
Body is defined as at least two composed atoms.
This contrasts with the first view.
3
Understand the first conclusion
Some parts of the world are a body of at least two composed things.
This follows directly from the definition.
4
Understand the second conclusion
Some parts of the world contain countless composite atoms, making them a body.
This refers to larger bodies composed of many atoms.
5
Appreciate the closing supplication
The passage ends with 'And Allah is the grantor of success.'
A common phrase in Islamic scholarly texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنتم تأبون هذا، فتفرق أصحابه في ذلك فمنهم من التزم هذا وقال: نعم كل جوهر جسم ولكن بشريطة قيام التأليف به إذ هو اسم للمتألف وليس من شرطه كثرة الأجزاء إذ هو ليس باسم للمتكثر، وعند الانفكاك عن صاحبه لا يسمى جسما، لا لانفراده بل لتعريه عن صفة الائتلاف القائمة به ومنهم من قال: إن كل جوهر وإن قام به الائتلاف لا يسمى جسما بل المؤتلفان بمجموعهما جسم واحد، ولم يرض في تحديد الجسم القول بأنه المؤتلف، بل قال: الجسم هو المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم المؤتلفان فصاعدا، وإذا عرف هذا عرف أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو جسم لما فيه من الجواهر المركبة بحيث لا تحصى كثرة، والله الموفق.
And you reject this, so his followers differed in that: among them is one who adhered to this and said: Yes, every atom is a body, but with the condition of composition (ta'lif) subsisting in it, for it is a name for the composed thing, and multiplicity of parts is not a condition for it, for it is not a name for the multi-part. And when it is separated from its companion, it is not called a body, not because of its isolation but because it is devoid of the attribute of composition subsisting in it. And among them is one who said: Not every atom, even if composition subsists in it, is called a body; rather the two composed things together are one body. And he was not satisfied in defining body with the statement that it is the composed thing, but said: The body is two composed things and upwards. And when this is known, it is known that some parts of the world are a body of two composed things and upwards. And when this is known, it is known that some parts of the world are a body because of the composite atoms it contains such that their multiplicity cannot be counted. And Allah is the grantor of success.

Commentary
This passage presents two differing views among the mutakallimun regarding whether every atom (jawhar) can be called a body (jism). The author begins by noting the disagreement. The first view holds that every atom is a body, provided that composition (ta'lif) is actually subsisting in it. This is because 'body' is a name for the composed entity; it does not require a multiplicity of parts, only the presence of composition. When the atom is separated from its companion and becomes isolated, it is no longer called a body—not because it is alone, but because it has lost the attribute of composition. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

جسم (jism)
ائتلاف (i'tilaf)
المؤتلفان (al-mu'talifan)
فصاعدا (fa-sa'idan)

Misconception: Any atom with composition is automatically a body.
Correction: The second view requires at least two composed atoms to form one body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define something—whether a concept, a person, or a situation—do you ever settle for a definition that is too narrow, like calling a single atom a body? Where in your life are you making that mistake right now?
?
The scholars in this lesson debated the precise definition of 'body.' How carefully do you define the terms of your own faith? Are there areas where you accept vague or incomplete definitions that could lead you to misunderstand Allah's creation?
?
If a body requires at least two composed parts, consider your own spiritual life: what 'parts' (actions, beliefs, intentions) must come together for your faith to be a true 'body'? Is there a part missing?

The Kalam Argument for Indivisible Atoms
The author proves atoms via divine power over composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the argument for the existence of indivisible atoms (jawahir) based on God's power over composition and division.

Core Idea
If God can remove composition from a body, each part becomes indivisible.

This passage presents a classic kalam argument for the existence of atoms (jawahir). The author uses a dialectical approach, asking the opponent to affirm God's creation of composition in bodies, then questioning whether God can undo it. The answer leads to the conclusion that there must be indivisible parts. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's direct questioning style.
2
Identify the key question
The author asks: 'Who created the composition (ijtima') in this particular body?' The opponent must answer 'God'. This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Follow the dilemma
The author presents two possible answers to the follow-up question 'Can God remove composition?'. If they say no, God is impotent; if yes, then when composition is removed, parts become indivisible.
This is a classic kalam proof by contradiction.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that if God can remove composition, each part remains indivisible, proving the existence of jawahir (indivisible atoms).
The author notes that many proofs exist on this topic but he chooses brevity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من معلوماته ومقدوراته فلا نهاية لهما، ولا يقال فيهما إن إحداهما أكثر من الأخرى، فما أثبتنا فيه التزايد والكثرة فهو محصور متناهي، وما لا تناهي له لم يثبت فيه الكثرة فيطلب فيه المعارضة ويقال لهم : من الذي خلق الاجتماع في أجزاء هذا الجسم المعين ؟ فلابد من أن يقولوا الله تعالى، فيقال لهم: وهل يقدر على دفع الاجتماع وتخليق الافتراق بعد الاجتماع، فإن قالوا: لا، فقد عجزوا الله تعالى عن إعدام ما خلقه من الأعراض وتخليق ضده بدله، وإن قالوا: نعم، قلنا: إذا ارتفع الاجتماع عن الأجزاء لم يبق جزء قابل للتجزؤ إذ القابل للتجزؤ ما كان مجتمعا في نفسه، وما ليس بمجتمع فليس بقابل للتجزي، فإذا بقي كل جزء غير قابل للتجزي، فكانت أجزاء لا تتجزأ وهي المعنية بقولنا: جواهر، وهذه مسألة عظيمة فيها حجج كثيرة وشبه جمة صنف المتكلمون فيها تصانيف كثيرة من الجانبين، ولا حاجة بنا إلى إيراد ذلك على أن كتابنا هذا لا يتسع للكلام في مثل تلك المسائل، فأعرضنا عن ذلك إيثارا للتخفيف، والله الموفق.
From His knowledge and His powers, there is no end to them, and it is not said concerning them that one of them is more than the other. So whatever we have established increase and multiplicity in is limited and finite, and what has no end does not have multiplicity established in it, so opposition is sought therein. And it is said to them: Who created the composition in the parts of this particular body? They must say: God Most High. Then it is said to them: Is He able to remove the composition and create separation after composition? If they say no, they have rendered God Most High incapable of annihilating what He created of accidents and creating their opposite in their place. And if they say yes, we say: If composition is removed from the parts, no part remains divisible, for the divisible is that which is composed in itself, and what is not composed is not divisible. So if every part remains indivisible, then they are parts that do not divide, and they are meant by our saying 'jawahir' (atoms). This is a great issue, containing many proofs and numerous ambiguities; the mutakallimun have composed many works on it from both sides. We have no need to present that, since our book does not accommodate discussion of such issues. So we have refrained from that, preferring brevity, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of indivisible atoms (jawahir). The author begins by establishing that God's knowledge and power are infinite, but this does not apply to created things. He then presents a dialectical argument: He asks the opponents (who believe in infinite divisibility) who created the composition in a particular body. They must answer God. Then he asks whether God can remove that composition and create separation. If they say no, they attribute impotence to God, which is blasphemous. If they say yes, then when composition is removed, no part remains divisible, because divisibility requires internal composition (being composed in itself). Thus each part becomes indivisible, and these are the jawahir. The author notes that this topic has many arguments and counterarguments, but he will not delve into them for the sake of brevity.

Key Terms
جواهر (jawahir)
تجزؤ (tajazzu')
اجتماع (ijtima')
افتراق (iftiraq)
أعراض (a'rad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument limits God's power by suggesting He cannot divide matter indefinitely.
Correction: The argument does not limit God's power; rather, it uses God's power to show that infinite divisibility is impossible in physical bodies, leading to the existence of atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows if the opponents admit that God can remove composition from a body?
A) The body remains divisible into smaller parts.
B) Each part becomes indivisible, proving the existence of atoms.
C) God's power is limited.
D) Composition is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The existence of indivisible atoms (jawahir) is deduced from God's ability to annihilate composition, leaving each part without internal divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can remove the composition of a body and leave it as indivisible atoms, what does that tell you about your own physical existence? Do you live as if your body is merely a collection of parts that you control, or as if every atom is held together by His will? How would your daily choices change if you truly felt His power over the smallest parts of your being?
?
Consider a habit or sin that seems 'composed' into your life—something you feel cannot be broken apart. This lesson shows that God can remove composition from a body. Do you believe He can remove the 'composition' of that sin from your heart? If so, why do you not ask Him, with certainty, to do so? What are you holding back?
?
The argument forces a choice: either God is incapable of removing composition (absurd) or He can and does, leaving indivisible atoms. Do you accept that God's power is absolute over every detail, including the very structure of matter? If you truly accepted this, would you fear anything else? Or would you surrender your entire life to His power, trusting Him to rearrange even the most broken parts of your soul?

Definition and Types of Accidents (A'rād)
Definition, enumeration, and purpose of accidents in proving world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of accidents in Islamic theology, their main types, and why proving their distinctness from substances is essential for demonstrating the temporal creation of the world.

Core Idea
Accidents are real, non-permanent attributes that reveal the world's createdness.

The concept of 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) is central to the kalām argument for the world's beginning. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafī defines accidents, lists their categories, and explains why proving their reality is a stepping stone to demonstrating that the universe had a Creator. He also notes that certain groups denied accidents altogether, making this a contested topic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Note the key terms like ʿaraḍ (accident) and jawhar (substance).
Pay attention to the list of examples.
2
Identify the definition and categories
Notice that accidents are defined as 'meanings other than substances' and are close to thirty types. List the ones mentioned: colors, modes, tastes, smells, knowledges, powers, wills, beliefs, doubts, moistures, dryness.
The list is not exhaustive.
3
Understand the theological motivation
The ultimate goal is to prove the temporal origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). How does establishing accidents help? Because accidents are contingent and require a creator.
4
Recognize the opposing views
The author mentions Materialists (Dahriyya), Dualists (Thanawiyya), and Abu Bakr al-Asamm among the Mu'tazila as denying accidents. Consider why they might deny them.
Denying accidents would undermine the proof of temporality.
5
Reflect on the approximate nature
The text says 'close to thirty types' and includes 'others', indicating flexibility. This is a common misunderstanding: it is not a fixed, exact list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في الأعراض فنقول : إن الأعراض ثابتة عند أكثر العقلاء، وهي الألوان والأكوان والطعوم والروائح، والعلوم والقدر والإرادات والاعتقادات والشكوك، والرطوبات واليبوسات وغيرها، وهي عند التحصيل قريبة من ثلاثين نوعا في أكثرها نزاع بين مثبتيها ، ونفاتها أصلا طوائف الدهرية والثنوية وأبي بكر الأصم من جملة المعتزلة، ويجب صرف العناية إلى إقامة الدليل على ثبوتها معاني غير الجواهر، إذ بثبوتها يتوصل إلى إثبات حدوث العالم.
As for accidents, we say: Accidents are established according to most rational people, and they are colors, modes, tastes, smells, knowledges, powers, wills, beliefs, doubts, moistures, dryness, and others. And they are, upon investigation, close to thirty types, and concerning most of them there is dispute between those who affirm them and those who deny them, such as the groups of the Materialists, the Dualists, and Abu Bakr al-Asamm among the Mu'tazila. And one must direct attention to establishing proof of their existence as meanings other than substances, for through their establishment one reaches the proof of the temporal origination of the world.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of accidents (aʿrāḍ). The author begins by stating that accidents are established according to most rational people. He then enumerates the types: colors, modes (akwān: motion, rest, etc.), tastes, smells, knowledges, powers, wills, beliefs, doubts, moistures, dryness, and others. He notes that upon investigation they number close to thirty types, and there is disagreement about most of them: some deny them entirely, such as the Materialists (Dahriyya), the Dualists (Thanawiyya), and Abu Bakr al-Asamm from the Mu'tazila. The author then states the necessity of proving that accidents are meanings (maʿānī) distinct from substances (jawāhir), because through establishing their existence one can prove the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world. This sets the stage for the proof that follows in the next section.

Misconception: The list of accident types is exactly thirty and exhaustive.
Correction: The text says 'close to thirty' and includes 'others', indicating it is approximate and not exhaustive.

Checkpoint

A) To refute the Mu'tazila.
B) To prove the temporal origination of the world.
C) To establish the existence of God.
D) To classify the types of accidents.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are real, non-substantial entities whose existence proves the world's temporal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have just read that accidents are real, non-permanent attributes that prove the world was created. Look at the colors, tastes, and smells you experience today. Do you live as though they are mere illusions, or as signs that everything around you is contingent and dependent on the Creator? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly saw each changing attribute as a witness to God's power?
?
The passage mentions groups like the Materialists who deny accidents entirely. Have you ever caught yourself acting as if the material world is self-sufficient, ignoring the constant change that points to its need for a Sustainer? What specific habit could you adopt today to remind yourself that every accident—every blink, every thought—is a sign of your own contingency?

The author says one must 'direct attention to establishing proof' of accidents. Consider your own beliefs: do you have a reasoned conviction that the world is created, or have you merely absorbed it from your environment? The Objection About Blackness and the First Answer
Nasafi presents an objection about blackness's attachment and its first answer based on essential specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the objection about accidental attachment and the first answer that blackness is essentially tied to its specific subject.

Core Idea
Blackness attaches to its subject by essence, not by an additional meaning.

This passage illustrates how mutakallimun defend the view that accidents have specific subjects. By analyzing a three-pronged objection and the answer that blackness is essentially tied to its subject, you will see how they avoid absurdities like accidents subsisting in accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: the objector presents three possible responses from the defender and refutes each.
The objection is a dilemma with three branches.
2
Identify the three alternatives
The objector asks: is the attachment of blackness to its subject due to essence, due to a meaning, or neither? Each leads to a contradiction or absurdity.
The third alternative is a 'demolishing' of the proof.
3
Understand the answer
The answer from many mutakallimun: blackness is attached to its subject by its very essence (li-'aynihi). Therefore, its existence cannot be conceived in another subject.
This avoids the need for an additional meaning subsisting in an accident.
4
Reflect on the difference from change
The answer clarifies that this does not negate change (e.g., black becoming white) because change involves the entire accident being replaced, not transferring to another subject.
The common misunderstanding is addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا السواد إذا وجد في محل هل كان من الجائز بأن وجد في محل آخر على البدل؟ فإن قلتم: لا فقد تركتم أصلكم، لأن من قولكم جواز ذلك، وإن قلتم: كان ذلك جائزا قلنا: اختصاصه بهذا المحل على جواز اختصاصه بمحل آخر كان لذاته أم لمعنى؟ فإن قلتم: لذاته فقد ناقضتم، لأنكم قلتم بجواز وجود عين هذا الذات في غير هذا المحل. وإن قلتم لمعنى، فقد أثبتم للسواد معنى قائما به ووقعتم في المحال، لأن قيام العرض بالعرض غير جائز عند القائلين بثبوت الأعراض. وإن قلتم: اختص بهذا المحل مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، فقد هدمتم دليلكم، وأبطلتم حجتكم. أجبنا عن ذلك: بأن كثيرا من المتكلمين قالوا إن السواد اختص بهذا المحل لعينه فلا يتصور وجوده إلا مختصا بهذا المحل حتى قال هؤلاء: إن الله تعالى أعاد هذا السواد بعد العدم لأعاده في عين هذا المحل لا في غيره، لاقتضاء عين السواد ذلك، فسقط هذا السؤال على هذا الأصل، وظهر الفرق لأن الأسود وجد بعد ذلك أبيض على ما قررنا وهذا يخالفه
If they say: 'When blackness exists in a subject, is it possible that it could have existed in another subject instead? If you say: No, then you have abandoned your principle, because your statement permits that [possibility]. And if you say: That is possible, we say: Its attachment to this subject despite the possibility of its attachment to another subject — is it due to its essence or due to a meaning? If you say: Due to its essence, then you have contradicted [yourselves], because you [previously] said that it is possible for this identical essence to exist in another subject. And if you say: Due to a meaning, then you have affirmed for blackness a meaning subsisting in it and have fallen into an absurdity, because the subsistence of an accident in an accident is not permissible according to those who affirm the existence of accidents. And if you say: It is attached to this subject while it is possible that it is not attached to it — not due to its essence nor due to a meaning — then you have demolished your proof and invalidated your argument.' We answer that: Many of the mutakallimun said that blackness is attached to this subject by its very essence, so its existence cannot be conceived except attached to this subject, such that these [scholars] said: If Allah Most High were to bring back this blackness after non-existence, He would bring it back in this very subject, not in another, because the essence of blackness necessitates that. So this question falls away according to this principle, and the difference appears because the black thing later became white according to what we have established, and this opposes [the case].
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the nature of accidents, specifically blackness (sawad). The objector presents a dilemma: if blackness exists in a subject, could it have existed in a different subject instead? He offers three possible answers to the defender. First, if the defender says it is not possible, he abandons his own principle which permits such possibility. Second, if he says it is possible, then the actual attachment of blackness to this particular subject must be either due to its essence (dhat) or due to a meaning (ma'na) additional to it. If due to its essence, he contradicts his previous claim that the essence could exist in another subject. If due to a meaning, he falls into absurdity because that would mean an accident (the meaning) subsists in another accident (blackness), which is impossible. Third, if he says the attachment is neither due to essence nor meaning but simply happens while possibility remains, then his proof collapses. The answer from the mutakallimun is that many of them hold that blackness is attached to its specific subject by its very essence. They illustrate this by saying that if Allah were to recreate that blackness, He would recreate it in the exact same subject, not another, because the essence of blackness necessitates that particular subject. Therefore, the objection fails, and the difference between this case and the one under discussion (where a black thing becomes white) is clear.

Key Terms
سواد
محل
اختصاص
ذات
معنى
عرض
قيام العرض بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if blackness essentially requires a specific subject, then change (like black becoming white) is impossible.
Correction: The answer clarifies that change involves the whole accident being replaced, not the same accident transferring. The case of blackness being recreated in the same subject is hypothetical; in reality, blackness can be replaced by whiteness through divine action.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, why does blackness attach to a specific subject?
A) Due to a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in blackness
B) Due to the nature of the subject itself
C) Due to its very essence (dhat)
D) By mere coincidence without cause

Key Takeaway
The first answer resolves the objection by asserting that blackness is essentially tied to its specific subject, so no additional meaning is needed and the dilemma is avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire identity were tied to a single attribute like color, as blackness is tied to its subject, would you be able to accept that God could change you fundamentally? Does your faith allow for the possibility that your core traits are not fixed by essence but by His will? Examine a personal attribute you believe is essential to you—could you still be you without it?
?
The objector assumes that what is possible for a thing must be actualizable without contradiction. Do you ever assume that your own possibilities are unlimited? How does the mutakallimun's insistence on essential natures challenge your view of personal growth and change?
?
When you reflect on your own sins or virtues, do you consider them as accidents that could be replaced, like blackness becoming white? Or do you see them as essential to your identity? How does this lesson's distinction affect your hope for transformation?

Blackness Necessitates a Subject in General: The Second View
Nasafi presents the second answer that blackness necessitates a subject in general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why blackness requires a subject in general, not a specific one, and how this distinction between absolute judgment and relation is argued.

Core Idea
Blackness demands any subject, not a specific one, because specification is relation, not essence.

This lesson examines a second position held by some mutakallimun: that accidents like blackness necessitate a subject absolutely, not a particular subject. The argument hinges on the distinction between absolute judgment and relation. Nasafi clarifies that while blackness requires a subject, the specific identity of that subject is accidental, not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument unfold naturally.
Notice how the argument moves from a general statement to a detailed example.
2
Distinguish absolute judgment from relation
The passage argues that blackness necessitates a subject by its essence, but the specific subject (e.g., Zayd) is a relation, not an essential requirement. Understand why relations do not provide a cause.
The key is that 'judgment' (hukm) is what provides the cause, not the relation (idafa).
3
Examine the example of blackness
Consider the illustration: saying 'the black thing is black by blackness' is fine, but saying 'by this blackness' is absurd because it would prevent the subject from becoming black by another blackness. This shows the absolute judgment is what matters.
This example clarifies the difference between a general necessity and a specific one.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
Relate this second view to the main theme: the author has already proven that 'being black' is not from the implications of the essence, but from a meaning. This answer supports that by showing blackness only requires a subject in general.
This reinforces that accidents do not necessitate specific subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المتكلمين من قال: إن السواد يقتضي لذاته محلا ما، وكذا كل عرض، فأما العرض لا يقتضي أن محله زيد أم عمرو لأنه إضافة، والإضافة لا تعلل إنما الحكم هو الذي يعلل، ألا ترى أنا إذا قلنا: الأسود أسود بسواد لزم أن لا يوجد أسود إلا سواد، ولا يجوز أن يقال: هو أسود بهذا السواد فإنه يوجب أن لا يصير أسود بسواد آخر وهذا محال. فظهر بهذا أن مطلق الحكم يعلل لا الإضافة، وإذا كان كذلك كان الحكم المطلق للسواد أنه يقتضي محلا مطلقا وذلك من موجبات ذات السواد، فلا يوجد السواد إلا وجد اقتضاؤه ذلك من غير اعتبار المحل المخصوص، لأن ذلك من باب الإضافة وعرض ما لا يقتضيه على ما قررنا وفيما نحن فيه أبطلنا أن يكون كونه أسود من مقتضيات ذاته فلابد من أن يكون من مقتضيات المعنى، والله الموفق
And among the mutakallimun are those who say: Blackness necessitates by its essence a subject in general, and likewise every accident. But the accident does not necessitate that its subject be Zayd or Amr, because it is a relation, and the relation does not provide a cause; rather the judgment itself provides the cause. Do you not see that if we say: 'The black thing is black by blackness,' it would necessitate that no black thing exists except by blackness, and it is not permissible to say: 'It is black by this blackness,' because that would necessitate that it cannot become black by another blackness, and that is absurd. Thus it becomes clear that the absolute judgment provides the cause, not the relation. And if that is the case, then the absolute judgment for blackness is that it necessitates a subject absolutely, and that is among the implications of the essence of blackness; so blackness does not exist without its implication [of a subject] existing, without consideration of a particular subject, because that [particular] is a matter of relation and an accident that it does not necessitate, according to what we have established. And in what we are discussing, we have invalidated that its being black is from the implications of its essence; rather it must be from the implications of the meaning. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a second answer from another group of mutakallimun. They hold that blackness (sawad) by its essence necessitates a subject (mahall) in general, not a specific one. The key distinction is that an accident (al-‘arad) does not necessitate a particular subject (like Zayd or Amr) because that is a relation (idafa), and relations do not provide a cause (‘illah); rather, the absolute judgment (al-hukm al-mutlaq) is what provides the cause. The author gives an example: if we say 'the black thing is black by blackness,' that is permissible, but if we say 'it is black by this blackness' (pointing to a specific blackness), that would imply that it cannot become black by another blackness, which is absurd. Therefore, the absolute judgment for blackness is that it necessitates a subject absolutely (mahallan mutlaqan), meaning any subject, not a particular one. This is from the implications (mujibat) of the essence of blackness. The specific subject is a matter of relation and does not belong to what blackness necessitates. The author then connects this to the main discussion: they have already proven that 'being black' (kawnuhu aswad) is not from the implications of the essence, but rather from the implications of a meaning (ma‘na). So this second view supports the idea that blackness itself does not require a specific subject, but it does require a subject in general.

Key Terms
سواد
محل
اقتضاء
حكم
إضافة
تعليل
موجبات ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This second answer contradicts the first answer about blackness requiring a subject.
Correction: It does not contradict; it clarifies that blackness requires a subject in general, while the specific subject is accidental and not necessitated by blackness itself.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, what does the absolute judgment (al-hukm al-mutlaq) for blackness necessitate?
A) A specific subject like Zayd
B) A subject in general (mahallan mutlaqan)
C) A meaning (ma'na) subsisting in it
D) No subject at all

Key Takeaway
The second view holds that blackness by essence requires any subject, but the specific subject is a matter of relation, not essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that blackness does not necessitate a particular subject; its need is only for a subject in general. In your life, do you treat temporary roles (like job, status, or relationships) as if they are necessary to your identity, even though your essence as a servant of Allah needs only Him? What would it look like to hold those roles lightly?
?
The argument distinguishes between what is essential (necessitating a subject) and what is relational (specific subject). Consider one aspect of your life you have defined by a specific context (e.g., 'I am successful only if I have this job'). How can you reframe it as an essential quality of your relationship with Allah, independent of that context, and what concrete step will you take this week to act on that reframing?

The Impossibility of Denying Accidents: A Refutation
Imam Nasafi refutes the denial of accidents with moral and legal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the reality of accidents leads to absurdities in moral and legal judgments, and how Imam Nasafi uses examples of belief, punishment, and beating to prove the necessity of accidents.

Core Idea
Denying accidents is impossible because moral and legal distinctions depend on them.

Imam Nasafi addresses the objection that accidents are unreal by showing that our most basic moral and legal concepts—obedience, punishment, slander, fornication—presuppose the existence of accidents. In this passage, he presents a series of compelling examples that force the denier into contradiction. You will see how the argument proceeds from general principle to specific cases, culminating in a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Imam Nasafi builds his refutation step by step.
2
Identify the main arguments
Find the two main examples: the moral example about belief/disbelief and obedience, and the legal example about fixed punishments (hudud). How does each example show that accidents must exist?
The moral example focuses on the distinction between essence and accident in actions.
3
Understand the beating example
The beating example shows that if beating (an accident) did not exist, then 100 lashes would not be more than 80 lashes, which is absurd. This demonstrates that accidents are real and quantifiable.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that denying accidents is akin to denying necessary truths and sense perception. How does this strengthen the overall argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا أسئلة من تأمل عرف جوابها، فأعرضنا عن ذكرها مخافة الإطالة، وهذا الإلزام ثابت في جميع الأعراض التي تتعاقب هي وأضدادها على محل واحد. ويقال للأصم: أليس أن من آمن كان مطيعا وينال الثواب، ومن كفر كان عاصيا ويستحق العقاب، وكذا عن كل طاعة ومعصية؟ أفيتعلق كل ذلك بوجود ذاته أم بوجود معنى وراء ذاته؟ فإن قال بذاته بان بهته وظهرت مكابرته، وإن قال بمعنى وراء ذاته فقد ترك مذهبه وانقاد للحق، ويقال له المأمور بالإيمان وغيره من الطاعات أمأمور به بتحصيل نفسه أم بتحصيل معنى غير نفسه؟ فإن قال بالأول ظهر عناده، وإن قال بالثاني فقد ترك مذهبهم، وكذا شتم غيره بسخطه ومدحه يرضيه والرضا والسخط لا يتعلقان بذات المادح والشاتم ولا رضاه وسخطه يرجعان إلى ذاته وعرف بهذا أن إنكار الأعراض من قبيل جحد الضروريات وإنكار المشاهدات، وكذا يقال: ما حد المفتري؟ فلابد من أن يقول: ثمانون، وفي حد الزنا لابد من أن يقول: مائة، ولابد من أن يعترف أن المائة أزيد من الثمانين بعشرين وليس الضارب بمتعدد ولا المضروب، ولا السوط الذي آلة الضرب، ولو لم يكن للضرب الذي هو عرض وجود لكان لا شيء أكثر من لا شيء بعشرين، وهذا محال لا يخفى بطلانه على المجانين فضلا عن العقلاء، وكذا هذا في أعداد ركعات الصلوات، والله الموفق.
And on this [basis] are questions; whoever reflects knows their answer, so we refrain from mentioning them for fear of prolixity. And this [necessary] implication is established in all accidents that alternate with their opposites on a single substrate. And it is said to the deaf one (al-asamm): Is it not that whoever believes is obedient and attains reward, and whoever disbelieves is disobedient and deserves punishment, and likewise for every act of obedience and disobedience? Does all that attach to the existence of His essence or to the existence of a meaning (ma'na) beyond His essence? If he says [it attaches] to the essence, his falsity becomes clear and his obstinacy appears, and if he says [it attaches] to a meaning beyond His essence, he has abandoned his school and submitted to the truth. And it is said to him: The one commanded to believe and other acts of obedience – is he commanded to acquire his own essence or to acquire a meaning other than his essence? If he says the first, his stubbornness appears, and if he says the second, he has abandoned their school. Likewise, he reviles another with his displeasure and praises him with his pleasure, while pleasure and displeasure do not attach to the essence of the praiser and reviler, nor does his pleasure and displeasure refer back to his essence. And by this it is known that the denial of accidents (a'rad) is of the category of rejecting necessities and denying perceptibles. And likewise it is said: What is the hadd (fixed punishment) of the slanderer? He must say: Eighty [lashes]. And the hadd of the fornicator: he must say: One hundred. And he must acknowledge that one hundred exceeds eighty by twenty, while the beater is not multiple nor the beaten one, nor the whip which is the instrument of beating. And if the beating which is an accident did not exist, then nothing would be more than nothing by twenty. This is an impossible absurdity whose falsity is not hidden from madmen, let alone the rational. And likewise in the numbers of rak'as of prayers. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the denial of accidents (a'rad). Imam Nasafi addresses an objector called 'al-asamm' (the deaf one) who denies the reality of accidents. The argument proceeds in several steps: First, it points out that moral states like belief and disbelief are accidents that cannot be reduced to the essence alone, for if they were, then the essence would change with every act. Second, it uses the example of legal punishments (hudud) for slander and fornication: the numbers 80 and 100 are accidents that differ. If the beating (which is an accident) had no existence, then there would be no basis for saying 100 is more than 80. Thus, the denial of accidents leads to absurdity. The passage concludes that denying accidents is tantamount to denying necessary truths and sense perception. The examples of prayer rak'as further reinforce the point.

Key Terms
الأعراض
الأصم
المعنى
الذات
حد
المفتري
الزنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents are merely conceptual and have no real existence.
Correction: The text shows that accidents are real because moral and legal distinctions (obedience, punishment, numbers) depend on them; without accidents, these distinctions collapse into absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the punishment for slander and fornication prove?
A) That numbers are eternal
B) That punishments vary by crime
C) That accidents exist
D) That the whip is essential

Key Takeaway
Denying the reality of accidents leads to logical absurdities in moral and legal reasoning, proving that accidents must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you genuinely feel that you have acquired a real accident that changes your relationship with Allah, or do you act as if your essence remains untouched? This lesson forces you to choose: either your actions matter and are real, or you are denying a necessary truth. Which will it be?
?
Consider the example of pleasure and displeasure. When you feel pleasure at someone's good deed or displeasure at their sin, do you treat those feelings as real accidents that affect your soul? Or do you dismiss them as mere illusions? How would your spiritual life change if you fully embraced the reality of these inner states?
?
The hadd punishments show that numbers and differences are real accidents. In your own life, do you treat the commands of Allah (like the number of rak'as) as real obligations that carry real weight, or do you view them as arbitrary rituals? What does your answer reveal about your belief in the reality of divine commands?

The World: Only Substances and Accidents
Imam Nasafi concludes that the world comprises only substances and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world is divided into exactly two categories: substances (self-subsistent) and accidents (non-self-subsistent).

Core Idea
Everything that exists is either self-subsistent or dependent—no third option.

Having established the reality of accidents, Imam Nasafi now delivers a sweeping classification: every entity in the universe, from the highest heaven to the smallest insect, falls into one of two categories. This binary division is not arbitrary—it follows from the very definition of existence. Let's see how he arrives at this conclusion and why there is no middle ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the comprehensive scope: 'entire world' and all examples given.
The passage is a single sentence, so read it carefully.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage mentions two fundamental kinds: 'a'yan (substances) and a'rad (accidents). Understand what each means: substances are self-subsistent, accidents are not.
The Arabic terms are given: قائم بنفسه and لا يقوم بنفسه.
3
Notice the exhaustive scope
Imam Nasafi lists many pairs (lower/upper, inanimate/growing, etc.) to show that all possible things are included. Ask yourself: can you think of anything that doesn't fit?
This reinforces the claim that the division is complete.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
The key phrase is 'لا واسطة'—there is no intermediary. Understand why logically there cannot be a third option: something either exists on its own or depends on something else.
This is a foundational axiom in kalam ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف أن العالم بأسره ما ذكرنا من الأعراض والأعيان مركبة وغير مركبة لا يوجد للعالم قسم إلا وهو داخل تحت ما ذكر سفليا كان أو علويا جمادا كان أو ناميا نباتا كان أو حيوانا عجما كان أو ناطقا إذ لا واسطة بين ما يقوم بنفسه وبين ما لا يقوم بنفسه
And when it is known that the entire world – of what we have mentioned of accidents and substances (a'yan), composite and non-composite – there is no division of the world except that it falls under what we have mentioned, whether lower or upper, whether inanimate or growing, whether plant or animal, whether non-rational or rational, for there is no intermediary between that which is self-subsistent (qa'im bi nafsihi) and that which is not self-subsistent.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a conclusive classification of all existing things. After proving the reality of accidents, Imam Nasafi states that the entire world (al-'alam) consists only of what has been mentioned: accidents (a'rad) and substances (a'yan). These two categories cover every possible entity, whether it is celestial or terrestrial, inanimate or living, plant or animal, rational or non-rational. The reason is that there is no third category between that which is self-subsistent (qa'im bi nafsihi) and that which is not self-subsistent. This is a fundamental ontological division in kalam.

Key Terms

الأعراض
الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some people think that there are more than two fundamental categories of existence (e.g., Platonic forms, independent matter, or spirits).
Correction: The text asserts that there is no intermediary (لا واسطة) between self-subsistent and non-self-subsistent, so only two categories are possible.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Everything that exists is either self-subsistent (a substance) or dependent (an accident); there is no third option.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own qualities—your intelligence, your emotions, your strength—do you see them as accidents that depend on you (a substance)? What does it mean that these things are not self-subsistent? How does that reshape your pride or self-reliance?
?
If every created thing is either a substance or an accident, and you are a substance (body and soul) composed of atoms and accidents, then you are entirely contingent. What in your life have you been treating as if it were self-sufficient? Confront that illusion.
?
The text says there is no intermediary between self-subsistent and non-self-subsistent. That means every moment you exist as a substance, you are sustained by the only truly Self-Subsistent. Can you feel that dependency? How would you live today differently if you truly believed there is no third category—no neutral ground?

Defining Qadim: Al-Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Al-Qattan
Imam Nasafi presents definitions of qadim from al-Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and al-Qattan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of qadim (eternal) proposed by al-Ash'ari, the Mu'tazila, and al-Qattan, and why their divergence matters in kalam.

Core Idea
The definition of qadim is not uniform; it shapes theological discourse.

In kalam, precise definitions are crucial. Before discussing divine attributes, one must define what 'eternal' (qadim) means. This passage presents two contrasting definitions: one from al-Ash'ari (also accepted by the Mu'tazila with a qualification) and another from 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. Understanding this divergence helps us grasp how different schools approach the nature of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two definitions: al-Ash'ari's and al-Qattan's. See how the Mu'tazila align with al-Ash'ari but with a condition.
The passage is from early kalam.
2
Compare the definitions
What is the key difference? Al-Ash'ari defines qadim as that which has no beginning; al-Qattan defines it as 'that which has qidam' (eternity). Why might one definition be preferable?
The incomplete sentence suggests further discussion.
3
Reflect on implications
Think about why the Mu'tazila accept al-Ash'ari's definition but add that Allah is not described by a meaning beyond the essence. How does this connect to their rejection of eternal attributes?
This touches on the core debate about divine attributes.

Source Text

And 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan said: The eternal is one who has eternity (qidam). And from the difference in definition between 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id [and others arises...] (sentence incomplete).

The text begins to discuss the consequences of this difference but is cut off.

Dilemma of Motion Transfer and Refutation of Manifestation and Latency
Sanusi presents a dilemma and refutes the manifestation-latency theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dilemma of motion transfer proves that accidents are created, and why the manifestation-latency theory leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
Motion cannot transfer between bodies without new creation; avoiding this leads to impossible opposites.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a powerful rational argument against those who deny that accidents (a'raad) are created. The author sets up a dilemma: if motion transfers from one body to another, either it pre-exists in the first body (impossible) or occurs anew (admitting creation). A subsequent objection proposes that rest and motion simply alternate between manifest and latent states. The author refutes this by showing that either the change involves transfer (same dilemma) or it does not, leading to a combination of opposites. Thus, the very change proves the creation of new attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: dilemma → objection → refutation.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The author presents a dilemma about motion transferring from one body to another. Find both options and their consequences.
Option 1: motion existed in the first body — impossible. Option 2: motion occurs new — admits accident creation.
3
Analyze the objection and its refutation
The opponent claims rest and motion alternate between manifest and latent. The author refutes by showing this either involves transfer (same dilemma) or leads to opposites in one locus.
Focus on the logical moves: 'If by transference...' and 'If not by transference...'.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the change from manifest to latent itself proves the creation (hadath) of accidents. This is the key point: the theory of manifestation/latency does not avoid admitting new creations.
The statement 'in this is what we intended: the establishment of accidents' highlights the purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحل، وخرجتم عن المعقول لم ينفعكم هذا، لأنا نقول لكم إذا انتقلت الحركة من محل إلى محل آخر وصارت متحركة، وكذا السكون إذا انتقل وصار متحركا كانت هذه الحركة موجودة في الحركة والسكون وقت كونهما في المكان الأول أم حدثت؟ فإن قلتم كانت موجودة، فقد ادعيتم محالا حيث جعلتم الحركة قائمة بالجسم الأول مع وجود انتقالها عنه إلى غيره والقول بجعل الشيء ثابتا في محل انتقاله عنه محال وإن قلتم حدثت تلك الحركة، فقد أقررتم بحدوث شيء من الأعراض. فإن قيل: ما أنكرتم أن السكون كان ظاهرا في الجسم فكمن فيه، والحركة كانت كامنة فظهرت فكان الجسم ساكنا غير متحرك لكون السكون ظاهرا وكون الحركة كامنة، ولما انقلب الأمر صار متحركا ولم يبق ساكنا؟ قيل: إن كان الكون والظهور بالانتقال من بعض أجزاء الجسم إلى بعض الأجزاء لزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم في السؤال الأول، وإن لم يكن بالانتقال، فإذاً محل السكون والحركة واحد فيكون فيه جمع بين الضدين، وفيه كون ما قام به السكون متحركا، وكون ما قامت به الحركة ساكنا، وهذا كله محال ممتنع. ثم نقول: ما كان ظاهرا فكمن فقد انعدم فيه الظهور وحدث فيه الكون وكذا ما كان كامنا فظهر فقد انعدم فيه الكمون وحدث الظهور، وفيه ما أردنا من إثبات الأعراض ولن ينفعكم ركوب هذه المحالات.
The place, and you have departed from what is rational, this will not benefit you. For we say to you: when motion transfers from one place to another and becomes moving, and likewise rest when it transfers and becomes moving, was this motion existent in motion and rest at the time they were in the first place, or did it occur? If you say it was existent, then you have claimed an impossibility, since you have made motion subsisting in the first body while it transfers from it to another, and the statement of making a thing fixed in the place of its transfer from it is impossible. And if you say that motion occurred, then you have admitted the occurrence of something from among the accidents. If it is said: What prevents you from [saying] that rest was manifest in the body, then it became latent in it, and motion was latent, then it became manifest, so the body was at rest not moving due to rest being manifest and motion being latent, and when the situation reversed, it became moving and did not remain at rest? It is said: If the becoming and manifestation are by transference from some parts of the body to some parts, then all that we required of you in the first question applies to you. And if it is not by transference, then the locus of rest and motion is one, so there would be in it a combination of opposites, and in it is that that which rest subsists in becomes moving, and that which motion subsists in becomes at rest, and all of this is impossible, prohibited. Then we say: That which was manifest, then became latent — its manifestation has ceased and the becoming has occurred in it; and likewise that which was latent, then became manifest — its latency has ceased and manifestation has occurred. And in this is what we intended: the establishment of accidents. And riding these impossibilities will not benefit you.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma and then refutes an objection. The author argues against those who deny the occurrence of accidents. First, he sets up a dilemma: if motion transfers from one body to another, is the motion in the first body at the time of transfer? If you say yes, that's impossible because the same motion would be in two bodies simultaneously. If you say it newly occurs, then you admit that accidents occur. Then an objection is raised: rest and motion are not separate accidents; rather, rest is manifest and motion latent, and they switch. The answer: if the change is by transference of parts, the same dilemma applies; if not, then rest and motion would have the same locus, leading to a combination of opposites, which is impossible. Finally, the author concludes that the very change from manifest to latent proves the creation of new attributes, thus establishing that accidents are created.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
محل
عرض
كمون
ظهور
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest can simply appear and disappear without being newly created; they are merely manifest or latent.
Correction: The change from manifest to latent involves the cessation of one attribute and the occurrence of another, which is exactly what is meant by the creation of accidents.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the change from manifest to latent?
A) That accidents are eternal.
B) That accidents are created.
C) That motion and rest are the same thing.
D) That manifestation and latency are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma of motion transfer and the refutation of manifestation-latency show that any change in bodily states requires the creation of new accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a change in your own body—like illness turning to health—do you instinctively attribute it to a latent potential or to a fresh act of creation by Allah? How might your awareness of His constant creative action change if you consciously affirmed that every change is a new accident?
?
Sanusi argues that the theory of manifestation-latency is an attempt to 'ride impossibilities.' Are there areas of your faith where you have rationalized away uncomfortable truths rather than embracing the straightforward evidence? What would it cost you to confront those head-on?
?
This passage shows that even the process of manifestation and latency involves the creation and cessation of accidents. If you truly believed that every moment your attributes are being created anew by Allah, how would that reshape your view of your own actions—and your sense of accountability for them?
