Why Accidents Cannot Inhere in Accidents
The author proves the impossibility of an accident subsisting in another accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that an accident cannot subsist in another accident, based on infinite regress and the nature of self-subsistence.

Core Idea
If accidents could inhere in accidents, the chain would go on forever—impossible.

This lesson examines a foundational principle in kalam: that accidents (non-substantial attributes) cannot themselves serve as loci for other accidents. The author presents a concise but powerful argument. By following his reasoning, you will see why this principle is necessary for a coherent ontology of accidents and substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the key terms: قيام العرض بالعرض (subsistence of accident in accident) and تسلسل (infinite regress).
The style is dense—read slowly.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument uses two main reasons: (1) infinite regress if allowed, and (2) what is not self-subsistent cannot be a locus. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the regress argument
If accident A could inhere in accident B, then accident B could inhere in accident C, and so on without end. Since such an infinite chain is impossible (in this context), the initial possibility is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the self-subsistence argument
An accident by definition is not self-subsistent; it needs a locus. Something that itself depends on a locus cannot serve as a locus for another. This reinforces the conclusion.
5
Apply to the example of latency and manifestation
The author gives the example of latency (كمون) and manifestation (ظهور) supposedly inhering in motion and rest. Show how the principle prevents that.
This connects the abstract proof to a concrete case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لزمكم قيام الكمون والظهور بالحركة والسكون، وقيام العرض بالعرض محال، لأنه لو جاز قيام الثاني بالأول جاز قيام الثالث بالثاني وكذا قيام الرابع بالثالث فيتسلسل إلى غير النهاية، وكذا ما لا يستقل بذاته ولا يتصور قيامه بنفسه مستغنيا عن محل يحله، يستحيل حمله لغيره.
And likewise it is necessary for you that latency and manifestation subsist in motion and rest. And the subsistence of an accident in an accident is impossible, because if the subsistence of a second [accident] in a first were possible, then the subsistence of a third in a second would be possible, and likewise the subsistence of a fourth in a third, leading to an infinite regress. And also, what is not self-subsistent and cannot be conceived as subsisting by itself independently of a locus that it inheres in, it is impossible for it to be carried by another.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage proves the impossibility of an accident inhering in another accident. The argument uses a regress: if accident A can subsist in accident B, then accident B can subsist in accident C, and so on ad infinitum. Since infinite regress is impossible for entities in ordered hierarchies, it is impossible. Additionally, any entity that is not self-subsistent (i.e., needs a locus) cannot be the locus for another entity. Therefore, attributes like latency and manifestation cannot subsist in motion and rest if motion and rest themselves are accidents; instead, motion and rest must be accidents directly inhering in bodies.

Key Terms
قيام العرض بالعرض
تسلسل
استقلال بالذات
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that accidents can serve as the loci for other accidents, e.g., whiteness inhering in motion.
Correction: The passage shows this leads to an infinite regress and violates the definition of accident as dependent on a substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'subsistence of an accident in an accident' impossible?
A) Because accidents are eternal.
B) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
C) Because motion and rest are not accidents.
D) Because the body cannot be a locus.

Key Takeaway
An accident cannot subsist in another accident because it would require an infinite regress and because an accident lacks the self-subsistence to be a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that only self-subsistent beings can carry others. In your life, where have you placed your ultimate trust in something that is itself dependent—like wealth, status, or people—rather than in the only Self-Subsistent, Allah? What would it take to shift that reliance today?
?
The argument uses infinite regress to show impossibility. Are there areas of your life—like seeking satisfaction from worldly achievements—where you are trapped in an endless chain without reaching the ultimate goal? How can this lesson help you cut the chain and turn directly to Allah?
?
If an accident cannot carry another accident, then your own existence is entirely dependent on Allah. Do you live with a constant awareness of this dependence, or do you often feel self-sufficient? What practical step can you take this week to remind yourself that you are not self-subsistent?

Distinguishing Manifest and Latent from Motion and Rest
Proof that manifest and latent are distinct from motion and rest, establishing accidentality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why manifestation and latency must be distinct attributes from motion and rest, and how this proves that motion and rest are accidents.

Core Idea
Manifestation and latency are not the essences of motion and rest; they are additional attributes.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle claim: that the qualities of being manifest or latent are identical to the essences of motion and rest. By showing this leads to contradictions, he proves they are separate and thereby establishes that motion and rest are accidents — things that undergo change. This argument strengthens the Maturidi position that all bodies and their attributes are contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
Notice the author uses a reductio ad absurdum (proof by contradiction) structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says manifestation and latency are not additional meanings beyond the essence of motion and rest. Understand what this means: they think 'manifest' and 'latent' are the same as the essence of motion/rest.
3
Follow the reductio for rest
If rest is manifest by essence, it cannot become latent (since its essence would have to change). If it is latent by essence, it must have always been latent. Both are impossible. Therefore, manifestation and latency must be distinct attributes.
The same logic is then applied to motion.
4
Connect to accidentality
The author states that this distinction proves motion and rest are accidents (they undergo change). He also notes that all genera of accidents are impermanent, hinting at a future proof.
5
Reflect on the broader argument
Consider why this argument is important: it defends the contingency of the physical world and refutes those who claim that attributes like motion and rest are eternal.
The author promises a later proof for the impossibility of accidents enduring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قلتم إن الظهور والكمون ليسا بمعنيين زائدين على ذات الحركة والسكون، ادعيتم ما هو محال لأن السكون إن كان ظاهر الذات فلا يتصور صيرورته كامنا مع الذات الذي هو علة كونه ظاهرا وكذا لو كان كامنا لذاته حين صار كامنا لكان هذا الذات قبل ذلك موجودا فكان ينبغي أن يكون حينئذ كامنا، ولأن فيه جعل الشيء واحد كامنا لذاته ظاهرا لذاته وهو محال، وكذلك هذا الإلزام في جانب الحركة. وكل دليل أقمنا على إثبات الأعراض فذلك الدليل يدل على كون الكمون والظهور معنيين زائدين على الذات وفي ثبوتهما معنيين زائدين على الذات فساد كون الحركة والسكون كامنين، وفي فساد ذلك ثبوت حدوث الحركة بالحس وثبوت حدوث السكون بالاستدلال، والله الموفق. ولأن جميع أجناس الأعراض مستحيلة البقاء ودعوى قدم ما لا يتصور بقاؤه محال، ونقيم الدلالة على استحالة بقائها إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الاستطاعة إن شاء الله تعالى.
And if you say that manifestation and latency are not additional meanings beyond the essence of motion and rest, you have claimed what is impossible. For if rest is manifest by essence, then its becoming latent is inconceivable while retaining the essence that is the cause of its being manifest. Likewise, if it were latent by essence when it became latent, that essence would have existed before, so it should have been latent at that time. And because in it is the making of one thing both latent by essence and manifest by essence — which is impossible. And likewise this requirement applies on the side of motion. And every proof we have established for the occurrence of accidents indicates that latency and manifestation are additional meanings beyond the essence. And in their being established as additional meanings beyond the essence is the falsity of motion and rest being latent. And in the falsity of that is the establishment of the occurrence of motion by sense-perception and the establishment of the occurrence of rest by inference. And God is the Grantor of success. And because all genera of accidents are impossible to endure, and the claim of eternality for that which is not conceivable to endure is impossible. And we will establish the proof of the impossibility of their endurance when we reach the question of capacity (al-istita'a) if God wills.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that manifestation and latency are identical with the essence of motion and rest. It demonstrates that if rest is essentially manifest, it cannot become latent; likewise, if it becomes latent, it must have been latent before. Therefore, manifestation and latency must be distinct attributes. This reinforces that motion and rest are accidents because they undergo change. Furthermore, the author notes that all accidents are impermanent, and the proof for that will come later.

Key Terms
ذات
ظاهر الذات
كامن الذات
حدوث
حس
استدلال
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that attributes like 'manifest' and 'latent' are identical to the essence of motion or rest, and that change in these states is merely the essence itself changing.
Correction: The author shows that if they were identical, the essence would have to change from manifest to latent or vice versa, which is impossible because an essence cannot become something else. Therefore, manifestation and latency must be distinct attributes that inhere in the essence, indicating that motion and rest are accidents.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the impossibility of rest being both manifest and latent by essence?
A) That rest and motion are eternal.
B) That manifestation and latency are additional meanings.
C) That rest is not an accident.
D) That motion is not perceptible.

Key Takeaway
Manifestation and latency are distinct attributes from motion and rest, proving that motion and rest are accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe your own habits—like rest turning into movement—do you assume that change is merely a surface shift, or do you recognize it as evidence of a deeper dependence on the Creator? Have you ever reflected that each state (rest or motion) requires a fresh act of God's creation?
?
If manifestation and latency are additional attributes bestowed by Allah, then what does that say about your own moments of 'being hidden' (like thoughts) and 'being apparent' (like actions)? Are you accountable for both? How does this lesson push you to see your entire existence as constantly sustained by His power?
?
The author argues that if attributes were essential, change would be impossible. Consider a sin you struggle with: you may think it is 'part of your nature' (essential), but this lesson teaches that attributes are not fixed. How does this truth challenge you to seek transformation, knowing that your state can change because it is accidental and not your unchanging essence?

Distinguishing Essential and Non-Essential Freedom from Accidents
Al-Nasafi distinguishes two ways to be free from an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible reasons why something can be free from an accident, as introduced by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Freedom from an accident is either by essence or by a cause.

Imam al-Nasafi begins an argument about how substances can be free from accidents. He first distinguishes two possibilities: the entity is intrinsically incapable of receiving that accident, or it is free due to some external reason. This passage introduces the first possibility with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the passage is cut off mid-sentence.
The passage is incomplete; we only see the first disjunct.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage says an entity is free from an accident either by its own essence or by another cause. Find where it states this distinction.
3
Understand the example
The example given is motion and blackness. Why is motion free from blackness? Because motion is an accident and cannot accept another accident. This illustrates freedom by essence.
This example shows that accidents cannot subsist in other accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل عليه أن ما يخلو عن عرض من الأعراض إما أن كان خاليا عنه لذاته كالحركة تخلو عن السواد، والسواد عن الحركة، وكذا كل عرض، إذ يستحيل قبول عرض عرضا آخر يقوم به فكان متعريا عنه لذاته، وإما أن كان
And the proof for that is that whatever is free from an accident among accidents is either free from it by its own essence, like motion is free from blackness, and blackness from motion, and likewise every accident, for it is impossible for an accident to accept another accident subsisting in it, so it is divested of it by its own essence; or it is free from it...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage begins a proof. The text is cut off, but it establishes an important distinction: an entity can be free from a particular accident for two possible reasons: either because it is intrinsically incapable of receiving that accident (like an accident cannot bear another accident), or for some other reason (which is not completed in the available text). The examples given are that motion is naturally free from blackness (color) because motion as an accident cannot be colored; similarly, blackness cannot move. This leads to the conclusion that if a substance is free from all accidents, it must be because it is intrinsically incapable of receiving them, or because of some external factor. The incomplete nature suggests the argument likely concludes that substances are not intrinsically incapable of receiving accidents, so their freedom from all accidents would have to be due to another cause, which leads to a contradiction. However, as this is incomplete, further explanation is not possible.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
ذات (essence)
حركة (motion)
سواد (blackness)
يقوم به (subsisting in it)
يتعري عنه (divested of it)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an accident can subsist in another accident, like blackness subsisting in motion.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is impossible for an accident to accept another accident subsisting in it; thus motion cannot be black, and blackness cannot move.

Checkpoint
According to the beginning of this proof, why is motion free from blackness?
A) Because motion is too fast to be black.
B) Because motion is an accident and cannot accept another accident.
C) Because blackness cannot be in space.
D) Because motion is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi establishes that freedom from an accident can be by the entity's own essence, as in accidents being intrinsically unable to receive other accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to be free from a sin or weakness, do you attribute that freedom to your own essence (as if you are intrinsically incapable) or to an external cause (e.g., Allah's protection)? How does this distinction change the way you view your own nature?
?
Think of a habit you believe you are free from. Are you free from it by your own essence (i.e., you are simply not that kind of person) or by constant effort and Allah's help? How does this lesson's distinction challenge your pride in your own character?
?
The passage uses the example of motion being free from blackness. In your own life, what is something you think you are 'essentially' free from? Is that belief justified, or might it be due to circumstance? How can you know the difference?

The Error of Using Imagination for the Unseen
The author distinguishes rational from imagined and criticizes reliance on imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why imagination (wahm) cannot be used to judge matters beyond sensory experience, and how the rational differs from the imagined.

Core Idea
Imagination is a product of sensation; it cannot reach the unseen.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla addresses a common epistemological error: equating the rational with the imagined. The author defines imagination as a sensory imprint and argues that using it to know the unseen leads to absurdity, like trying to hear colors. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of imagination
Read how the author defines imagination (wahm) as a product of sensation—an imprint of a sensible image after the object leaves the senses. This anchors his critique.
Imagination is not independent of the senses.
2
Identify the faulty analogy
Notice the author's comparison: using imagination to know the unseen is like using ears to distinguish colors. This shows the category error.
Each sense has its proper domain.
3
Trace the root cause of the error
The author explains that people err because they have never observed creation ex nihilo, so they cannot imagine it and thus deny its rationality.
They confuse absence of imagination with irrationality.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why this distinction matters for theology: Allah and the unseen are rational realities beyond sensory imagination.
Rationality extends beyond what the mind can picture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحمد الله تعالى، فكان معقولا غير أنه ليس بموهوم لأن الوهم من انتاج الحس إذ هو انطباع صورة المحسوس في المفكرة بعد زواله عن الحس فمن أراد أن يعرف [?] ما غاب عن الحس بما هو من نتائج الحس وهو الوهم فقد أراد معرفة الشيء بغير ما وضع لمعرفته من أسباب المعارف، فصار كمن أراد أن يميز بين الألوان بسمعه وبين الأصوات ببصره ويعرف طعم الأجسام بيده وهذا جهل مفرط، وهذا لأنه لم ير إيجاد الأجسام ممن له قدرة إيجادها ومن رأى من الفاعلين لم يكن لهم قدرة إيجادها فلم يتصور ذلك في وهمه لبعده عن حسه، فظن أن ما ليس بموهوم ليس بمعقولا جهلا منه بالفرق بين المحسوس والمعقول، على أنه رأى حدوث الصنعة في المادة لا عن أصل، إذ الصنعة توجد في المحل أما الصنعة فلا تحدث عن صنعة أخرى تكون مادة للأولى، وكذا الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق فإذا عرف هو وجود شيء لا من شيء فبطل كلامه والله الهادي للعباد إلى سبيل الرشاد.
Praise be to God Almighty, so it is rational but not imagined, because imagination is a product of sensation, as it is the imprint of the sensible image in the memory after its departure from the senses. So whoever wants to know [?] what is absent from the senses by means of what is a product of sensation, namely imagination, has desired to know a thing by other than the means established for its knowledge among the causes of knowledge. Thus he becomes like one who wants to distinguish colors with his hearing, sounds with his sight, and know the taste of bodies with his hand—this is extreme ignorance. This is because he has not seen the creation of bodies from one who has the power to create them, and among the agents he has seen, none had the power to create them, so that was not imagined in his imagination due to its remoteness from his senses. So he thought that what is not imagined is not rational, out of ignorance of the difference between the sensible and the rational. Moreover, he saw the origination of craftsmanship in matter without an origin, for craftsmanship occurs in the subject, but craftsmanship does not arise from another craftsmanship that would be matter for the first. Similarly for motion, rest, conjunction, and separation. So if he knows the existence of a thing from nothing, his argument is refuted. And God is the Guide of servants to the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an argument against those who mistakenly equate the rational (ma'qul) with the imagined (mawhum) and use imagination (wahm) to judge matters beyond sensory experience. The author begins by stating that something can be rational without being imagined. He then gives the definition of imagination: it is a product of sensation (intaj al-hiss), specifically the imprint of a sensible image in the memory after the object departs from the senses. Therefore, imagination is inherently tied to sensory experience. Whoever tries to know what is absent from the senses (al-gha'ib) using imagination is misusing the proper means of knowledge, like trying to distinguish colors with hearing or sounds with sight—an extreme folly. The author diagnoses the psychological root of this error: these people have never observed the creation of bodies ex nihilo (from nothing) by an agent capable of it, because all agents they see lack such power. Thus, they cannot imagine it and falsely conclude that what is unimaginable is also irrational. They fail to distinguish the sensible (mahsus) from the rational (ma'qul). The author further argues that they have observed craftsmanship (sina'a) arising in matter without an origin from prior craftsmanship—like motion and rest occurring in a subject without being generated from a previous similar accident. So if they can conceive of something coming to be from nothing in these cases, their objection to creation ex nihilo is baseless.

Key Terms
الوهم (wahm)
المعقول (ma'qul)
المحسوس (mahsus)
الحس (hiss)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whatever cannot be imagined is not rational.
Correction: Rationality (ma'qul) extends beyond sensory imagination; something can be rationally necessary without being picturable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the origin of imagination (wahm)?
A) Reason
B) Sensation
C) Divine revelation
D) Intuition

Key Takeaway
Imagination is tied to the senses, so it cannot judge matters of the unseen; rationality is broader than imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you have rejected simply because you could not picture it, even though reason accepts it? Will you now reconsider that belief in light of this lesson?
?
The author says that using imagination for the unseen is like using your ear for colors. In what areas of your life—faith, doubts, or daily decisions—are you making this same mistake?
?
If you truly accept that rationality extends beyond imagination, what unseen reality (like the attributes of Allah or the afterlife) will you now take more seriously, and what will you do differently starting today?

Refutation of Defining Jawhar as Qa'im bi al-Dhat
Refutes the definition of jawhar as 'self-subsisting' and offers an alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of jawhar as 'self-subsisting' is rejected and how alternative definitions based on composition and receptivity to accidents are argued.

Core Idea
Jawhar is not self-subsistence; it means a particle that composes bodies.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla continues the debate over the term jawhar. The opponents define jawhar as 'self-subsisting' (qa'im bi al-dhat) and argue that since God is self-subsisting, He can be called jawhar. Al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that the definition is arbitrary and that other definitions, such as 'origin of bodies' or 'receptive to accidents', are equally valid. The passage uses logical tools like coextension (ittirād) and co-exclusion (in'ikās) to challenge the opponents' claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' claim
The opponents claim that jawhar is defined as 'self-subsisting' and that this definition is coextensive and co-exclusive in the witnessed world. This would allow them to apply the term to God.
2
Notice the symmetry argument
Al-Nasafi points out that jawhar-ness also revolves with being an origin for bodies, so the opponents cannot privilege their definition.
This uses the same logical criteria they rely on.
3
Examine the reception-of-accidents argument
In the witnessed world, every jawhar is receptive to accidents. If this is taken as definition, then God—not receptive to accidents—cannot be jawhar, which contradicts the opponents' aim.
This creates a dilemma for the opponents.
4
Appreciate the linguistic argument
The word 'jawhar' itself suggests 'origin' (asl) rather than 'self-subsistence', making the alternative definition more appropriate.
5
Draw the conclusion
The passage shows that defining jawhar as self-subsisting is not logically necessary and leads to contradictions, so it cannot be applied to Allah.
This safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ادعوا أن الجوهر اسم للقائم بالذات دعوى ممنوعة فأما ما ادعوا من صحة الاطراد والانعكاس في الشاهد فقلنا لهم كما تدور الجوهرية مع القيام بالذات طردا وعكسا ووجودا وعدما فكذا تدور مع كونه أصلا يتركب منه الأجسام فلم كان جعلكم القائم بالذات حدا له أولى من جعل خصومكم كونه أصلا حدا له وأنتم المدعون حيث تريدون تعدية الاسم إلى الغائب بتعدي الوصف بأنه القائم بالذات وخصومكم يمنعونكم عن ذلك. ثم يقال لهم أليس أن في الشاهد كل جوهر قابل للعرض وكل ما هو قابل للعرض جوهر فلابد من أن يقولوا بلى قيل أتجعلون كونه قابلا للعرض حدا له لدورانه معه وجودا وعدما وطردا وعكسا فإن قالوا نعم تركوا أصلهم فإنهم يقولون بأنه تعالى ليس بقابل للأعراض وإن قالوا لا أبطلوا دليلهم ثم يقال لهم ما تنكرون على من يقول إن الله تعالى ليس بجوهر لأن حد الجوهر أنه قابل للأعراض ويستدل على صحة حده بالطرد والعكس والدوران وجودا وعدما فإن قبلوا ذلك تركوا مذهبهم وإن لم يقبلوا أبطلوا دليلهم ويقال لهم لما كانت الجوهرية تدور مع القيام بالذات وجودا وعدما وكذا كونه أصلا للأجسام ثم ليس من لفظ الجوهر ما ينبئ عن القيام بالذات وفيه ما ينبئ عن كونه أصلا كان جعله جوهرا لكونه أصلا أولى من جعله جوهرا لكونه قائما بالذات إذ إطلاق اسم معنوي على.
And their claim that 'jawhar' is a name for the self-subsisting (al-qa'im bi al-dhat) is an objectionable claim. As for their claim of the validity of coextensiveness and co-exclusiveness in the witnessed world, we said to them: just as jawhar-ness revolves with self-subsistence in coextension and co-exclusion, in existence and non-existence, so too does it revolve with being an origin from which bodies are composed. So why is your making self-subsistence its definition more appropriate than your opponents making its being an origin its definition? And you are the claimants when you wish to extend the name to the absent by extending the description that it is self-subsisting, and your opponents prevent you from that. Then it is said to them: Is it not the case that in the witnessed world every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and everything receptive to accidents is a jawhar? They must say yes. Say: Do you make its being receptive to accidents a definition for it, due to its revolving with it in existence and non-existence, coextension and co-exclusion? If they say yes, they abandon their principle, for they say that He the Exalted is not receptive to accidents. And if they say no, they invalidate their evidence. Then it is said to them: What do you deny from one who says that God the Exalted is not a jawhar, because the definition of jawhar is that it is receptive to accidents, and he argues for the soundness of his definition by coextension, co-exclusion, and revolving in existence and non-existence? If they accept that, they abandon their doctrine; and if they do not accept, they invalidate their evidence. And it is said to them: Since jawhar-ness revolves with self-subsistence in existence and non-existence, and likewise with being an origin for bodies, and then the word 'jawhar' does not indicate self-subsistence, while it does indicate being an origin, making it a jawhar for being an origin is more appropriate than making it a jawhar for being self-subsisting, because applying a meaning-based name ...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage continues the rebuttal by critiquing the definition of jawhar as 'self-subsisting' (al-qa'im bi al-dhat). The author argues that this definition is arbitrary. The opponents claim that the definition holds in the witnessed world because jawhar-ness and self-subsistence are coextensive and co-exclusive. The author counters that the same can be said for the definition of jawhar as 'origin from which bodies are composed', so the opponents cannot claim priority. Moreover, the author uses a dialectical argument: in the witnessed world, every jawhar is receptive to accidents. If the opponents accept that receptivity to accidents is a defining characteristic, then since God is not receptive to accidents, He cannot be a jawhar, undermining their doctrine. If they reject this definition, their own argument based on coextensiveness is refuted. Finally, the author points out that the word 'jawhar' linguistically suggests 'origin' (asl) rather than 'self-subsistence', so it is more appropriate to define it that way.

Key Terms
قائم بالذات (qa'im bi al-dhat)
اطراد (coextension)
انعكاس (co-exclusion)
دوران (revolving)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term jawhar is a positive attribute of God and can be applied to describe His essence.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that jawhar is a term for created substances defined by self-subsistence, composition, or receptivity to accidents—none of which apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what problem arises if the opponents define jawhar as 'receptive to accidents'?
A) It would mean God is a jawhar because He is receptive to accidents.
B) It would mean God is not a jawhar because He is not receptive to accidents.
C) It would mean the definition is not coextensive.
D) It would mean the definition is linguistic.

Key Takeaway
The term jawhar refers to created substances and cannot be applied to Allah, as it implies composition or receptivity impermissible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah, do you ever use terms that are inherently tied to created things, even if you mean them positively? This passage challenges you to examine your own words—are you willing to abandon a comfortable but imprecise description of Allah for the sake of tanzih?
?
The opponents insisted on their definition despite clear logical flaws. Where in your own beliefs or practices do you cling to a position because it's familiar, even when evidence against it is clear? What would it cost you to let go?
?
If the term 'jawhar' cannot be applied to Allah because it implies receptivity to accidents, what other concepts might you unknowingly attribute to Allah that carry hidden implications? Are you ready to submit your understanding to the precise language of revelation and reason, even if it means unlearning something you held dear?

The Rotation Argument
Nasafi uses the method of tarad wa 'aks to challenge the definition of jawhar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that co-extensiveness alone cannot define jawhar because it rotates equally with two different meanings.

Core Idea
Co-extensiveness alone cannot settle the definition of jawhar.

Nasafi begins his refutation by challenging the Mu'tazilite claim that jawhar is defined as self-subsistent. He points out that if we use their own method of co-extensiveness, jawhariyya rotates with both self-subsistence and being an origin, so neither definition is inherently superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how Nasafi introduces the issue and rejects the Mu'tazilite claim as forbidden.
Pay attention to the phrase 'دعوى ممنوعة'.
2
Identify the two rotating meanings
Find where Nasafi says that jawhariyya rotates with both 'self-subsistence' and 'being an origin'. Understand that rotation means co-extensiveness.
The terms are 'قيام بالذات' and 'كونه أصلا'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument undermines the Mu'tazilite definition—they cannot privilege one meaning without additional justification.
Think about how this relates to the transfer of the term to God.

بأنه القائم بالذات وخصومكم يمنعونكم عن ذلك. ثم يقال لهم: أليس أن في الشاهد كل جوهر قابل للعرض، وكل ما هو قابل للعرض جوهر؟ فلابد من أن يقولوا: بلى. قيل: أتجعلون كونه قابلا للعرض حدا له، لدورانه معه وجودا وعدما، وطردا وعكسا؟ فإن قالوا نعم تركوا أصلهم، فإنهم يقولون بأنه تعالى ليس بقابل للأعراض. وإن قالوا: لا، أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم يقال لهم: ما تنكرون على من يقول: إن الله تعالى ليس بجوهر، لأن حد الجوهر أنه قابل للأعراض؟ ويستدل على صحة حده بالطرد والعكس والدوران وجودا وعدما، فإن قبلوا ذلك تركوا مذهبهم وإن لم يقبلوا أبطلوا دليلهم. ويقال لهم: لما كانت الجوهرية القيام بالذات وجودا وعدما، وكذا كونه أصلا يكون إلا لذلك المعنى، ومن ادعى إطلاقه عليه لا لذلك المعنى بل لأمر آخر كان مخطئا في دعواه، كمن ادعى أن ما قامت به الحركة لم يسم متحركا لقيام الحركة به بل لوجوده أو لقيامه بالذات كان مخطئا في دعواه، وكذا في الأسود والأبيض والمجتمع والمتفرق وغير ذلك من الصفات فكذا هذا. وجاء من هذا أن كل معنى تعلق بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ويدور معه وجودا وعدما لا يوجب تعلقه به تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولا يوجب تعدي أحدهما إلى الغائب تعدية الآخر على الإطلاق، بل بشريطة استحالة إضافته إلى معنى آخر، فأما ما لم يشتمل إضافته إلى معنى آخر فلم يعلم أن تعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولهذا لم يجعل قولنا القابل للأعراض حدا للجوهر على ما قررنا، وإن وجد الدوران، لأنه يمكن أن يضاف كونه جوهرا إلى معنى آخر وراء كونه قابلا للأعراض، ويجعل تعلق قيام الحركة بمحل لاتصاف المحل بكونه متحركا تعلق العلة بالمعلول، لما أن إضافة كونه متحركا يستحيل إلى معنى آخر سوى قيام الحركة به، وفيما نحن فيه لا يستحيل إضافة كونه جوهرا على معنى سوى القيام بالذات وهو كونه أصلا بل وجبت الإضافة إليه لما مر من الدلالة اللغوية على ذلك فلم يجز إضافته إلى كونه قائما بالذات، وهذا الجواب مما لا يمكن التعويل عليه على أصول المعتزلة، لأنهم يجعلون حد العرض مما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه وإن لم يكن في لفظة العرض ما يدل عليه، ولم يجعلوا حده ما يستحيل From it [the body], it is not a jawhar. And their claim that 'jawhar' is a name for the self-subsistent, and that its being self-subsistent is a definition of it, is a forbidden claim. As for their claim of the validity of co-extensiveness and converse in the witnessed realm, we said to them: just as jawhariyya (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence in co-extensiveness and converse, existence and non-existence, so too does it rotate with its being an origin from which bodies are composed. So why is your making 'self-subsistent' its definition more appropriate than your opponents making 'its being an origin' its definition? And you are the claimants, when you want to transfer the name to the unseen by transferring the description that He is self-subsistent, while your opponents prevent you from that. Then it is said to them: Is it not the case that in the witnessed realm every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and everything receptive to accidents is a jawhar? They must say: Yes. It is said: Do you make its being receptive to accidents a definition of it, due to its rotation with it in existence and non-existence, and co-extensiveness and converse? If they say yes, they abandon their principle, for they say that He, exalted, is not receptive to accidents. If they say no, they invalidate their proof. Then it is said to them: What do you deny of one who says: God, exalted, is not a jawhar, because the definition of jawhar is that it is receptive to accidents, and he proves the validity of his definition by co-extensiveness, converse, and rotation in existence and non-existence? For if they accept that, they abandon their school, and if they do not accept, they invalidate their proof. And it is said to them: Since jawhariyya (substantiality) is self-subsistence in existence and non-existence, and likewise its being an origin for bodies, yet there is nothing in the word 'jawhar' that indicates self-subsistence, while there is in it what indicates its being an origin, then making it a jawhar because it is an origin is more appropriate than making it a jawhar because it is self-subsistent; for the application of a meaningful name to a locus in which that meaning is found can only be for that meaning. And whoever claims its application to it not for that meaning but for another matter is mistaken in his claim, like one who claims that that to which motion attaches is not called 'moving' because of the attachment of motion to it, but because of its existence or its being self-subsistent, he would be mistaken in his claim. Similarly for black, white, gathered, separated, and other attributes. So too this. And from this it follows that every meaning that attaches to a thing with an inseparable attachment, and rotates with it in existence and non-existence, does not necessitate that its attachment to it is the attachment of cause to effect, nor does it necessitate transferring one of them to the unseen with the absolute transfer of the other; rather, it is conditioned upon the impossibility of its attribution to another meaning. As for that which does not preclude its attribution to another meaning, it is not known that the attachment of one to the other is the attachment of cause to effect. For this reason, we did not make our statement 'receptive to accidents' a definition of jawhar, as we established, even if rotation is found, because its being a jawhar can be attributed to another meaning beyond its being receptive to accidents. And the attachment of the occurrence of motion to a locus, due to the locus being described as 'moving', is made the attachment of cause to effect, because the attribution of its being moving to another meaning other than the attachment of motion to it is impossible. In our case, it is not impossible to attribute its being a jawhar to a meaning other than self-subsistence, namely its being an origin; rather, attribution to it is obligatory due to the linguistic indication that passed, so attribution to its being self-subsistent is not permissible. And this answer cannot be relied upon according to the principles of the Mu'tazilites, because they make the definition of accident that which is impossible to subsist by itself, even if there is nothing in the word 'accident' that indicates it, and they do not make its definition that which is impossible to endure, even if the word 'accident' linguistically indicates it. Similarly, the Christians do thus in the definition of...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition of 'jawhar' (substance/atom) as 'qā'im bi-dhātih' (self-subsistent). Nasafi argues that the correct definition is 'aṣl yutarkabu minhu al-ajsām' (an origin from which bodies are composed). He begins by noting that if a body is composed of jawhar, then the body itself is not a jawhar. Then he addresses the Mu'tazilite claim that 'jawhar' denotes self-subsistence and that this definition is valid through co-extensiveness (ṭard wa 'aks). Nasafi counters: if 'jawhariyya' (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence, it also rotates with being an origin for bodies. The Mu'tazilites cannot privilege one over the other without justification. He then uses a second argument: in the witnessed world, every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and vice versa. If the Mu'tazilites accept this as a definition, they contradict their own principle that God is not receptive to accidents. If they reject it, they undermine their own method of using co-extensiveness. Next, he presents a third argument based on linguistic indication: the word 'jawhar' itself suggests 'aṣl' (origin) and not 'qā'im bi-dhātih'. Therefore, if one applies the name to God, it must be for the meaning of 'aṣl', not self-subsistence. He supports this with an analogy: a thing is called 'moving' only because motion attaches to it, not because of its existence or self-subsistence. Similarly, attributes like black, white, etc., are named for their specific meanings. He then generalizes: a meaning that is inseparable from a thing may not be causally related; transfer to the unseen requires that the meaning cannot be attributed to anything else. In the case of jawhar, since it can be attributed to being an origin in addition to self-subsistence, the definition as self-subsistent fails. He concludes that this argument is valid against the Mu'tazilites because they themselves define 'accident' by a logical condition rather than linguistic indication, but here they are forced to accept the linguistic evidence.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
قائم بالذات (qā'im bi-dhātih)
أصل (aṣl)
طرد وعكس (ṭard wa 'aks)
دوران (dawarān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a property is co-extensive with a term, it must be its definition.
Correction: Co-extensiveness alone is insufficient; both self-subsistence and being an origin are co-extensive with jawhar, so one cannot be chosen over the other without further proof.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi use to challenge the Mu'tazilite definition of jawhar?
A) Linguistic indication
B) Co-extensiveness (ṭard wa 'aks)
C) Receptivity to accidents
D) The impossibility of defining God

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's first move is to show that co-extensiveness does not favor self-subsistence over being an origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own theological reasoning, do you cling to definitions that seem obvious to you without realizing that others might offer equally plausible alternatives? How does Nasafi's challenge here unsettle that comfort?
?
If you were a Mu'tazilite defender in this debate, what would it cost you to admit that co-extensiveness is not enough? Are you willing to let go of a cherished argument for the sake of truth?
?
When you say 'God is self-subsistent,' do you truly mean it as a description of His uniqueness, or have you unconsciously applied a human category (like jawhar) to Him? What would change in your worship if you stopped doing that?

The Argument from Receptivity to Accidents
Nasafi presents a dilemma regarding the definition of jawhar as receptive to accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the co-extensiveness of jawhar and receptivity to accidents to force the Mu'tazilites into a dilemma.

Core Idea
If being receptive to accidents defines jawhar, then God cannot be a jawhar.

Nasafi now presents a dilemma for the Mu'tazilites using the observable property that every jawhar in the witnessed world is receptive to accidents. He asks them whether this can serve as a definition, and shows that both answers contradict their own principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialogue
Read the Arabic passage that poses a series of questions to the Mu'tazilites. Notice the logical structure.
The passage uses 'قيل لهم' (it is said to them).
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two options presented: if they accept receptivity to accidents as a definition, they must say God is not jawhar; if they reject it, they invalidate their own proof method.
Look for 'فإن قالوا نعم... وإن قالوا لا'.
3
Understand the extension
Nasafi then presents a hypothetical opponent who uses the same method to conclude that God is not a jawhar. The Mu'tazilites are trapped.
Consider how this argument strengthens Nasafi's position.

وكذا في الأسود والأبيض والمجتمع والمتفرق وغير ذلك من الصفات فكذا هذا. وجاء من هذا أن كل معنى تعلق بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ويدور معه وجودا وعدما لا يوجب تعلقه به تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولا يوجب تعدي أحدهما إلى الغائب تعدية الآخر على الإطلاق، بل بشريطة استحالة إضافته إلى معنى آخر، فأما ما لم يشتمل إضافته إلى معنى آخر فلم يعلم أن تعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولهذا لم يجعل قولنا القابل للأعراض حدا للجوهر على ما قررنا، وإن وجد الدوران، لأنه يمكن أن يضاف كونه جوهرا إلى معنى آخر وراء كونه قابلا للأعراض، ويجعل تعلق قيام الحركة بمحل لاتصاف المحل بكونه متحركا تعلق العلة بالمعلول، لما أن إضافة كونه متحركا يستحيل إلى معنى آخر سوى قيام الحركة به، وفيما نحن فيه لا يستحيل إضافة كونه جوهرا على معنى سوى القيام بالذات وهو كونه أصلا بل وجبت الإضافة إليه لما مر من الدلالة اللغوية على ذلك فلم يجز إضافته إلى كونه قائما بالذات، وهذا الجواب مما لا يمكن التعويل عليه على أصول المعتزلة، لأنهم يجعلون حد العرض مما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه وإن لم يكن في لفظة العرض ما يدل عليه، ولم يجعلوا حده ما يستحيل الدوام عليه وإن كانت لفظة العرض من حيث اللغة منبئا عنه. وكذا النصارى يجعلون هكذا في حد
From it [the body], it is not a jawhar. And their claim that 'jawhar' is a name for the self-subsistent, and that its being self-subsistent is a definition of it, is a forbidden claim. As for their claim of the validity of co-extensiveness and converse in the witnessed realm, we said to them: just as jawhariyya (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence in co-extensiveness and converse, existence and non-existence, so too does it rotate with its being an origin from which bodies are composed. So why is your making 'self-subsistent' its definition more appropriate than your opponents making 'its being an origin' its definition? And you are the claimants, when you want to transfer the name to the unseen by transferring the description that He is self-subsistent, while your opponents prevent you from that. Then it is said to them: Is it not the case that in the witnessed realm every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and everything receptive to accidents is a jawhar? They must say: Yes. It is said: Do you make its being receptive to accidents a definition of it, due to its rotation with it in existence and non-existence, and co-extensiveness and converse? If they say yes, they abandon their principle, for they say that He, exalted, is not receptive to accidents. If they say no, they invalidate their proof. Then it is said to them: What do you deny of one who says: God, exalted, is not a jawhar, because the definition of jawhar is that it is receptive to accidents, and he proves the validity of his definition by co-extensiveness, converse, and rotation in existence and non-existence? For if they accept that, they abandon their school, and if they do not accept, they invalidate their proof. And it is said to them: Since jawhariyya (substantiality) is self-subsistence in existence and non-existence, and likewise its being an origin for bodies, yet there is nothing in the word 'jawhar' that indicates self-subsistence, while there is in it what indicates its being an origin, then making it a jawhar because it is an origin is more appropriate than making it a jawhar because it is self-subsistent; for the application of a meaningful name to a locus in which that meaning is found can only be for that meaning. And whoever claims its application to it not for that meaning but for another matter is mistaken in his claim, like one who claims that that to which motion attaches is not called 'moving' because of the attachment of motion to it, but because of its existence or its being self-subsistent, he would be mistaken in his claim. Similarly for black, white, gathered, separated, and other attributes. So too this. And from this it follows that every meaning that attaches to a thing with an inseparable attachment, and rotates with it in existence and non-existence, does not necessitate that its attachment to it is the attachment of cause to effect, nor does it necessitate transferring one of them to the unseen with the absolute transfer of the other; rather, it is conditioned upon the impossibility of its attribution to another meaning. As for that which does not preclude its attribution to another meaning, it is not known that the attachment of one to the other is the attachment of cause to effect. For this reason, we did not make our statement 'receptive to accidents' a definition of jawhar, as we established, even if rotation is found, because its being a jawhar can be attributed to another meaning beyond its being receptive to accidents. And the attachment of the occurrence of motion to a locus, due to the locus being described as 'moving', is made the attachment of cause to effect, because the attribution of its being moving to another meaning other than the attachment of motion to it is impossible. In our case, it is not impossible to attribute its being a jawhar to a meaning other than self-subsistence, namely its being an origin; rather, attribution to it is obligatory due to the linguistic indication that passed, so attribution to its being self-subsistent is not permissible. And this answer cannot be relied upon according to the principles of the Mu'tazilites, because they make the definition of accident that which is impossible to subsist by itself, even if there is nothing in the word 'accident' that indicates it, and they do not make its definition that which is impossible to endure, even if the word 'accident' linguistically indicates it. Similarly, the Christians do thus in the definition of...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition of 'jawhar' (substance/atom) as 'qā'im bi-dhātih' (self-subsistent). Nasafi argues that the correct definition is 'aṣl yutarkabu minhu al-ajsām' (an origin from which bodies are composed). He begins by noting that if a body is composed of jawhar, then the body itself is not a jawhar. Then he addresses the Mu'tazilite claim that 'jawhar' denotes self-subsistence and that this definition is valid through co-extensiveness (ṭard wa 'aks). Nasafi counters: if 'jawhariyya' (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence, it also rotates with being an origin for bodies. The Mu'tazilites cannot privilege one over the other without justification. He then uses a second argument: in the witnessed world, every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and vice versa. If the Mu'tazilites accept this as a definition, they contradict their own principle that God is not receptive to accidents. If they reject it, they undermine their own method of using co-extensiveness. Next, he presents a third argument based on linguistic indication: the word 'jawhar' itself suggests 'aṣl' (origin) and not 'qā'im bi-dhātih'. Therefore, if one applies the name to God, it must be for the meaning of 'aṣl', not self-subsistence. He supports this with an analogy: a thing is called 'moving' only because motion attaches to it, not because of its existence or self-subsistence. Similarly, attributes like black, white, etc., are named for their specific meanings. He then generalizes: a meaning that is inseparable from a thing may not be causally related; transfer to the unseen requires that the meaning cannot be attributed to anything else. In the case of jawhar, since it can be attributed to being an origin in addition to self-subsistence, the definition as self-subsistent fails. He concludes that this argument is valid against the Mu'tazilites because they themselves define 'accident' by a logical condition rather than linguistic indication, but here they are forced to accept the linguistic evidence.

Key Terms
عرض ('araḍ)
قابل للعرض (qābil li-l-'araḍ)

?
Nasafi forces the Mu'tazilites to face a contradiction between their method and their theology. Where in your own life do you hold inconsistent beliefs? What would it cost you to bring them into alignment?
?
The dilemma asks whether you will accept the logical conclusion of a principle even if it contradicts a deeply held position. Is there a belief you cling to that you have never tested against its own logical consequences? Will you test it today?
?
If you were in the Mu'tazilites' place, would you abandon your method or your theology? Which is more precious to you—the way you reason or the conclusions you hold? Why?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
6 min
The Linguistic Indication Argument
Nasafi argues from linguistic indication that jawhar means 'origin'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the lexical meaning of 'jawhar' to argue that it means 'origin' rather than 'self-subsistent'.

Core Idea
The word 'jawhar' itself points to 'origin', not 'self-subsistent'.

Nasafi now turns to linguistic analysis. He argues that the word 'jawhar' contains an indication (dalāla) of being an origin, whereas there is no such indication for self-subsistence. This linguistic fact should determine the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic claim
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the statement 'ليس من لفظ الجوهر ما ينبئ عن القيام بالذات، وفيه ما ينبئ عن كونه أصلا'.
Understand that Nasafi is making a claim about the word's inherent meaning.
2
Study the analogy
Nasafi uses the example of 'mutaḥarrik' (moving) to illustrate that a name is applied for the specific meaning that attaches to it.
The analogy shows that if motion attaches to something, it is called moving because of that motion, not for another reason.
3
Apply the principle
Understand that if 'jawhar' linguistically indicates 'origin', then the term should be defined by that meaning, not by self-subsistence.
This argument strengthens Nasafi's case that God is not a jawhar (if jawhar means origin, since God is not composed).

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition of 'jawhar' (substance/atom) as 'qā'im bi-dhātih' (self-subsistent). Nasafi argues that the correct definition is 'aṣl yutarkabu minhu al-ajsām' (an origin from which bodies are composed). He begins by noting that if a body is composed of jawhar, then the body itself is not a jawhar. Then he addresses the Mu'tazilite claim that 'jawhar' denotes self-subsistence and that this definition is valid through co-extensiveness (ṭard wa 'aks). Nasafi counters: if 'jawhariyya' (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence, it also rotates with being an origin for bodies. The Mu'tazilites cannot privilege one over the other without justification. He then uses a second argument: in the witnessed world, every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and vice versa. If the Mu'tazilites accept this as a definition, they contradict their own principle that God is not receptive to accidents. If they reject it, they undermine their own method of using co-extensiveness. Next, he presents a third argument based on linguistic indication: the word 'jawhar' itself suggests 'aṣl' (origin) and not 'qā'im bi-dhātih'. Therefore, if one applies the name to God, it must be for the meaning of 'aṣl', not self-subsistence. He supports this with an analogy: a thing is called 'moving' only because motion attaches to it, not because of its existence or self-subsistence. Similarly, attributes like black, white, etc., are named for their specific meanings. He then generalizes: a meaning that is inseparable from a thing may not be causally related; transfer to the unseen requires that the meaning cannot be attributed to anything else. In the case of jawhar, since it can be attributed to being an origin in addition to self-subsistence, the definition as self-subsistent fails. He concludes that this argument is valid against the Mu'tazilites because they themselves define 'accident' by a logical condition rather than linguistic indication, but here they are forced to accept the linguistic evidence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of a term is purely conventional and can ignore linguistic roots.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the linguistic indication (dalāla) of the word must be considered in determining its definition, especially when applying it to God.

?
The analogy of 'moving' shows that names are tied to the specific meaning that causes the name. Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate

The General Principle: Inseparability and Transfer to the Unseen
Nasafi establishes a general principle about inseparable attributes and causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's principle that an inseparable attribute only defines a thing if it cannot be attributed to any other meaning, and how this applies to the definition of jawhar.

Core Idea
Inseparability does not imply causation; transfer requires impossibility of another meaning.

Nasafi concludes with a general methodological point: just because a property is inseparable from a thing does not mean it is the cause or the defining attribute. To transfer a term like 'jawhar' to God, we must ensure that the meaning cannot be attributed to anything else. Here, since 'jawhar' can be attributed to 'origin' as well, the definition as 'self-subsistent' fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the general rule
Read the opening sentence of the Arabic passage: 'وجاء من هذا أن كل معنى تعلق بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما...'
This sets up a general principle about inseparable attachment.
2
Identify the condition
Find the condition for causal transfer: 'بشريطة استحالة إضافته إلى معنى آخر'.
This condition is crucial for Nasafi's argument.
3
Apply to the case of jawhar
Nasafi explains that since being an origin is another possible meaning for jawhar, self-subsistence cannot be the defining attribute. Understand why the Mu'tazilites are inconsistent.
Consider the contrast with the example of 'moving' where no other meaning is possible.

بأنه القائم بالذات وخصومكم يمنعونكم عن ذلك. ثم يقال لهم: أليس أن في الشاهد كل جوهر قابل للعرض، وكل ما هو قابل للعرض جوهر؟ فلابد من أن يقولوا: بلى. قيل: أتجعلون كونه قابلا للعرض حدا له، لدورانه معه وجودا وعدما، وطردا وعكسا؟ فإن قالوا نعم تركوا أصلهم، فإنهم يقولون بأنه تعالى ليس بقابل للأعراض. وإن قالوا: لا، أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم يقال لهم: ما تنكرون على من يقول: إن الله تعالى ليس بجوهر، لأن حد الجوهر أنه قابل للأعراض؟ ويستدل على صحة حده بالطرد والعكس والدوران وجودا وعدما، فإن قبلوا ذلك تركوا مذهبهم وإن لم يقبلوا أبطلوا دليلهم. ويقال لهم: لما كانت الجوهرية القيام بالذات وجودا وعدما، وكذا كونه أصلا للأجسام ثم ليس من لفظ الجوهر ما ينبئ عن القيام بالذات، وفيه ما ينبئ عن كونه أصلا، كان جعله جوهرا لكونه أصلا، أولى من جعله جوهرا لكونه قائما بالذات إذ إطلاق اسم معنوي على محل وجد فيه ذلك المعنى، لن يكون إلا لذلك المعنى، ومن ادعى إطلاقه عليه لا لذلك المعنى بل لأمر آخر كان مخطئا في دعواه، كمن ادعى أن ما قامت به الحركة لم يسم متحركا لقيام الحركة به بل لوجوده أو لقيامه بالذات كان مخطئا في دعواه، وكذا في الأسود والأبيض والمجتمع والمتفرق وغير ذلك من الصفات فكذا هذا. وجاء من هذا أن كل معنى تعلق بشيء تعلقا لا انفكاك بينهما، ويدور معه وجودا وعدما لا يوجب تعلقه به تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولا يوجب تعدي أحدهما إلى الغائب تعدية الآخر على الإطلاق، بل بشريطة استحالة إضافته إلى معنى آخر، فأما ما لم يشتمل إضافته إلى معنى آخر فلم يعلم أن تعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، ولهذا لم يجعل قولنا القابل للأعراض حدا للجوهر على ما قررنا، وإن وجد الدوران، لأنه يمكن أن يضاف كونه جوهرا إلى معنى آخر وراء كونه قابلا للأعراض، ويجعل تعلق قيام الحركة بمحل لاتصاف المحل بكونه متحركا تعلق العلة بالمعلول، لما أن إضافة كونه متحركا يستحيل إلى معنى آخر سوى قيام الحركة به، وفيما نحن فيه لا يستحيل إضافة كونه جوهرا على معنى سوى القيام بالذات وهو كونه أصلا بل وجبت الإضافة إليه لما مر من الدلالة اللغوية على ذلك فلم يجز إضافته إلى كونه قائما بالذات، وهذا الجواب مما لا يمكن التعويل عليه على أصول المعتزلة، لأنهم يجعلون حد العرض مما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه وإن لم يكن في لفظة العرض ما يدل عليه، ولم يجعلوا حده ما يستحيل الدوام عليه وإن كانت لفظة العرض من حيث اللغة منبئا عنه. وكذا النصارى يجعلون هكذا في حد
From it [the body], it is not a jawhar. And their claim that 'jawhar' is a name for the self-subsistent, and that its being self-subsistent is a definition of it, is a forbidden claim. As for their claim of the validity of co-extensiveness and converse in the witnessed realm, we said to them: just as jawhariyya (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence in co-extensiveness and converse, existence and non-existence, so too does it rotate with its being an origin from which bodies are composed. So why is your making 'self-subsistent' its definition more appropriate than your opponents making 'its being an origin' its definition? And you are the claimants, when you want to transfer the name to the unseen by transferring the description that He is self-subsistent, while your opponents prevent you from that. Then it is said to them: Is it not the case that in the witnessed realm every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and everything receptive to accidents is a jawhar? They must say: Yes. It is said: Do you make its being receptive to accidents a definition of it, due to its rotation with it in existence and non-existence, and co-extensiveness and converse? If they say yes, they abandon their principle, for they say that He, exalted, is not receptive to accidents. If they say no, they invalidate their proof. Then it is said to them: What do you deny of one who says: God, exalted, is not a jawhar, because the definition of jawhar is that it is receptive to accidents, and he proves the validity of his definition by co-extensiveness, converse, and rotation in existence and non-existence? For if they accept that, they abandon their school, and if they do not accept, they invalidate their proof. And it is said to them: Since jawhariyya (substantiality) is self-subsistence in existence and non-existence, and likewise its being an origin for bodies, yet there is nothing in the word 'jawhar' that indicates self-subsistence, while there is in it what indicates its being an origin, then making it a jawhar because it is an origin is more appropriate than making it a jawhar because it is self-subsistent; for the application of a meaningful name to a locus in which that meaning is found can only be for that meaning. And whoever claims its application to it not for that meaning but for another matter is mistaken in his claim, like one who claims that that to which motion attaches is not called 'moving' because of the attachment of motion to it, but because of its existence or its being self-subsistent, he would be mistaken in his claim. Similarly for black, white, gathered, separated, and other attributes. So too this. And from this it follows that every meaning that attaches to a thing with an inseparable attachment, and rotates with it in existence and non-existence, does not necessitate that its attachment to it is the attachment of cause to effect, nor does it necessitate transferring one of them to the unseen with the absolute transfer of the other; rather, it is conditioned upon the impossibility of its attribution to another meaning. As for that which does not preclude its attribution to another meaning, it is not known that the attachment of one to the other is the attachment of cause to effect. For this reason, we did not make our statement 'receptive to accidents' a definition of jawhar, as we established, even if rotation is found, because its being a jawhar can be attributed to another meaning beyond its being receptive to accidents. And the attachment of the occurrence of motion to a locus, due to the locus being described as 'moving', is made the attachment of cause to effect, because the attribution of its being moving to another meaning other than the attachment of motion to it is impossible. In our case, it is not impossible to attribute its being a jawhar to a meaning other than self-subsistence, namely its being an origin; rather, attribution to it is obligatory due to the linguistic indication that passed, so attribution to its being self-subsistent is not permissible. And this answer cannot be relied upon according to the principles of the Mu'tazilites, because they make the definition of accident that which is impossible to subsist by itself, even if there is nothing in the word 'accident' that indicates it, and they do not make its definition that which is impossible to endure, even if the word 'accident' linguistically indicates it. Similarly, the Christians do thus in the definition of...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition of 'jawhar' (substance/atom) as 'qā'im bi-dhātih' (self-subsistent). Nasafi argues that the correct definition is 'aṣl yutarkabu minhu al-ajsām' (an origin from which bodies are composed). He begins by noting that if a body is composed of jawhar, then the body itself is not a jawhar. Then he addresses the Mu'tazilite claim that 'jawhar' denotes self-subsistence and that this definition is valid through co-extensiveness (ṭard wa 'aks). Nasafi counters: if 'jawhariyya' (substantiality) rotates with self-subsistence, it also rotates with being an origin for bodies. The Mu'tazilites cannot privilege one over the other without justification. He then uses a second argument: in the witnessed world, every jawhar is receptive to accidents, and vice versa. If the Mu'tazilites accept this as a definition, they contradict their own principle that God is not receptive to accidents. If they reject it, they undermine their own method of using co-extensiveness. Next, he presents a third argument based on linguistic indication: the word 'jawhar' itself suggests 'aṣl' (origin) and not 'qā'im bi-dhātih'. Therefore, if one applies the name to God, it must be for the meaning of 'aṣl', not self-subsistence. He supports this with an analogy: a thing is called 'moving' only because motion attaches to it, not because of its existence or self-subsistence. Similarly, attributes like black, white, etc., are named for their specific meanings. He then generalizes: a meaning that is inseparable from a thing may not be causally related; transfer to the unseen requires that the meaning cannot be attributed to anything else. In the case of jawhar, since it can be attributed to being an origin in addition to self-subsistence, the definition as self-subsistent fails. He concludes that this argument is valid against the Mu'tazilites because they themselves define 'accident' by a logical condition rather than linguistic indication, but here they are forced to accept the linguistic evidence.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla)
معلول (maʿlūl)
تعلق (taʿalluq)
استحالة (istiḥāla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition does Nasafi give for transferring a term to the unseen?
A) The attribute must be co-extensive
B) The attribute must be inseparable
C) The attribute must be impossible to attribute to another meaning
D) The attribute must be mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Nasafi establishes a methodological rule: an inseparable attribute only defines a thing if it cannot be attributed to any other meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely assume that things you experience consistently (like motion and moving) are necessarily connected as cause and effect. But the lesson warns that inseparability does not guarantee causation. Where in your own beliefs about Allah's attributes might you be guilty of assuming a necessary connection without proven impossibility of alternative attribution? Will you re-examine that assumption today?
?
The passage criticizes the Mu'tazilites for defining 'accident' based on logical reasoning rather than linguistic revelation. Are there any theological conclusions you hold that are built more on your own reasoning than on the explicit wording of the Quran and Sunnah? This lesson demands you to assess the foundation of your beliefs—are they built on language or logic?
?
The principle that a definition must be based on an attribute that cannot be attributed to another meaning is applied to jawhar. Consider your own identity: what do you consider your defining attribute? Is it truly unique to you, or could it be attributed to something else? This introspection should remind you that only Allah's attributes are truly inseparable and defining—where is your ultimate identity rooted?

The Dispute Over the Definition of Body (Jism)
Sanusi presents the disagreement over the linguistic meaning of 'jism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the semantic disagreement between Muslims and their opponents regarding the linguistic definition of 'jism' (body) and its theological implications.

Core Idea
The definition of 'body' is contested, not its impossibility for Allah.

This passage from Sanusi's work addresses a key disagreement in Islamic theology: while both Muslims and their opponents agree that Allah cannot be a body in the composed sense, they differ on what the word 'jism' actually means. This semantic debate has important consequences for how we understand divine attributes. Let's examine Sanusi's presentation of this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that the passage is incomplete due to page break. Focus on the contrasting definitions of 'jism'.
The passage cuts off at 'وعند'. Do not worry about the missing part for now.
2
Identify the point of agreement
Notice that both Muslims and opponents agree that composition (تركب) is impossible for the Eternal (القديم). This is the common ground.
3
Distinguish the two definitions
Muslims define 'jism' as 'composed and compounded' (المتألف المتركب), while opponents define it simply as 'existent' (الموجد). Understand that this is a linguistic, not a factual, disagreement.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Ask yourself: If 'jism' simply means 'existent', then is it problematic to say Allah is a 'body'? How does this affect the discussion of divine attributes?
This connects to broader kalam discussions on anthropomorphism.

As for the discourse between us and those who oppose us concerning the term 'body' (jism) – with their denial of what is the true meaning of body, i.e., composition and compounding, and their agreement with us on the impossibility of that for the Ancient One, may His remembrance be exalted – it revolves around the term: what does it apply to in language? According to us: upon the composed, compounded, as preceded earlier in defining body. According to them: upon the existent. And according to ...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion on the definition of 'body' (jism). The author notes that despite opponents (likely the Mu'tazila or philosophers) agreeing with Muslims that composition is impossible for Allah, they disagree on the linguistic definition of jism. Muslims define jism as something composed and compounded, while opponents define it simply as 'existent'. The passage cuts off, but the main point is the contrast: the two definitions lead to different theological conclusions regarding divine attributes.

?

?
If the definition of a single term like 'jism' can be so contested, how much more careful must you be when speaking about Allah, the One beyond all human concepts? Will you take the time to learn the precise meaning of words in Islamic theology before using them?

Refuting the Objection on 'Qadim' and 'Aqdam'
Nasafi refutes an objection about tafdil in 'qadim' and 'aqdam'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi defends the argument that tafdil in terms like 'jism' indicates increase, while 'qadim' and 'aqdam' do not undermine that because they either involve a real meaning (qidam) or denote priority in existence.

Core Idea
Comparison terms like 'aqdam' do not imply increase in existence but indicate priority.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a clever objection: if we can say 'this is qadim and this is aqdam', doesn't that prove that comparative forms (tafdil) can be used without any underlying increase? This objection could damage the previous argument that the comparative 'ajsam' must indicate increase in parts. Nasafi responds with two powerful arguments, clarifying the nature of qidam and the proper use of 'aqdam'. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that the existence of comparative forms like 'aqdam' shows that tafdil does not always imply increase in the underlying attribute. Identify the core of their challenge.
Notice they use the phrase 'ليس في ورود لفظة التفضيل... من معنى ما يدل على ثبوت التزايد'.
2
Understand Nasafi's sharp response
Nasafi first dismisses the objection as bankrupt. Then he provides two distinct answers. The first answer states that qidam is a real meaning (ma'na) according to early Maturidis, so comparison and increase are possible. The second answer clarifies that even if qidam is not a meaning, 'aqdam' does not indicate increase in existence but priority in existence.
Pay attention to the two-part structure: 'ثم نقول: القدم عند قدماء أصحابنا معنى...' and the alternative view.
3
Connect to the original argument about jism
Nasafi concludes by contrasting the case of jism, where tafdil is based on increase in parts (kathrat al-ajza'), which is a real increase. Terms like 'qadim' and 'aqdam' do not involve increase in existence or self-subsistence.
This shows that the objection does not invalidate the earlier reasoning about ajsam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ليس في ورود لفظة التفضيل والمبالغة من معنى ما يدل على ثبوت التزايد في ذلك المعنى فإنهم يقولون: هذا قديم وهذا أقدم منه ، والقدم ليس بمعنى، وما ليس بمعنى لا يجري فيه التزايد قيل : هذا كلام لا يحمل عليه إلا الإفلاس والوقاحة، فإن إثبات التفضيل فيما لا يتصور فيه التزايد والتفضيل محال ، ومثل هذا المحال لا يصح بما نظن أنه نظيره ثم نقول : القدم عند قدماء أصحابنا معنى فيجري فيه التفاضل، بعضهم وإن لم يكن هو معنى ولكنه اسم للمتقدم في الوجود بشرط المبالغة لا لمطلق الوجود، فيكون قولهم : أقدم أي أسبق وجودا ، ولا يطلق إلا عند هذا، فأما فيما نحن فيه فلا تزايد في الوجود ولا في القيام بالذات، ولا يطلق لفظة التفضيل عنه عند ترادف الوجود أو السبق في الوجود أو التأخر، إنما يطلق عند كثرة
For it is said: 'The occurrence of the expression of comparison (tafḍīl) and intensification (mubālagha) does not indicate the establishment of increase in that meaning, for they say: “This is eternal (qadīm) and this is more eternal (aqdam) from it”, and eternality (al-qidam) is not a meaning (maʻnā), and what is not a meaning does not allow increase.' It is said: 'This speech cannot be borne except by bankruptcy and impudence, for establishing comparison in what cannot be conceived as having increase and comparison is impossible. And such an impossible thing cannot be made correct by what we think is its parallel. Then we say: Eternality (al-qidam), according to our early companions, is a meaning, so comparison applies to it. Some of them [say], even if it is not a meaning, it is a name for what is prior in existence (al-mutaqaddim fī al-wujūd) with the condition of intensification, not for absolute existence. So their saying “aqdam” means “more prior in existence” (asbaq wujūdan), and it is not used except for this. As for our present case, there is no increase in existence nor in self-subsistence, and the expression of comparison is not used for it when existence is equal or priority in existence or posteriority; it is only used when there is abundance (kathra).'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a point: The use of comparative and intensive forms (like 'aqdam' from 'qadim') does not necessarily prove that the underlying meaning admits of increase. For example, people say 'this is qadim and this is aqdam from it', yet qidam (eternality) is not a real meaning (maʿnā) that can increase; it is just a name. So the previous argument linking mubalagha/tafdil to increase in meaning is not absolute. The author responds sharply: this objection is born of bankruptcy and impudence. He explains that establishing comparison in something that cannot even be conceived of having increase is impossible. To compare two things in a quality that cannot vary is absurd. Then he offers a two-part answer. First, according to the early Maturidi scholars, qidam is a real meaning (maʿnā) that allows comparison. Some early scholars treat it as a meaning that can vary. Second, even if one does not consider qidam a meaning, the word 'aqdam' is not used for increase in existence itself, but as an expression for priority in existence (asbaq wujūdan) with a connotation of intensification—it denotes 'more prior', not 'more existent'. So the comparison here is not based on increase in existence, but on temporal priority. In the case of jism, however, the comparison is explicitly based on increase in parts (kathrat al-ajzā'), which is a real increase. Therefore, the objection does not undermine the original argument: existence and self-subsistence do not admit increase, and terms like 'jism' are tied to increase, not to those stable attributes.

Key Terms
قديم (qadīm)
أقدم (aqdam)
قدم (qidam)
معنى (maʻnā)
تزايد (tazāyud)
تفضيل (tafḍīl)
سبق في الوجود (sabq fī al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the use of comparative forms like 'aqdam' proves that qidam can increase, or conversely, that since 'aqdam' exists, tafdil does not require increase at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that either qidam is a meaning that does admit increase, or 'aqdam' indicates priority in existence (not increase in existence). Thus, the objection does not undermine the argument that tafdil in 'jism' implies increase in parts.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does 'أقدم' (aqdam) mean if qidam is not a real meaning?
A) More eternal in essence
B) More prior in existence
C) Greater in number of parts
D) A meaningless term

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the objection by showing that 'aqdam' either relies on qidam as a meaningful attribute admitting increase or simply denotes priority in existence, neither of which undermines the original argument that tafdil in 'jism' indicates increase in parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'al-Qadim', do you truly understand that His eternity is beyond any concept of 'more eternal' or comparison? Or do you unknowingly reduce His attributes to human measures? Reflect on how your heart's understanding of Allah's attributes might still carry traces of creaturely comparisons. What must you consciously reject today?
?
The objector in this passage relies on a superficial similarity between words. Where in your own religious thinking do you use analogies that might lead to mistaken conclusions about Allah? Are you willing to surrender that comfortable reasoning for the sake of tanzih?
?
Nasafi calls the objection 'bankruptcy and impudence'. When you hear or read objections about Allah's attributes, does your heart respond with the same firmness? Or do you waver? What would it take for you to be as anchored in the creed as these scholars were?
