The Ash'ari Definition of Two Similar Things
Author states the Ash'ari definition and the first proof from self-resemblance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of two similar things (al-mithlayn) and the first indication that similarity applies only between distinct things.

Core Idea
Two similar things are two distinct realities, each substitutable for the other.

Al-Ash'ari and his followers defined similarity in a precise way: two similar things are two distinct entities, each capable of taking the place of the other. This definition is essential for understanding the Islamic theological rejection of anthropomorphism. In this lesson, we examine the definition itself and the first logical indication that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the key terms 'المشتبهين' and 'المثلين'.
The definition includes two conditions: distinctness and substitutability.
2
Understand the first indication
The passage argues that a thing does not resemble itself. Ask yourself: why does this show that similarity requires two distinct things?
3
Reflect on the implication
If similarity requires distinctness, then nothing can be similar to itself. This is a basic logical point that underpins the entire argument against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلتنا على نفي التشبيه إن الناس قد اختلفوا في ذلك فزعم الأشعري ومن تابعه أن المشتبهين والمثلين هما غيران يسد كل واحد منهما مسد صاحبه، ودلالة تقيد الحد بالمغايرة أن الشيء لا يشبه نفسه ولا يماثله فدل أن ذلك جار بين المتغايرين ودلالة قولهم: يسد مسده فإن ما لا يسد مسد صاحبه لا يعد مثلا له وإن كانت بينهما موافقة في أوصاف كثيرة كالسواد مع البياض فإنهما ليسا بمثلين وإن كان كل واحد منهما موجودا، وعرضا ولونا، فدل ارتفاع المماثلة بين هذين اللونين مع حصول الموافقة بينهما فيما بينا من الأوصاف على أن لا مماثلة مع ثبوت المخالفة بوجه من الوجوه إذ عند ثبوت المخالفة لا يسد أحدهما مسد صاحبه، وهذه هي النكتة في وهي بين المخالفة والمماثلة من التقابل ما بين المتضادين فلا يتصور أن اجتماعهما في محل واحد، فكان من ضرورة ثبوت المخالفة انتفاء المماثلة، ولأن سير الأحوال يظهر صحة هذا الحد، فإن المتماثلين ما كانا متماثلين لكونهما جوهرين لجريان المماثلة بين العرضين، ولا لكونهما عرضين لجريانها بين الجوهرين، ولا لكونهما سوادين لجريانها في غيرهما، وكذا لا لكونهما بياضين أو طعمين أو رائحتين، ولا لكون المماثلة معنى قائما بالمتماثلين.. لجريانها بين العرضين مع استحالة قيام العرض بالعرض. المسألة وإذا امتنعت هذه الوجوه لم يبق إلا ما ذكر
Our proofs for the negation of anthropomorphism. Indeed people differed about that. Al-Ash'ari and those who followed him claimed that the two similar things (al-mushtabihayn) and the two likes (al-mithlayn) are two distinct things, each one taking the place of its companion. And the indication of restricting the definition to difference (al-mughayara) is that a thing does not resemble itself nor is it like itself, so that indicates that this applies between distinct things. And the indication of their statement 'it takes its place' is that that which does not take the place of its companion is not considered a likeness to it, even if there is agreement between them in many attributes, like blackness with whiteness, for they are not likes even though each one is existent, an accident, and a color. So the absence of likeness between these two colors, despite the agreement between them in the attributes we mentioned, indicates that there is no likeness when there is difference in any respect, because when difference is established, one does not take the place of its companion. And this is the point: there is between difference and likeness a relation of opposition, so it is inconceivable that they both exist in one locus. So it follows necessarily from the establishment of difference the negation of likeness. And because the course of circumstances shows the correctness of this definition: indeed the two similar ones are not similar by virtue of being two substances, because similarity occurs between accidents; nor by virtue of being two accidents, because it occurs between substances; nor by virtue of being two black things, because similarity occurs in other things; likewise not by virtue of being two white things or two tastes or two smells; nor because similarity is a meaning subsisting in the two similar things, because it occurs between accidents while it is impossible for an accident to subsist in an accident. The question: And when these ways are impossible, nothing remains except what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a proof against anthropomorphism based on the definition of similarity (tamathul) provided by al-Ash'ari and his followers. The author begins by stating that people differed on the definition. The Ash'ari definition is that two similar things (or two likes) are two distinct entities, each capable of taking the place of the other. The author provides two indications supporting this definition: first, a thing does not resemble itself, so similarity applies only to distinct things. Second, if something cannot take the place of another, it is not considered its likeness, even if they share many attributes (like black and white, which share existence, accidental nature, and color, yet are not similar). Therefore, the presence of any difference negates similarity, because difference and likeness are opposites that cannot coexist. The author then refutes alternative bases for defining similarity: it is not due to both being substances, because accidents can be similar; nor due to both being accidents, because substances can be similar; nor due to both being black, because similarity also occurs in other colors; nor due to being white, taste, or smell. Finally, it cannot be due to similarity being a quality inherent in the similar things, because that would require an accident to inhere in an accident, which is impossible. Thus, the only remaining correct definition is the one stated. This establishes a precise criterion for similarity, which is then used to argue that God cannot be similar to anything, since He is utterly unique.

Key Terms
المشتبهين
المثلين
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think similarity can be defined simply by sharing attributes, without requiring full substitutability.
Correction: The Ash'ari definition requires both distinctness and substitutability; sharing some attributes is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari definition in this passage, what are the two conditions for two things to be considered similar (مثلين)?
A) They must be of the same color and shape.
B) They must be two distinct things and each can take the place of the other.
C) They must be two accidents or two substances.
D) They must share the same genus.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of similarity requires two distinct things that are mutually substitutable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is merciful', do you ever think of His mercy as similar to human mercy? How does the Ash'ari definition of similarity—requiring two distinct entities—change how you should understand God's attributes? What is one practical way you can remind yourself that God's mercy is not like any other?
?
Consider the phrase 'each one takes the place of the other'. In what ways do you try to 'substitute' something for God in your life—whether it's worldly goals, relationships, or comforts? What would it take for you to truly believe that nothing and no one can take His place?

Why Sharing Attributes Does Not Ensure Similarity
Author explains the second indication and the opposition between difference and likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to the Ash'ari definition, things like black and white are not similar despite sharing many attributes, and how difference negates similarity.

Core Idea
Even if two things share many attributes, they are not similar if they differ in any respect.

Al-Ash'ari's definition of similarity requires full substitutability. Two things may share many attributes, like black and white both being colors and accidents, yet they are not similar because they are not interchangeable. This lesson examines the second indication from the passage and the logical relationship between difference and likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the second indication
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the example of blackness and whiteness: they share existence, accidental nature, and color, yet are not similar.
2
Understand the concept of substitutability
Ask yourself: why can't black take the place of white? Because they differ in their specific nature. This difference prevents substitutability.
3
Grasp the opposition between difference and likeness
The passage states that difference and likeness are opposites (تقابل). When difference is present, likeness is impossible. Why is that important for the argument against anthropomorphism?
If God is different from all creation, then nothing can be like Him.

Refuting Alternative Criteria for Similarity
Author refutes alternative foundations for similarity and affirms the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari definition cannot be based on shared substance, accident, color, or a quality inhering in the similar things.

Core Idea
Similarity is not due to being substances, accidents, colors, or a quality, but only due to full substitutability.

After establishing the definition and its implications, the author anticipates and refutes alternative possible bases for similarity. Could two things be similar because they are both substances? Both accidents? Both black? Or because similarity is a quality in them? The passage systematically eliminates each possibility, leaving only the stated definition. This lesson examines these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutations
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify each proposed basis and its counterargument.
2
Understand the logic behind each refutation
For each, the author shows that if that basis were sufficient, similarity would hold in cases where it obviously does not. For example, if being a substance were the basis, then accidents could never be similar—but they are.
Follow the pattern: not by X, because similarity occurs in non-X.
3
Grasp the final impossibility
The last refutation targets the idea that similarity is a quality inhering in the two things. The argument is that similarity occurs between two accidents, but an accident cannot inhere in another accident. So this basis fails too.
This uses a metaphysical principle: accidents cannot subsist in accidents.
4
Conclude with the only remaining definition
When all alternatives are impossible, only the original definition (distinctness + substitutability) remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Four Types of Similarity (Mumathala)
Al-Nasafi defines similarity as a genus with four species and explains the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that similarity (mumathala) is a genus comprising four specific types, and you will grasp the definitions of the first two: mushabaha (likeness in quality) and mudahah (correspondence in relation).

Core Idea
Similarity is not one thing; it has four distinct forms.

Al-Nasafi has argued that partial similarity between creator and creation is possible. To clarify, he now defines similarity as a broad term covering four species. This classification shows that similarity occurs in different aspects—qualitative, relational, and others—so it is not a single monolithic concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi first states that mumathala is a genus with four species, then defines the first two.
The passage ends after explaining the second type; the remaining two are not elaborated here.
2
Identify the four species
List the four terms: mushabaha, mudahah, mushakala, musawa. Understand that they are distinct categories under the generic term mumathala.
3
Understand the first type: mushabaha
Mushabaha is defined as likeness in a type of quality (kayfiyya), e.g., two substances both capable of receiving colors. This is similarity in a qualitative aspect.
The example shows that accidents like colors can be shared by different substances.
4
Understand the second type: mudahah
Mudahah is defined as correspondence in a type of relation (idafa), e.g., Zayd and 'Amr both being sons of Khalid. This is similarity in a relational aspect.
The relation is the same, but the individuals are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن المماثلة اسم جنس يشتمل على أنواعه، وأنواعه أربعة وهي: المشابهة، والمضاهاة، والمشاكلة، والمساواة. فأما المشابهة: فجارية في الحقيقة على نوع من الكيفية، على صورة واحدة كاشتراك الذاتين في قبول الألوان، وغيرها من الأعراض. وأما المضاهاة في الحقيقة فهي: جارية في نوع من الإضافة كاشتراك زيد وعمرو في النسبة إلى خالد إذا كان أبا لهما، فإن زيدا كما ينسب إلى خالد فيقال: هو ابنه . ينسب إليه عمرو أيضا على تلك الجهة.
What verifies it is that similarity (mumathala) is a generic name encompassing its types. Its types are four: al-mushabaha (likeness), al-mudahah (correspondence), al-mushakala (similitude), and al-musawa (equality). As for al-mushabaha: it runs in reality upon a type of quality (kayfiyya), upon a single form, like the sharing of two essences in receiving colors and other accidents. As for al-mudahah: in reality it runs upon a type of relation (idafa), like the sharing of Zayd and 'Amr in relation to Khalid when he is father to both, for Zayd is as he is related to Khalid, it is said: he is his son, and 'Amr is also related to him in that same manner.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage. Al-Nasafi explains that the term 'mumathala (similarity)' is a generic noun that includes four specific types: mushabaha (likeness), mudahah (correspondence), mushakala (similitude), and musawa (equality). He then defines the first two. (1) Mushabaha pertains to a type of quality (kayfiyya) in a single form, e.g., two substances sharing the ability to receive colors or other accidents. This is similarity in a qualitative aspect. (2) Mudahah pertains to a type of relation (idafa), e.g., Zayd and 'Amr both being sons of Khalid. Here the similarity is in a relational property. The remaining two types (mushakala and musawa) are not described in the available text, though they are listed. This classification helps to show that similarity is not monolithic, supporting al-Nasafi's earlier argument that partial similarity is possible: two things can be similar in one type (e.g., qualitative) while differing in others.

Key Terms
مماثلة (mumathala)
مشابهة (mushabaha)
مضاهاة (mudahah)
مشاكلة (mushakala)
مساواة (musawa)
كيفية (kayfiyya)
إضافة (idafa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think similarity always means full equality in all respects.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that similarity is a genus with different types; two things can be similar in one aspect (e.g., qualitative) while differing in others.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how many types of similarity (mumathala) are there, and what is the basis of the first type (mushabaha)?
A) Two types; mushabaha is based on quantity.
B) Four types; mushabaha is based on a type of quality.
C) Three types; mushabaha is based on relation.
D) Four types; mushabaha is based on equality.

Key Takeaway
Mumathala (similarity) is a genus with four species, the first two being mushabaha (qualitative likeness) and mudahah (relational correspondence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own understanding of similarity—especially in matters of faith—do you tend to reduce everything to one type? Could it be that you have overlooked the relational dimension (idafa) that Al-Nasafi highlights? What would it cost you to accept that similarity can take forms you haven't considered? Are you willing to let this classification reshape how you think about Allah's attributes?
?
Al-Nasafi gives the example of two substances sharing the ability to receive colors. In your daily interactions, do you judge people or ideas as 'similar' based only on surface qualities? How might remembering the distinction between mushabaha and mudahah help you see others more deeply? Will you commit to pausing before labeling something as 'the same' and instead ask: 'In what type of similarity is this?'

Affirming Similarity in One Aspect
The author argues for partial similarity using linguistic and prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of partial similarity (al-mumāthala min wajh) is argued for using linguistic and prophetic evidence.

Core Idea
Similarity does not require complete equivalence; even partial likeness is sufficient.

In this passage, the author tackles a potential objection: how can we say something is 'like' something else if there are also differences? He shows that in everyday language and Islamic law, similarity is often understood as partial. By examining everyday examples and a prophetic hadith, he establishes that 'like for like' can refer to equality in a specific aspect only.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the author states a principle, then gives examples.
The passage uses both general linguistic patterns and a specific hadith.
2
Identify the examples
Find the three types of examples: everyday speech (Zayd and 'Amr), jurisprudence (substitute in a role), and the hadith (wheat exchange). Notice how each demonstrates partial similarity.
The hadith is the strongest evidence because it comes from the Prophet.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The principle is that 'like' can refer to similarity in one aspect even if there are differences in others. Reflect on why this matters for theological discussions.
This principle allows using the term 'mithl' for created things without implying complete likeness to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول : يجوز أن يكون شيء مماثلا لشيء من وجه مخالفا له من وجه، فإنا نجد أهل اللغة لا يمنعون من القول بأن زيدا مثل لعمرو، وفي الفقه إذا كان يساويه فيه ويسد مسده في ذلك الباب وإن كان بينهما مخالفة لوجوه كثيرة، وكذا في الطول والقصر والحسن والدمامة والشجاعة والجبن، وغير ذلك حتى إن السلطان لو ولى رجلا لحماية ثغر ثم إنه عزله بمن يقوم مقامه ويسد مسده في الحماية والقدرة على دفع الأعداء لا يمتنع أهل اللغة أن يقولوا: عزل السلطان فلانا بمن هو مثله، وإن كانت بينهما مخالفة في أسباب كثيرة وصفات جمة، وكذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: 'الحنطة بالحنطة مثلا بمثل' وأراد به الاستواء في الكيل دون الوزن والصلابة والرخاوة وأشباه ذلك.
We say: It is permissible for something to be similar to something from one aspect while differing from it from another aspect. For we find that the people of language do not forbid saying 'Zayd is like 'Amr', and in jurisprudence, if he equals him in it and takes his place in that area, even if there is between them difference in many respects. Similarly in length and shortness, beauty and ugliness, bravery and cowardice, and other things. So much so that if a sultan appoints a man to protect a frontier, then dismisses him with someone who takes his place and fills his role in protection and ability to repel enemies, the people of language do not refrain from saying: 'The sultan dismissed so-and-so with someone like him,' even if there is between them difference in many causes and numerous attributes. Likewise, the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like,' intending by this equality in measure, not in weight, hardness, softness, and the like.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is an argument supporting the permissibility of using the term 'like' (mithl) for partial similarity. The author begins by stating the principle that something can be similar in one respect and different in another. He then provides examples from everyday language: we call Zayd like 'Amr when they are equal in some context, despite many differences. Similarly, in legal reasoning, if one person replaces another in a specific role, they are called 'like' even with many other differences. The ultimate example is the Prophet's hadith about exchanging wheat, where 'like for like' refers to equality in measure only, not in other physical properties. Thus, the author establishes that similarity in one aspect is a valid basis for using the term.

مثل
مماثلة
وجه
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Thinking similarity requires complete equivalence in all respects.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that 'like' (mithl) can be used for partial similarity, as shown in language and the hadith.

Checkpoint

A) Zayd is like 'Amr in length
B) Wheat for wheat, like for like
C) The sultan appointing a man to a frontier
D) Two people sharing in medicine

Key Takeaway
The term 'like' (mithl) can refer to similarity in a specific aspect, not total equivalence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Types of Similarity
Enumeration and definition of four types of similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the four types of similarity (mushābaha, muḍāhāh, mushākala, musāwāh) as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Similarity is a generic term covering four distinct categories: quality, relation, substance, and quantity.

Before we can discuss theological comparisons, we need a precise vocabulary for similarity. In this passage, the author provides a detailed classification of similarity into four types, each corresponding to a different Aristotelian category. This sets the foundation for understanding how terms like 'like' and 'similar' are used in kalam and usul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the four types
Read the Arabic passage that introduces and defines each type: mushābaha, muḍāhāh, mushākala, musāwāh. Pay attention to the examples provided for each.
The examples are crucial for understanding the categories.
2
Identify the category for each type
Note that each type is associated with a specific Aristotelian category: quality (mushābaha), relation (muḍāhāh), substance (mushākala), and quantity (musāwāh). Compare the examples to see how they illustrate these categories.
3
Examine the generic noun concept
The passage begins by stating that similarity is a generic noun encompassing these types. Reflect on how a single generic term can cover multiple distinct meanings.
This is similar to 'animal' covering humans, beasts, etc.
4
Self-check with the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question that asks you to match a type to its example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أنواع المماثلة]: يحققه أن المماثلة اسم جنس يشتمل على أنواعه، وأنواعه أربعة وهي: المشابهة، والمضاهاة، والمشاكلة، والمساواة. فأما المشابهة: فجارية في الحقيقة على نوع من الكيفية، على صورة واحدة كاشتراك الذاتين في قبول الألوان، وغيرها من الأعراض. وأما المضاهاة في الحقيقة فهي: جارية في نوع من الإضافة كاشتراك زيد وعمرو في النسبة إلى خالد إذا كان أبا لهما، فإن زيدا كما ينسب إلى خالد فيقال: هو ابنه . ينسب إليه عمرو أيضا على تلك الجهة. وأما المشاكلة في الحقيقة فجارية في نوع من الجوهر على رتبة واحدة كثوبي قطن، وثوبي كتان كل واحد منهما من شكل صاحبه. وأما المساواة في الحقيقة فجارية في نوع من الكمية على مقدار واحد كخشبتين أو ثوبين كل واحد منهما عشرة أذرع أو صبرتين من حنطة كل واحدة منهما عشرة أقفرة، وزبرتين من حديد كل واحدة منهما خمسة أمناء. كذا قال بعض من له دراية بالحقائق : وإذا كانت المماثلة اسم جنس وتحته هذه الأنواع ثم لا شك أن إطلاق اسم الجنس على كل نوع من أنواعه جائز، فإن الآدمي يقال له حيوان، وكذا جميع أنواع الدواب والسباع والطيور وغير ذلك، وكذا إطلاق اسم المثل على هذه الأنواع كلها ثم قد يختص شيئان بثبوت المساواة بينهما وهي الاشتراك في القدر مع انعدام المشاكلة والمضاهاة والمشابهة، وكذا كل نوع مع سائر أنواعه، ولا شك أن عند انعدام الأنواع الأخر تثبت المخالفة من ذلك ذلك لا يمتنع أهل اللغة من إطلاق لفظة المماثلة لثبوت ما ثبت من الوجه، ومع له، هذه الأنواع، ولهذا قال أهل اللغة وأهل الأصول: كلام التشبيه لا عموم وإذا كان الأمر كذلك صح ما بينا غير أن الاستواء في الجهة التي تقع بها المشاركة شرط لإطلاق اسم المماثلة، فإن اثنين لو اشتركا في الكلام أو الفقه أو الطب أو غير ذلك من العلوم والصناعات، ولم يكن بينهما في ذلك النوع مساواة ينوب أحدهما مناب صاحبه ويسد مسده، لا يستجيز أحد من أرباب اللسان أن يقول : فلان مثل فلان في علم كذا أو في صفة كذا، وإذا كانت بينهما مساواة يسد أحدهما مسد
[Types of Similarity]: He verifies that similarity is a generic noun encompassing its types, and its types are four: al-mushābaha (resemblance), al-muḍāhāh (parallel), al-mushākala (conformity), and al-musāwāh (equality). As for al-mushābaha, it runs in reality on a type of quality, in a single form, like the sharing of two essences in receiving colors and other accidents. As for al-muḍāhāh in reality, it runs on a type of relation, like the sharing of Zayd and 'Amr in relation to Khalid if he is father to both, for Zayd as he is related to Khalid, it is said he is his son, and 'Amr is also related to him in that same manner. As for al-mushākala in reality, it runs on a type of substance in a single rank, like two cotton garments and two linen garments, each one is of the form of its companion. As for al-musāwāh in reality, it runs on a type of quantity in a single measure, like two pieces of wood or two garments each ten cubits, or two piles of wheat each ten aqfira, and two blocks of iron each five mann. Thus said one who has knowledge of realities: If similarity is a generic noun having these types, then there is no doubt that applying the generic noun to every type of its types is permissible. For example, the human is called animal, and likewise all types of beasts, predators, birds, and others. Likewise, applying the noun 'similar' to all these types. Then it may happen that two things are specifically characterized by the existence of equality between them, which is sharing in measure with the absence of conformity, parallelism, and resemblance. Likewise, every type with its other types. There is no doubt that when the other types are absent, difference is established; therefore, the people of language do not refrain from applying the word 'similarity' because of the existence of that which is established from the aspect... and therefore the people of language and the people of uṣūl said: 'The statement of tashbīh is not general.' And if it is so, what we have explained is correct, except that equality in the aspect in which participation occurs is a condition for applying the noun 'similarity'. For if two persons share in speech, jurisprudence, medicine, or other sciences and crafts, and there is not between them in that type equality such that one replaces the other and takes his place, no one from the people of the tongue would allow saying: 'So-and-so is like so-and-so in such science or attribute.' And if there is between them equality such that one replaces the other...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage first classifies the four types of similarity, each corresponding to a different Aristotelian category: resemblance (quality), parallelism (relation), conformity (substance), and equality (quantity). Then it discusses how the generic term 'similarity' applies to any of these types, just as 'animal' applies to all its species. The author also notes that sometimes only one type, equality, exists between two things without the others. Finally, he stipulates that for something to be called 'like' another, there must be equality in the specific aspect of sharing—mere sharing without the ability to substitute is insufficient. The text is cut off before completing the thought.

Key Terms
مماثلة
مشابهة
مضاهاة
مشاكلة
مساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that equality (musāwāh) is the only type of similarity.
Correction: The author clarifies that there are three other distinct types: resemblance, parallel, and conformity.

Checkpoint
Which type of similarity is defined as 'running on a type of quality'?
A) Mushābaha
B) Muḍāhāh
C) Mushākala
D) Musāwāh

Key Takeaway
The author classifies similarity into four distinct types based on Aristotelian categories: quality, relation, substance, and quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Could that lack of precision be causing misunderstandings in your relationships? This week, commit to clarifying what kind of similarity you mean when you compare yourself to others.

?

Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes might you be wrongly applying one type of similarity (e.g., musāwāh) to a category that does not belong (e.g., mushābaha)? Conditions for Applying the Term Similarity
Discussion of the usage of the generic term 'similarity' and the condition of equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for two things to be called 'similar' in a specific aspect, they must have equality (musāwāh) in that aspect allowing one to substitute for the other.

Core Idea
Mere sharing is not enough; true similarity requires equality in the shared aspect.

After classifying the types of similarity, the author addresses a practical question: when can we correctly say two things are 'like' each other? He argues that the generic term 'similarity' can be applied to any of its types, but for a specific comparison, equality in the relevant aspect is necessary. This clarifies an important rule in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the generic noun and condition
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'Wa idhā kānat al-mumāthala ism jins...' to the end. Note how the author first states the permissibility of applying the generic noun to any type, then introduces the condition of equality.
2
Distinguish between generic and specific application
The author explains that just as 'animal' applies to all species, 'similarity' applies to any of its types. However, when we say 'X is like Y in a certain respect', more is needed.
3
Understand the condition of equality
Focus on the condition: for two things to be called similar in a specific science or attribute, they must have equality so that one can substitute for the other. Mere sharing without equality is insufficient.
4
Test with the checkpoint
Apply the condition to the example given: two people who share knowledge of medicine but are not equal — can we say one is like the other?

Definition of مشاكلة and مساواة as Types of مماثلة
Defines مشاكلة and مساواة as types of مماثلة and states مماثلة is a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meanings of مشاكلة and مساواة as specific types of مماثلة, and how مماثلة functions as a genus term in Kalam discussions.

Core Idea
مشاكلة and مساواة are distinct types of مماثلة, not synonyms.

In this passage, the author establishes the conceptual distinction between two key terms used in discussions of similarity: مشاكلة (likeness in substance) and مساواة (equality in quantity). Both fall under the broader genus of مماثلة (similitude). Understanding these precise definitions is crucial for accurately interpreting theological comparisons. Let's examine the passage to see how these terms are defined and how they relate to the genus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two definitions
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how the author defines مشاكلة as occurring in substance and مساواة in quantity. The examples given (two cotton garments, two pieces of wood) clarify each type.
Pay attention to the specific conditions: 'on one rank' for مشاكلة and 'on one measure' for مساواة.
2
Understand the genus-species relationship
Notice that the author states مماثلة is a genus name that applies to both types, similar to how 'animal' applies to humans and beasts. This analogy helps grasp the logical structure.
3
Reflect on the language principle
The passage concludes that language permits using مماثلة even when other specific types are absent, as long as some shared aspect exists. This explains why the term is flexible.
The principle 'kalam al-tashbih la umum' (discourse of comparison is not general) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشاكلة في الحقيقة فجارية في نوع من الجوهر على رتبة واحدة كثوبي قطن، وثوبي كتان كل واحد منهما من شكل صاحبه. وأما المساواة في الحقيقة فجارية في نوع من الكمية على مقدار واحد كخشبتين أو ثوبين كل واحد منهما عشرة أذرع أو صبرتين من حنطة كل واحدة منهما عشرة أقفرة، وزبرتين من حديد كل واحدة منهما خمسة أمناء. كذا قال بعض من له دراية بالحقائق : وإذا كانت المماثلة اسم جنس وتحته هذه الأنواع ثم لا شك أن إطلاق اسم الجنس على كل نوع من أنواعه جائز، فإن الآدمي يقال له حيوان، وكذا جميع أنواع الدواب والسباع والطيور وغير ذلك، وكذا إطلاق اسم المثل على هذه الأنواع كلها ثم قد يختص شيئان بثبوت المساواة بينهما وهي الاشتراك في القدر مع انعدام المشاكلة والمضاهاة والمشابهة، وكذا كل نوع مع سائر أنواعه، ولا شك أن عند انعدام الأنواع الأخر تثبت المخالفة من ذلك ذلك لا يمتنع أهل اللغة من إطلاق لفظة المماثلة لثبوت ما ثبت من الوجه، ومع له، هذه الأنواع، ولهذا قال أهل اللغة وأهل الأصول: كلام التشبيه لا عموم.
As for similarity (مشاكلة) in essence, it occurs in a type of substance on one rank, like two cotton garments, two linen garments each of the same shape. As for equality (مساواة) in essence, it occurs in a type of quantity on one measure, like two pieces of wood or two garments each ten cubits, or two piles of wheat each ten qafiz, and two pieces of iron each five weights. Thus said some who have knowledge of realities. And since مماثلة (similitude) is a genus name and under it are these types, then there is no doubt that applying the genus name to each of its types is permissible, for man is called animal, and likewise all types of beasts, predators, birds and others, and likewise applying the name 'مثل' (like) to all these types. Then two things may be specified by the existence of equality between them, which is sharing in quantity with the absence of مشاكلة (likeness), مضاهاة (resemblance), and مشابهة (similarity). And likewise each type with its other types. And there is no doubt that when the other types are absent, difference is established. That does not prevent the people of language from using the word مماثلة due to the existence of whatever aspect is present. And for this reason the people of language and the people of usul said: The discourse of tashbih (comparison) is not general.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage defines two types of مماثلة (similitude): مشاكلة (likeness in substance) and مساواة (equality in quantity). It then explains that مماثلة is a genus term that can be applied to both types, just as 'animal' is a genus for humans and beasts. The author emphasizes that the people of language allow using مماثلة even when other types are absent, as long as some shared aspect exists. The passage concludes with the principle that the discourse of tashbih (comparison) is not general, meaning that the term 'like' is not used for all types of sharing.

The Condition for Using مماثلة: Equality in the Aspect of Participation
States the condition for applying مماثلة with examples and application to bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term مماثلة (similitude) can only be applied when there is exact equality in the specific aspect of participation, not mere partial similarity.

Core Idea
مماثلة requires equality in the relevant aspect, not just any shared feature.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi establishes a critical rule for the proper use of the term مماثلة. Without this condition, the term would be applied loosely, leading to confusion in theological discourse. The text uses practical examples to illustrate the point and then extends it to the discussion of accidents in bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm, then read the full translation. Note the central condition mentioned at the start.
2
Identify the condition for مماثلة
The passage states that equality (استواء) in the aspect of participation is a condition. Mark the phrase that states this.
Look for 'غير أن الاستواء في الجهة التي تقع بها المشاركة شرط'.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives examples from sciences and crafts. Notice the contrast between cases where equality exists and where it does not. This clarifies the condition.
The example shows that even shared knowledge does not guarantee مماثلة unless there is actual equality.
4
Apply to the case of bodies and accidents
The passage extends the condition to bodies: two bodies can be similar in one accident and different in another. This shows that مماثلة is aspect-specific.
The concept of 'عرض' (accident) is key—similar accidents cause partial مماثلة.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن الاستواء في الجهة التي تقع بها المشاركة شرط لإطلاق اسم المماثلة، فإن اثنين لو اشتركا في الكلام أو الفقه أو الطب أو غير ذلك من العلوم والصناعات، ولم يكن بينهما في ذلك النوع مساواة ينوب أحدهما مناب صاحبه ويسد مسده، لا يستجيز أحد من أرباب اللسان أن يقول : فلان مثل فلان في علم كذا أو في صفة كذا، وإذا كانت بينهما مساواة يسد أحدهما مسد صاحبه يستجيزون ذلك، فدل أن الأمر على ما بينا، وهذا لأن المماثلة بين صفتين الصفات. تثبت لاستوائهما في جميع ثم مماثلة الأجسام لقيام الأعراض المتماثلة بها، فإذا قام بجسمين عرضان متماثلان وعرضان غير متماثلين كانت بين الجسمين ماثلة بالعرض الأول ومخالفة بالعرض الثاني.
Rather, equality in the aspect by which participation occurs is a condition for applying the term مماثلة. For if two persons share in speech, jurisprudence, medicine, or other sciences and crafts, and there is no equality between them in that type such that one can take the place of the other and fill his position, none of the people of language would permit saying: So-and-so is like (مثل) so-and-so in such knowledge or such attribute. But if there is between them equality such that one fills the position of the other, they permit that. So it indicates that the matter is as we have explained. And this is because مماثلة between two attributes is established due to their equality in all [aspects?]. Then the similitude of bodies is due to the existence of similar accidents in them. So if in two bodies there exist two similar accidents and two dissimilar accidents, then between the two bodies there is similarity (ماثلة) by the first accident and difference by the second accident.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage establishes a crucial condition for the application of the term مماثلة: equality (مساواة) in the specific aspect of participation. Without such equality, it is not permissible to say one thing is like another in that attribute. The author illustrates with examples from sciences and crafts. He then applies this to bodies: two bodies can be similar in one accident and different in another, showing that مماثلة is aspect-specific. The condition ensures that مماثلة is not used loosely.

Misconception: Any partial similarity is enough to say two things are 'like' each other.
Correction: The passage asserts that مماثلة requires full equality (مساواة) specifically in the aspect of participation—mere sharing of a category is insufficient.

Similarity of Bodies Based on Accidents
Defines how similarity between bodies is determined by shared accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how similarity and difference between bodies are defined in kalam based on the accidents that inhere in them.

Core Idea
Similarity between bodies is not absolute but depends on shared accidents.

In kalam, bodies are not just substances; they are characterized by accidents. To compare two bodies, scholars look at the accidents that inhere in them. This lesson introduces the foundational principle: similarity and difference are determined by the accidents, not the substances themselves. The passage explains this with a clear conditional example.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms مماثلة (similarity), أعراض (accidents), تماثل (similar), and مخالفة (difference). Understand their meanings in context.
3
Understand the conditional statement
The passage gives a conditional: if two bodies have two similar accidents and two dissimilar accidents, then they are similar by the first and different by the second. This shows relativity.
Similarity and difference are not mutually exclusive; they can coexist from different angles.
4
Apply the concept
Think of two objects, e.g., a red apple and a red ball. They share the accident of color (red) but differ in shape. Apply the principle.
5
Test with checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مماثلة الأجسام لقيام الأعراض المتماثلة بها، فإذا قام بجسمين عرضان متماثلان وعرضان غير متماثلين كانت بين الجسمين ماثلة بالعرض الأول ومخالفة بالعرض الثاني.
Then the similarity of bodies is due to the occurrence of similar accidents in them. So if there occur in two bodies two similar accidents and two dissimilar accidents, then there is between the two bodies similarity by the first accident and difference by the second accident.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines how similarity (مماثلة) and difference (مخالفة) are established between bodies. The author states that similarity of bodies is based on the occurrence of similar accidents (أعراض متماثلة) in them. He then gives an example: if two bodies possess two similar accidents and two dissimilar accidents, then they are similar with respect to the first accident and different with respect to the second. This shows that similarity and difference are not absolute but relative to specific attributes.

Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini on Absolute and Partial Similarity
Al-Isfarayini defines absolute vs partial similarity and author affirms it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why absolute similarity requires complete sharing in every aspect, and how partial similarity allows for both similarity and difference to coexist.

Core Idea
Absolute similarity demands total overlap; partial similarity allows for both likeness and difference.

This lesson introduces a crucial distinction in kalam: absolute versus partial similarity. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini refines the concept of similarity, arguing that two things can be similar in one aspect and different in another. This nuanced view is foundational for theological reasoning about divine attributes and created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the flow from al-Isfarayini's view to the author's commentary.
The passage is dense; a first reading helps orient you.
2
Understand the key distinction
Note how al-Isfarayini defines absolute similarity (مماثلة مطلقة) as sharing in every aspect, and contrast with partial sharing (اشتراك بوجه). He says partial sharing does not make things absolutely similar or absolutely different.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
See the author's agreement
The author (Nasafi) explicitly agrees with al-Isfarayini, then references other works where different Ash'ari views are expressed.
4
Analyze the substitution criterion
The Ash'aris define similars as things where each can take the place of the other. Nasafi adds that substitution can be partial, so things can be similars in one aspect but not another.
This nuance allows for both similarity and difference.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for theological debates: it prevents overgeneralizing from partial similarity to absolute similarity, which is relevant for discussions about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى مثل هذا ذهب أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني فإنه ذكر وجميع في كتابه المسمى بشرح الاعتقاد أن عند الاشتراك بوجه لا يسميان مثلين على الإطلاق، ولا بالمخالفة بوجه يسميان بمختلفين على الإطلاق، وإنما تثبت المماثلة المطلقة عند الاشتراك في كل الوجه. ونحن أيضا هكذا نقول هذا هو المذكور في كتاب شرح الاعتقاد فأما المذكور في كتاب تعجيز المعتزلة، وكتاب ترتيب المذهب، فعلى ما ذكره غيره من الأشعرية، وما يقوله الأشعرية إنا سبرنا الأحوال فوجدنا المتماثلين يتماثلان بما ذكرنا، نحن نساعدهم أنهما كانا مثلين ليسد كل واحد منهما مسد صاحبه إلا أنا نقول : قد يسد أحد الشيئين مسد الأخر بجهة، ولا يسد بجهة فكانا مثلين من جهة دون جهة.
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini went to something like this, for he mentioned in his book called Sharh al-I'tiqad that when there is sharing in one aspect, they are not called 'two similars' absolutely, nor by difference in one aspect are they called 'two different things' absolutely. Rather, absolute similarity is established only when there is sharing in every aspect. And we also say thus. This is what is mentioned in Sharh al-I'tiqad. As for what is mentioned in the book Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila and the book Tartib al-Madhhab, it is according to what other Ash'aris mentioned. And what the Ash'aris say: we have investigated the states and found that two similar things are similar by what we mentioned. We assist them that they are such that each one takes the place of the other, except that we say: one thing may take the place of the other in one aspect and not take its place in another aspect, so they are similars in one aspect without another.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini on the definition of similarity. He states that partial sharing (اشتراك بوجه) does not warrant calling two things 'absolute similars' nor does partial difference make them 'absolute differents'. Absolute similarity (مماثلة مطلقة) requires sharing in every aspect. The author (Nasafi) affirms this position. He then contrasts this with other works of the Ash'aris. The Ash'aris argue that two similar things are such that each can substitute for the other. Nasafi responds that substitution can occur in one aspect but not another, hence they are similars in one aspect only. This nuanced view allows for both similarity and difference to coexist.

اشتراك
مثلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that any similarity means absolute similarity.
Correction: Absolute similarity requires sharing in every aspect; partial similarity only indicates likeness in some respects, not total identity.

Checkpoint

A) When there is sharing in one aspect
B) When there is difference in one aspect
C) When there is sharing in every aspect
D) When there is neither similarity nor difference

Key Takeaway
Absolute similarity requires sharing in every aspect; partial sharing yields only partial similarity, allowing for both likeness and difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Compatibility of Similarity and Difference
Response to objection that similarity and difference are incompatible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how opposite relational attributes can coexist in the same subject when considered from different aspects.

Core Idea
Opposite relational attributes can coexist if the aspects differ.

This lesson responds to the objection that similarity and difference are mutually exclusive. The author demonstrates that these are relational attributes that apply from different perspectives, using examples to show that no contradiction occurs when aspects differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that the objection is based on a misunderstanding of relational attributes.
2
Understand correlatives
Learn that similarity and difference are correlatives (mutadha'ifat) that require two things to exist.
Correlatives are defined in relation to each other.
3
Examine the father/son example
Analyze how a person can be both a father and a son without contradiction because the relations are to different people.
This illustrates the principle of differing aspects.
4
Apply to similarity and difference
Apply the same principle: two things can be similar in one attribute and different in another without conflict.
The key is that the aspects are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا من ثبوت المخالفة بينهما بجهة، وذلك مانع من ثبوت المماثلة لمنافاة بينهما. :قلنا هذا اعتراض على إجماع العقلاء على ما قررت. ثم نقول : المخالفة والمماثلة لن يتصور ثبوتهما إلا بشيئين فكانا من قبيل المتضايفات، ولا استحالة في اجتماع الوصفين المتنافيين من الإضافيات في محل عند اختلاف الجهات وإنما الاستحالة في جهة واحدة فإن شخصا واحدا يكون بالإضافة إلى شخص آخر ابنا (وبالإضافة إلى شخص آخر أبا ) ولا منافاة بينهما عند اختلاف الجهة، إنما المنافاة عند اتحاد الجهة فيستحيل أن يكون الشخص الواحد (أبا لشخص) ابنا لذلك الشخص، وكذا القرب والبعد فإن الشخص الواحد يكون قريبا من شخص بعيدا
And what they said: the establishment of difference between them in one aspect prevents the establishment of similarity due to conflict between them. We said: This is an objection against the consensus of the rational people based on what we established. Then we say: Difference and similarity cannot be conceived to exist except between two things, so they are of the category of correlatives (mutadha'ifat). There is no impossibility in the conjunction of two conflicting descriptions among relational attributes in one subject when the aspects differ. Impossibility is only in one aspect. For example, one person can be a son relative to another person and a father relative to another person, and there is no conflict between them when the aspect differs. Conflict is only when the aspect is unified: it is impossible for one person to be a father of a person and a son of that same person. Similarly, nearness and distance: one person can be near to one person and far from another.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that if two things are different in one aspect, that prevents them from being similar in any aspect, because similarity and difference are contradictory. The author (Nasafi) responds that this objection contradicts the consensus of rational people. He then gives a logical argument: similarity (مخالفة) and difference (مماثلة) are relational attributes (إضافيات) that only exist between two things. They are correlatives (متضايفات). There is no impossibility in two seemingly opposite relational attributes applying to the same subject if the aspects differ. For example, one person can be both a son (relative to his father) and a father (relative to his son) without contradiction. Similarly, one person can be near to one and far from another. Thus, two bodies can be similar in one attribute and different in another without conflict. The key is that the aspects (جهات) are different.

Key Terms
مخالفة
مماثلة
متضايفات
إضافيات
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things differ in one aspect, they cannot be similar in any aspect because similarity and difference are contradictory.
Correction: Similarity and difference are relational attributes that can coexist in the same subject when considered from different aspects, as shown by the father/son analogy.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that opposite relational attributes can coexist?
A) Hot and cold
B) Father and son (relative to different people)
C) Light and heavy
D) Wet and dry

Key Takeaway
Similarity and difference are relational and can coexist when the aspects are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that seeming contradictions in attributes can be resolved when aspects differ. In your own life, where have you been too quick to judge someone as 'hypocritical' without considering the different relationships or contexts? How can you apply this principle to extend grace instead of condemnation?
?
You claim to believe in Allah's mercy and justice, yet sometimes you doubt how both can be true at once. After learning that opposite relational attributes can coexist when aspects differ, how does this reshape your trust in Allah's wisdom? Will you commit to not letting intellectual confusion weaken your reliance on Him?
?
Consider a relationship where you have both love and frustration toward the same person. This lesson says these opposites can coexist if the aspects differ. Are you willing to admit that your own heart is not simple, and that this complexity does not make you a contradiction? How does this truth free you from the lie that you must be 'all good' or 'all bad' to be acceptable?

Why God Is Called a Thing: Refuting Jahm ibn Safwan
The author refutes Jahm's denial that God is called a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Jahm ibn Safwan's denial that God is called a 'thing' is false, and how sharing a name does not imply similarity.

Core Idea
Sharing a name does not create similarity.

Jahm ibn Safwan argued that calling God a 'thing' would imply similarity between God and creation. In this passage, the author refutes this by invoking the earlier discussion on likeness: merely sharing the term 'thing' does not establish any real similarity. This lesson unpacks that refutation and clarifies a key principle in theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author connects the refutation to the earlier discussion on likeness (mumāthala).
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; the missing part likely gives an example.
2
Identify Jahm's claim
Jahm argued that if God were a 'thing' and other things exist, similarity would occur. Understand his reasoning: he assumed that sharing the name implies sharing attributes.
3
Grasp the counter-argument
The author counters that likeness is not established by mere sharing of the name 'thing'. The earlier definition of likeness (mumāthala) requires more than a common label.
Think about other examples: two very different objects can both be called 'thing' without being similar.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this principle applies to other theological terms. Many attributes (like 'existing', 'knowing') are shared between God and creation without implying similarity.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology to avoid anthropomorphism while affirming divine attributes.

God as Thing: Meaning and Uniqueness
Nasafi proves that 'thing' and 'existent' are synonymous and that God's necessary existence makes Him unlike all others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why calling God a 'thing' does not imply similarity with created things, and how the synonymy of 'thing' and 'existent' is central to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
To call God a 'thing' is to affirm His existence, not to compare Him to creation.

Nasafi begins by clarifying a foundational term in Islamic theology: 'thing' (shay'). He argues that 'thing' and 'existent' are synonymous, and that God is a thing by His essence, not by another. This sets the stage for understanding God's absolute uniqueness despite sharing the label 'existing' with creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once without over-analyzing. Notice the initial analogy about two opposite things and the claim that 'thing' and 'existent' mean the same.
The passage begins with an argument from opposites to illustrate a point about similarity.
2
Identify the synonymy statement
Find the sentence where Nasafi says 'thing' and 'existent' are two expressions for one meaning. This is crucial.
3
Understand the distinction in existence
Nasafi distinguishes God's necessary existence from all contingent existence. Note how this prevents any real similarity.
Similarity requires equality in an attribute; God's necessary existence is unique.
4
Reflect on the condition for similarity
The passage states that similarity occurs by one attribute, but only if there is equality in that attribute. Apply this to God.
No one shares God's necessary existence, so He is not similar to anything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع البياض شيئان ولا مماثلة بينهما مع أن كل واحد منهما موجود بإيجاد الغير، وبإيجاد موجده صار شيئا، والله تعالى لم يكن شيئا بغيرهما بل كان لذاته شيئا في الأزل ثم لما لم يكن شيئا يثبت شيئيتهما بغيرها متماثلين وهما المتضادان، لانعدام أن يسد أحدهما مسد صاحبه فلأن لا يكون شيء لم تكن شيئيته بغيره مثلا لشيء ثبتت شيئيته بغيره مع أن أحدهما لا يسد مسد صاحبه أولا. نحققه أن الشيء والموجود لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد ثم إن الله تعالى واجب الوجود لذاته وما سواه جائز الوجود فلم يساو الله تعالى موجود سواه من وجوب الوجود، فلم يكن مثلا له لانعدام التساوي من جهة الوجود، وقد بينا في خلال كلامنا أن المماثلة تقع بوصف واحد، ولكن بشريطة المساواة في ذلك الوصف لما قررنا، ولا مساواة لأحد مع الله تعالى في وجوب الوجود فلا يكون مثلا له، وإن كان موجودا. ثم نقول له: إنك لما امتنعت عن قولك هو شيء أتقول أنه لا شيء؟ فإن قال: نعم. قد انسلخ عن الدين، لأن قول الناس لا شيء إخبار عن العدم بحقيقته وعن سقوط القدر بمجازه لالتحاق ما هو ساقط القدر عديم النفع بالمعدوم إذ المعدوم لا قدر له ولا ينتفع به. وإن قال لا أقول ذلك. فقد أثبت بين السلب والإيجاب واسطة وبطلانه يعرف بالبديهة. والله الموفق
With the whiteness [are?] two things, and there is no likeness between them, despite each of them being existent by the bringing into existence of another, and by the bringing into existence of its bringer it became a thing. And God Almighty was not a thing by another besides them, but rather He was by His essence a thing in pre-eternity. Then when it is not a thing whose thingness is established by another, they are two similar things, and they are the two opposites, because of the impossibility that one of them could take the place of the other. So it is even more so that a thing whose thingness was not by another is not similar to a thing whose thingness was established by another, despite the fact that one of them does not take the place of the other at all. We verify that 'thing' and 'existent' are two expressions that indicate one meaning. Then God Almighty is necessary of existence by His essence, and whatever is other than Him is contingent of existence, so God Almighty did not equal any existent other than Him in necessity of existence. So He was not similar to it due to the lack of equality in the aspect of existence. And we have explained in the course of our speech that similarity occurs by one attribute, but with the condition of equality in that attribute as we established. And there is no equality of anyone with God Almighty in the necessity of existence, so He is not similar to Him, even if He is existent. Then we say to him: Since you refrained from saying that He is a thing, do you say that He is not a thing? If he says: Yes, he has stripped himself of the religion, because the saying of people 'not a thing' is an informer about non-existence in its reality and about the fall of worth through its metaphor, due to the attachment of what is of fallen worth, useless, to the non-existent, since the non-existent has no worth and is not benefited from. And if he says: I do not say that, then he has established a middle between negation and affirmation, and its invalidity is known by intuition. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a twofold argument demonstrating that God is a thing (shay') and refuting those who deny it. First, Nasafi explains the relationship between 'thing' and 'existent' (mawjūd), asserting they are synonymous. He then distinguishes God's existence—necessary by essence (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātih)—from all other existents, which are contingent (jā'iz al-wujūd). This absolute uniqueness in the mode of existence precludes any genuine similarity (mumāthala) between God and creatures, even if both are called 'things'. The condition for similarity is equality in a shared attribute; since no one shares God's necessary existence, He cannot be similar to anything. Second, Nasafi poses a dilemma: if one refuses to call God a 'thing', they must either say He is 'not a thing' (i.e., nothing), which is apostasy because 'nothing' indicates pure non-existence, or they must affirm a third state between existence and non-existence, which is intuitively absurd. Thus, calling God a 'thing' is both true and safe from anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
شيء (shay')
موجود (mawjūd)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)
جائز الوجود (jā'iz al-wujūd)
مماثلة (mumāthala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that calling God a 'thing' implies similarity with created things.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'thing' merely indicates existence, and God's necessary existence makes Him absolutely unique, so no similarity follows.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the condition for two things to be considered similar?
A) They must share the same name
B) They must be equal in a shared attribute
C) They must both be existent
D) They must be created

Key Takeaway
God is a 'thing' by essence, but His necessary existence makes Him absolutely unique, so the term 'thing' implies no comparison with creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call God a 'thing' in your speech and prayers. Do you truly believe He is unlike anything you have ever seen or imagined, or does your heart still picture Him with human-like qualities? What concrete step will you take today to purify your concept of God from any likeness?
?
Nasafi says God is 'a thing by His essence in pre-eternity.' When you consider that God existed alone before all creation, does that truth awe you or does it remain an abstract idea? How can you make the reality of God's pre-eternal existence influence a decision you face this week?
?
The passage teaches that similarity requires equality—and no one equals God in necessity of existence. Is there any person, possession, or status that you secretly treat as if your life cannot exist without it? What would it look like for you to truly submit that everything else is contingent and that God alone is necessary?

The Dilemma of Denying God is a Thing
Nasafi poses a dilemma to those who deny God is a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma faced by those who refuse to call God a 'thing', and why their position leads either to apostasy or to absurdity.

Core Idea
Denying that God is a thing forces you to say He is nothing, which is impossible.

Nasafi now turns to refute those who deny God is a thing. He presents a powerful dilemma: either you admit God is 'not a thing' (i.e., nothing), which is apostasy, or you posit a third state between existence and non-existence, which is intuitively false. This argument strengthens the necessity of calling God a thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialogue carefully
Read the passage from 'thumma naqūlu lahu' to the end. Notice that it is a direct argument against an opponent.
2
Identify the two options
Find the two possible answers the opponent can give when asked 'Do you say He is not a thing?'
Option 1: 'yes' (He is not a thing). Option 2: 'no' (I do not say that).
3
Understand the consequence of 'yes'
If the opponent says 'yes', he has said God is 'not a thing', which implies non-existence. Nasafi explains why this is apostasy.
'Not a thing' language is used for non-existent or worthless things.
4
Understand the consequence of 'no'
If the opponent says 'no', he has placed a middle state between affirmation and negation. Nasafi says this is invalid by intuition.
There is no third possibility beyond 'thing' and 'not a thing'.
5
Conclude the necessity of affirming God as a thing
Both options lead to absurdity, so the only sound position is to affirm that God is a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Principle's Self-Contradiction
Nasafi presents and refutes al-Salihi's principle from within.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Salihi's principle that non-opposition to a thing implies non-opposition to its opposite leads to a self-contradiction.

Core Idea
A thing cannot oppose itself, yet the principle implies that.

Al-Salihi proposed a principle: if something does not oppose a thing, it also does not oppose that thing's opposite. This seems plausible but leads to absurdity. In this passage, Nasafi exposes the inherent self-contradiction in the principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Al-Salihi states: what opposes a thing opposes its opposite; what does not oppose a thing does not oppose that thing. Read this carefully.
2
See the application to life and death
He applies it: since knowledge and power do not oppose life, they also do not oppose death, allowing coexistence with death.
3
Grasp the refutation
Nasafi points out that if a thing must oppose the opposite of its opposite, it would end up opposing itself, which is absurd.
This refutes the principle directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يذكره الصالحي من الشبهة أن الأصل أن ما ضاد شيئا ضاد ضده، وما لم يضاد شيئا لا يضاد ذلك الشيء، فإن السواد لما كان يضاد البياض يضاد ما هو ضد البياض من الحمرة والصفرة والخضرة وغيرها والحركة لما لم تكن مضادة للحياة لم تكن مضادة للموت. وإذا كان الأصل هذا، ثم رأينا أن العلم والقدرة لا يضادان الحياة فلا يضادان الموت أيضا، وعند ارتفاع المضادة جاز الاجتماع كلام باطل متناقض ؛ لأن الشيء لو كان يجب أن يضاد ما هو ضد ضده لكان ينبغي أن يكون الشيء مضادا لنفسه، لأنه ضد ضده الذي ضاده، والقول بكون الشيء مضادا لنفسه ظاهر الفساد. ويقال له: بم تنفصل عمن يقول : تقرر في أوائل العقول تضاد العلم والموت، حيث تقرر تضاد الحياة والموت؟ ولو جاز لك أن تقول هذا جاز لغيرك أن يقول : إن الحياة ليست بمضادة للموت إذ لو كانت مضادة للموت لضادت ما هو ضد الموت وهو العلم، وإذا لم تضاد ما هو ضد الموت لا تضاد الموت أيضا، وهذا فاسد فكذا الأول؟
اجتماعهما
ولو قال : أعلم بالضرورة أن الحياة والموت يتضادان. قيل: يعلم خصومك بالضرورة أن العلم والموت يتضادان ( كذا : القدرة والموت فانفصل عنهم، ويقال له: استدلالك بجواز اجتماع العلم والحياة) وكذا القدرة والحياة على جواز مع الموت باطل، إذ ليس كل ما يجوز وجوده مع شيء يجوز وجوده مع ضده. فإن العلم بأن الجسم أسود ، والإخبار عن ذلك جائز الوجود في حال وجود السواد وليسا بجائز في الوجود في حال وجود البياض الذي هو ضد السواد
And what al-Salihi mentions as a specious argument (al-shubha): that the principle is that whatever opposes a thing opposes its opposite, and whatever does not oppose a thing does not oppose that thing. For example, since black opposes white, it opposes what is opposite to white, such as red, yellow, green, etc. And since motion is not opposite to life, it is not opposite to death. If this is the principle, and we see that knowledge and power do not oppose life, then they do not oppose death either, and when opposition is absent, coexistence is permissible. [This is] false and contradictory speech, because if a thing must oppose what is opposite to its opposite, then it would necessarily be that a thing opposes itself, since it is the opposite of its opposite that it opposes. And the statement that a thing opposes itself is clearly corrupt. And it is said to him: How do you separate from one who says: It is established in the first principles of intellects that knowledge and death are opposites, just as it is established that life and death are opposites? If it is permissible for you to say this, it is permissible for another to say: Life is not opposite to death, because if it were opposite to death, it would oppose what is opposite to death, namely knowledge; and if it does not oppose what is opposite to death, it does not oppose death either. This is corrupt, so likewise the first. Their coexistence. And if he says: I know by necessity that life and death are opposites, it is said: Your opponents know by necessity that knowledge and death are opposites (likewise power and death), so differentiate yourself from them. And it is said to him: Your inference from the permissibility of coexistence of knowledge and life, and likewise power and life, to their coexistence with death is invalid, because not everything whose existence with something is permissible is permissible with its opposite. For example, knowledge that a body is black and the report of that are permissible in the state of blackness, but they are not permissible in the state of whiteness, which is the opposite of blackness.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage refutes a specious argument (shubha) attributed to al-Salihi. He proposed a principle: if something opposes a thing, it also opposes the opposite of that thing; if something does not oppose a thing, it does not oppose that thing's opposite either. Applying this, since knowledge and power do not oppose life, they should not oppose death either, so they could coexist with death. The refutation first points out that this principle is self-contradictory: if a thing must oppose the opposite of its opposite, it would end up opposing itself, which is absurd. Next, the author presents a counter-argument: someone could claim that life does not oppose death because if it did, it would oppose death's opposite (knowledge), but it doesn't; thus life and death could coexist—a conclusion no one accepts, showing the principle's invalidity. Then an objector might appeal to necessary knowledge that life and death are opposites. The author replies: your opponents also know by necessity that knowledge and death are opposites, so you must refute them. Finally, the author provides a concrete example: knowledge that a body is black and the statement of that are possible only while blackness exists, not with whiteness. Similarly, knowledge and life coexist, but that does not justify coexistence with death, because not everything possible with a thing is possible with its opposite. Thus al-Salihi's argument fails.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubha)
التضاد (al-tadadd)
الضد (al-didd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-opposition to a thing implies non-opposition to that thing's opposite.
Correction: This is not always true; in fact, it leads to self-contradiction as shown.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Nasafi use to refute al-Salihi's principle?
A) A thing would become its own opposite
B) A thing would oppose itself
C) A thing would have no opposite
D) A thing would oppose all things

Key Takeaway
Al-Salihi's principle fails because it would require a thing to oppose itself, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there principles you hold in your faith or life that, when applied consistently to themselves, would lead to an absurdity you have never considered? What would it cost you to re-examine them honestly?
?
When have you accepted a rule of thumb because it worked in familiar cases, but failed to test it on the most fundamental case—yourself? What is one such rule you need to scrutinize today?
?
Nasafi’s refutation is sharp and definitive. Where in your own thinking have you allowed a comfortable falsehood to stand because exposing it requires dismantling something you prefer? What would it take to face that self-contradiction with the same courage?

Further Refutations and the Black-White Example
Nasafi continues refuting al-Salihi with additional arguments and a concrete example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional arguments Nasafi uses to refute al-Salihi, including the appeal to necessary knowledge and the counterexample of black and white.

Core Idea
Not everything permissible with a thing is permissible with its opposite.

After showing the principle's self-contradiction, Nasafi addresses potential objections. He shows that the same principle could be used to argue that life and death are not opposites, which is absurd, and provides a concrete example from black and white to illustrate the fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-argument
Nasafi imagines someone using the principle against al-Salihi: if life is opposite to death, then it should oppose death's opposite (knowledge), but life does not oppose knowledge, so life must not oppose death. This is absurd.
2
Examine the necessary knowledge claim
Al-Salihi might claim necessary knowledge that life and death are opposites. But opponents can claim the same for knowledge and death. This creates a standoff.
3
Analyze the black and white example
Nasafi gives an example: knowledge that a body is black and the report of it are possible only when black exists, not when white exists. This shows that coexistence with one thing does not imply coexistence with its opposite.
This directly refutes al-Salihi's inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يذكره الصالحي من الشبهة أن الأصل أن ما ضاد شيئا ضاد ضده، وما لم يضاد شيئا لا يضاد ذلك الشيء، فإن السواد لما كان يضاد البياض يضاد ما هو ضد البياض من الحمرة والصفرة والخضرة وغيرها والحركة لما لم تكن مضادة للحياة لم تكن مضادة للموت. وإذا كان الأصل هذا، ثم رأينا أن العلم والقدرة لا يضادان الحياة فلا يضادان الموت أيضا، وعند ارتفاع المضادة جاز الاجتماع كلام باطل متناقض ؛ لأن الشيء لو كان يجب أن يضاد ما هو ضد ضده لكان ينبغي أن يكون الشيء مضادا لنفسه، لأنه ضد ضده الذي ضاده، والقول بكون الشيء مضادا لنفسه ظاهر الفساد. ويقال له: بم تنفصل عمن يقول : تقرر في أوائل العقول تضاد العلم والموت، حيث تقرر تضاد الحياة والموت؟ ولو جاز لك أن تقول هذا جاز لغيرك أن يقول : إن الحياة ليست بمضادة للموت إذ لو كانت مضادة للموت لضادت ما هو ضد الموت وهو العلم، وإذا لم تضاد ما هو ضد الموت لا تضاد الموت أيضا، وهذا فاسد فكذا الأول؟
اجتماعهما
ولو قال : أعلم بالضرورة أن الحياة والموت يتضادان. قيل: يعلم خصومك بالضرورة أن العلم والموت يتضادان ( كذا : القدرة والموت فانفصل عنهم، ويقال له: استدلالك بجواز اجتماع العلم والحياة) وكذا القدرة والحياة على جواز مع الموت باطل، إذ ليس كل ما يجوز وجوده مع شيء يجوز وجوده مع ضده. فإن العلم بأن الجسم أسود ، والإخبار عن ذلك جائز الوجود في حال وجود السواد وليسا بجائز في الوجود في حال وجود البياض الذي هو ضد السواد
And what al-Salihi mentions as a specious argument (al-shubha): that the principle is that whatever opposes a thing opposes its opposite, and whatever does not oppose a thing does not oppose that thing. For example, since black opposes white, it opposes what is opposite to white, such as red, yellow, green, etc. And since motion is not opposite to life, it is not opposite to death. If this is the principle, and we see that knowledge and power do not oppose life, then they do not oppose death either, and when opposition is absent, coexistence is permissible. [This is] false and contradictory speech, because if a thing must oppose what is opposite to its opposite, then it would necessarily be that a thing opposes itself, since it is the opposite of its opposite that it opposes. And the statement that a thing opposes itself is clearly corrupt. And it is said to him: How do you separate from one who says: It is established in the first principles of intellects that knowledge and death are opposites, just as it is established that life and death are opposites? If it is permissible for you to say this, it is permissible for another to say: Life is not opposite to death, because if it were opposite to death, it would oppose what is opposite to death, namely knowledge; and if it does not oppose what is opposite to death, it does not oppose death either. This is corrupt, so likewise the first. Their coexistence. And if he says: I know by necessity that life and death are opposites, it is said: Your opponents know by necessity that knowledge and death are opposites (likewise power and death), so differentiate yourself from them. And it is said to him: Your inference from the permissibility of coexistence of knowledge and life, and likewise power and life, to their coexistence with death is invalid, because not everything whose existence with something is permissible is permissible with its opposite. For example, knowledge that a body is black and the report of that are permissible in the state of blackness, but they are not permissible in the state of whiteness, which is the opposite of blackness.

Commentary
This passage refutes a specious argument (shubha) attributed to al-Salihi.
