The Relational Nature of 'Qadim'
Al-Nasafi explains the linguistic classification of 'qadim' as an idafi name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'qadim' is classified as a relational (idafi) name and how it differs from names based on positive meanings such as 'alim'.

Core Idea
'Qadim' is a relational term, not a positive attribute.

This passage from al-Nasafi delves into the linguistic nature of the term 'qadim' (eternal). It may seem straightforward, but the author argues that it is actually a relational (idafi) name, similar to 'father' or 'precedent'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly grasping how divine attributes are described.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument settle.
Notice the examples: 'this building is ancient' and the proverb 'evil is ancient'.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Nasafi states that 'qadim' is from the category of relational names. Find the sentence that makes this clear.
Look for: 'فهذا الاسم من قبيل الأسماء الإضافية'
3
Understand the mutual implication
The passage explains that 'qadim' can be applied to something that is both precedent and subsequent relative to different things. Consider the example of Zayd.
This shows that 'qadim' does not denote a fixed absolute property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوده وجود غيره، كان هو بمقابلته قديما. ولهذا يقال : هذا بناء قديم، وشيخ قديم، ويقال في المثل السائر : الشر قديم فكان هذا الاسم من قبيل الأسماء الإضافية الثابتة باعتبار مقابلة الغير هذا هو حقيقة اللغة، فإنه مأخوذ من التقدم، وهو متعلق بغيره، ألا ترى أنه يتصف بالمتقابلين جميعا، فيقال: زيد متقدم على عمرو ومتأخر عن عبد الله كما هو خاصية المتضايقين، فيكون إثباتا لوجوده في وقت لم يوجد فيه ما يضايفه، فيكون من بابا الإضافة، فلا يكون مقتضيا معنى. غير أن الاسم لا يستحق إلا باعتبار ذلك الغير، وبالنفي ينتفي المقابل لا ذاته، كما يقال زيد ليس بأب ، لا يكون هذا نفيا لذاته بل نفيا لما يقابله الذي لأجله يستحق اسم الأب وهو الابن، وهذا هو حقيقة هذا الاسم لغة، ثم يستعمل فيما هو المتقدم في الوجود على كل محدث، وعلى هذا تسمى الصفات أيضا قدماء، واسم العالم ليس من قبيل هذا الاسم، لكونه من باب الأسامي المأخوذة عن المعاني دون المتضايفات، فمن اعتبر بعض هذه الأسامي بالبعض، ولم يهتد إلى حقيقة كل اسم، وخاصية كل قسم منها، فهو قليل الحظ من المعرفة بالحقائق.
His existence (is) existence of another, he is by his being opposite to it ancient. And for this it is said: this building is ancient, and an old sheikh, and it is said in the common proverb: Evil is ancient. So this name is of the category of relational names established by consideration of opposition to another. This is the reality of language, for it is derived from precedence, and it is related to another. Do you not see that it is described by both opposites? It is said: Zayd is precedent to Amr and subsequent to Abdullah, as is the characteristic of mutually implying terms. So it is an affirmation of his existence at a time when that which implies him did not exist. So it is from the category of relation, so it does not entail a meaning. However, the name is only deserved by consideration of that other, and by negation the opposite is negated, not its essence. Just as it is said: Zayd is not a father; this is not a negation of his essence but a negation of what is opposite to him, for which he deserves the name 'father', namely the son. And this is the reality of this name linguistically. Then it is used for that which is prior in existence to every originated thing. And on this basis, the attributes are also called 'ancient'. And the name 'Knowing' ('alim) is not of this category, because it is from the category of names taken from meanings, not from mutually implying terms. So whoever considers some of these names by others and does not attain the reality of each name and the specific characteristic of each type, he has little share of knowledge of realities.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic nature of the term 'qadim' (ancient/eternal) as a relative (idafi) name. Al-Nasafi explains that 'qadim' is derived from precedence and implies a relation to something else. It is a mutual implication: something can be described as both 'qadim' and 'hadith' (new) relative to different things, like Zayd being precedent to one person and subsequent to another. The term affirms existence at a time when the correlative did not exist. Therefore, saying something is 'qadim' does not affirm a positive attribute in itself but rather a relation. Negation of 'qadim' negates the relation, not the essence. Therefore, the attributes of God are called 'qadim' (eternal) because they are prior to all originated things. However, the name 'alim' (Knowing) is not relative; it is based on the meaning of knowledge itself. Al-Nasafi concludes that those who confuse these types of names lack true understanding.

Key Terms
قديم
إضافة
متضايفان
اسم إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Qadim' implies a positive attribute of eternal existence separate from any relation.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that 'qadim' is relational; it indicates precedence relative to another, not an absolute property.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what kind of name is 'qadim'?
A) A name based on a positive meaning
B) A relational name
C) A name that negates the existence of the object
D) A name that implies both precedence and subsequence simultaneously

Key Takeaway
'Qadim' is a relational name indicating precedence, not a positive attribute of eternal existence per se.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The negation of a relational name does not negate the essence. When you negate something about Allah (e.g., He is not unjust), are you affirming His perfection or just denying a lack? How can you ensure your tanzih (negation) is rooted in proper understanding?

Conditions for Valid Exhaustive Division
Critique of the logical conditions for valid exhaustive division (istiqra').

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two valid methods for exhaustive division (istiqra') and why it is invalid when all options are equally impossible.

Core Idea
Exhaustive division requires the chosen option to have a distinction—either possibility or necessity, not equal impossibility.

This passage critiques a flawed reasoning method used by Abu Hashim. The author lays out the proper logical conditions for eliminating options in an exhaustive division. Only when the remaining option has a special status—either possible or necessary—can the division be valid. Without that, the reasoning is arbitrary and ignorant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that the passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim's method of division. It begins by attributing his error to ignorance of the condition for valid istiqra'.
Abu Hashim argued for the existence of 'states' (ahwal) by eliminating two other possibilities.
2
Learn the two valid conditions
Read the section where the author explains the two valid ways to specify one division: either the other divisions are impossible (making the chosen one possible), or the other divisions are not necessary (making the chosen one necessary).
These are the only two acceptable logical grounds for preference.
3
Identify the fallacy
When all divisions are equally impossible, choosing one arbitrarily is pure ignorance. The author accuses Abu Hashim of doing exactly that: he eliminated two options as impossible without proving the third (states) is possible.
The refutation targets the lack of proof for possibility.
4
Reflect on the critique
The author points out that even if we grant the impossibility of the two options, Abu Hashim still needed to demonstrate the possibility of the third. He did not.
The text ends mid-sentence, but the logical point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن هذا النوع من التعليل صادر عن الجهل بشرط صحة الاستقراء وبيانه: أن شرط صحة الاستقراء وتعين بعض الأقسام لتعليق الحكم به أو للحكم بصحته، أن يكون لهذا القسم على غيره من الأقسام مزية وخصوصيته، إما بالإمكان إن كانت الأقسام الأخر ممتنعة بأن يقال: هذا يتنوع إلى كذا وكذا وكذا، فالأول ممتنع أن يدون أو يتعلق به الحكم، وكذا الثاني فتعين الثالث ضرورة، لإمكان ثبوته أو كونه علة للحكم. وإما بالوجوب بأن يقال: هذا يتنوع إلى كذا وكذا وكذا ولا وجوب للأول والثاني، والثالث واجب أو واجب أن يكون علة، فأما إذا استوت الأقسام كلها في الامتناع فتعين البعض للثبوت أو تعليق الحكم به، وإبطال الأقسام الأخر مع مساواتها الأول في المعنى جهل محض. وهو في هذا الكلام أبطل قسمين تصور عنده امتناعهما وعين الثالث، وهو القول بالأحوال من غير بيان إمكانه، بل امتناع ما ذكر من القسمين لو ساعدناه وجرينا معه على طريقة
Indeed, this type of reasoning arises from ignorance of the condition for the validity of exhaustive division (istiqra'). Its explanation: The condition for the validity of exhaustive division and for specifying one of the divisions to which a ruling is attached or to judge its validity, is that this division has a distinction and specificity over the other divisions—either through possibility, if the other divisions are impossible, such that it is said: 'This divides into such-and-such, such-and-such, and such-and-such. The first is impossible that it be [valid] or that the ruling attaches to it; likewise the second; so the third is determined necessarily, because of the possibility of its existence or its being a cause for the ruling.' Or through necessity: it is said: 'This divides into such-and-such, such-and-such, and such-and-such; there is no necessity for the first and second; the third is necessary or necessarily the cause.' But if all divisions are equal in impossibility, then specifying one for affirmation or attaching the ruling to it, and invalidating the other divisions despite their equality with the first in meaning, is pure ignorance. And he in this speech invalidated two divisions that he imagined to be impossible, and specified the third—namely the doctrine of states—without demonstrating its possibility; indeed [even if] the impossibility of the two mentioned divisions [were conceded], if we were to assist him and proceed with him according to his method...
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim's reasoning method. The author explains the proper conditions for a valid exhaustive division (istiqra') when determining a ruling. There are two valid methods: either the other divisions are impossible, so the remaining one is determined by possibility; or the other divisions are not necessary, and the remaining one is necessary. But if all divisions are equally impossible, then singling out one is arbitrary and ignorant. The author accuses Abu Hashim of doing exactly that: he eliminated two possibilities (reference to essence or meaning) as impossible, and then affirmed the third (states) without showing its possibility. The refutation is incomplete in the text, but it clearly criticizes the logical fallacy.

Key Terms
استقراء
قسم
امتناع
وجوب
إمكان
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simply eliminating two options (e.g., essence and meaning) is sufficient to affirm the third (states) without further evidence.
Correction: The author shows that elimination is only valid if the remaining option has a logical distinction (possibility or necessity). If all options are equally impossible, elimination is arbitrary and does not prove the third's existence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the condition for valid exhaustive division when the other divisions are impossible?
A) The remaining division must be necessary.
B) The remaining division must be possible.
C) All divisions must be equal.
D) The remaining division must be unknown.

Key Takeaway
Exhaustive division is only valid when the chosen option has a logical distinction—either it is possible while others are impossible, or it is necessary while others are not.

?
This passage shows a great scholar being critiqued for flawed logic. Are you willing to subject your most cherished intellectual arguments to the same scrutiny? What would it cost you to be wrong?

The Inconsistency of Mu'tazilī States: Willing and Knowing
Al-Nasafi presents the second contradiction regarding states of willing and speaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi exposes a logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazilī theory of states by showing that their criterion for admitting states is applied unevenly.

Core Idea
If states for willing need no cause, why not for knowing? Inconsistency.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a powerful dialectical argument against the Mu'tazilī theory of states. He shows that if they allow states for willing and speaking without a separate cause, they must also allow states for knowing and being powerful—which they reject. This reveals a fundamental inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the conditional structure.
The argument is tightly packed; take your time.
2
Identify the contradiction
Al-Nasafi sets up an equivalence between the witnessed and unseen. Find the specific states he mentions.
He mentions willing and speaking as allowed without cause.
3
Follow the logical implication
If the opponent accepts states A and B without cause, they must accept states C, D, etc. (knowing, powerful, etc.) without cause. But they do not.
The phrase 'إلا بإثبات ما يقتضيها' indicates they require a cause for those.
4
Understand the challenge
Al-Nasafi concludes that the opponent has no way to differentiate between the two sets without contradicting themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمناقضة الثانية: أنك لم تجوز إثبات الحال التي بها يصير الذات مريدا في الغائب بدون ما يقتضيها وهو الإرادة، وكذا الحال التي يصير بها متكلما في الغائب بدون الكلام، تسوية بين الشاهد والغائب، فلا يجوز أيضا إثبات الحال التي يصير بها الذات عالما، والحال التي يصير بها قادرا، وكذا الحال التي يصير بها باقيا سميعا بصيرا، إلا بإثبات ما يقتضيها، وإلا فالفرق وليس لك إلى الفرق سبيل. وجوز أيضا في الغائب الحال التي بها يصير الذات متحركا أو ساكنا، وكذا في جميع الأكوان والألوان والطعوم والروائح، من غير ثبوت ما يقتضيها، وإلا فافرق. وهذا الكلام يغني سماعه عن الاشتغال برده، ولولا اتباع أكثر معتزلة زماننا رأي هذا الملحد في ضلالاته، وإلا لما أشبعنا في إفساد هذا الكلام كل هذا الإشباع، لاستغنائنا عن ذلك بشهادة البداءة ببطلانه والتحاق قائله بالمتجاهلة. والله الموفق.
The second contradiction: You did not permit establishing the state (al-ḥāl) by which the essence becomes willing in the unseen without what necessitates it, namely will (al-irāda), and likewise the state by which it becomes speaking in the unseen without speech (al-kalām), as an equivalence between the witnessed and the unseen. So you also may not permit establishing the state by which the essence becomes knowing, and the state by which it becomes powerful, and likewise the state by which it becomes everlasting, hearing, seeing, except by establishing what necessitates them; otherwise, there is a difference (al-farq), and you have no way to the difference. And you also permitted in the unseen the state by which the essence becomes moving or stationary, and likewise in all the accidents (al-akwān), colors, tastes, and smells, without the existence of what necessitates them; otherwise, differentiate. And this speech renders listening to it sufficient from occupying oneself with refuting it, and were it not for the following of most Mu'tazila of our time to the opinion of this heretic in his errors, otherwise we would not have exhausted ourselves in corrupting this speech with all this exhaustiveness, because we are independent of that by the testimony of the beginning of its falsehood and the affiliation of its speaker with those who pretend ignorance. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the Mu'tazilī theory of states (aḥwāl). It is a dialectical objection: al-Nasafi argues that if the opponent allows states for willing and speaking in the unseen without postulating a separate cause (since they claim the state is the very being of the essence in a certain mode), then they must also allow states for knowing, being powerful, etc., without a cause, but they do not. He then points out that they also allow states for movement, rest, colors, etc., without a cause. This inconsistency exposes the fallacy of the theory. The author then dismisses the argument as obviously false, stating that he only bothers to refute it because many Mu'tazila follow this heretic's opinion. Otherwise, it is self-evidently false.

Key Terms
حال (ḥāl)
مناقضة (munāqaḍa)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā'ib)
إرادة (irāda)
كلام (kalām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether states exist at all.
Correction: The argument is not about the existence of states but about the inconsistent criterion for admitting them.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi identify as the inconsistency in the Mu'tazilī theory of states?
A) They permit states for willing and speaking without cause but not for knowing and being powerful.
B) They deny states altogether.
C) They require a cause for all states.
D) They claim that Allah has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilī theory of states fails because it applies its criterion unevenly, allowing some states without cause while requiring causes for others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a theological position, do you apply the same standard of proof to every aspect of God's attributes, or do you make exceptions without realizing it? Reflect on where you might be inconsistent and what that says about your attachment to a school of thought over truth.
?
If someone pointed out an inconsistency in your reasoning about God, would you be willing to abandon your position, or would you cling to it out of partisanship? This lesson exposes the Mu'tazilī's inconsistency—what do you fear more: being wrong or being inconsistent?
?
Consider one belief you hold about God that you have not critically examined. How would you feel if it were shown to be contradictory? Would you change your view, or would you rationalize to keep it? Your answer reveals your priority: intellectual integrity or comfort.

Extending the Inconsistency to Accidents and the Dismissive Conclusion
Al-Nasafi extends the contradiction to accidental states and dismisses the theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi extends his critique to states of accidents and why he considers the argument self-evidently false.

Core Idea
If states for moving need no cause, the theory collapses—but it's obviously false anyway.

Having exposed a core inconsistency, al-Nasafi now applies the same reasoning to accidental states like movement and color. He then dismisses the entire theory as obviously false, only bothering to refute it because many Mu'tazila follow this erroneous view. This final part underscores the perceived weakness of the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the extension to accidental states.
2
Note the extension
Al-Nasafi points out that the opponent allows states for movement, rest, colors, tastes, etc., without requiring a cause.
This is the same pattern as before.
3
Understand the rhetorical dismissal
Al-Nasafi declares that the argument is so absurd that just hearing it is enough. He only refutes it because many Mu'tazila follow this heretic's opinion.
The term 'al-mutajāhila' refers to those who pretend ignorance.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why al-Nasafi still spends time on an argument he considers self-evidently false.
It shows the importance of refuting widespread errors.

?

The Mu'tazilite Definition and Its Refutation
Refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition using two counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite definition of two distinct things as 'differing in description' is invalid both on their own principles and universally.

Core Idea
Two things can be distinct without differing in description.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by presenting the Mu'tazilite definition of al-ghayran, the two distinct things. He then shows its internal inconsistency within Mu'tazilism by citing their own doctrine of two blacknesses in one locus, and provides a universal counterexample using the one from ten and the hand from a human. This lesson establishes the first layer of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the Mu'tazilite definition of al-ghayran and note the two refutations that follow.
The definition is at the very beginning.
2
Understand the internal refutation
The author points out that the Mu'tazila allow two blacknesses to coexist in one locus, which are distinct yet have no difference in description. This contradicts their own definition.
This refutes the definition from within their school.
3
Understand the universal refutation
The example of the one from ten and the hand from a human shows that difference in description does not imply distinctness. The one and ten differ in description but the one is not other than the ten.
This refutes the definition universally and also invalidates a corollary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض المعتزلة : الغيران هما المختلفان في الوصف. وهذا باطل على أصلهم فإن من مذهبهم أن قيام سوداين في وقت واحد في محل واحد جائز وهما غيران ولا يختلفان بوصف ما ثم هو باطل عند الجميع بالواحد من العشرة واليد من الآدمي فإن الواحد مع العشرة يختلفان في الوصف، وكذا اليد مع الآدمي ولا تغاير وبهذا يبطل قول من يقول : إن ما ليس بالشيء فهو غيره فإن الواحد ليس بالعشرة وليس بغيرها وكذا اليد من الآدمي فإن اتبع واحد منهم رأي جعفر بن حرب وزعم أن الواحد من العشرة غير العشرة واليد من الآدمي غيره، قيل: هذا فاسد لم يقل به أحد من المتكلمين إلا جعفر بن حرب وخالفه فيه أخوانه من أهل الاعتزال وعد هذا من جهالاته، وهذا لأن العشرة اسم يقع على مجموع هؤلاء الأفراد فكان متناولا كل فرد مع أغياره فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه لأنه مع العشرة ولن تكون العشرة بدونه. وكذا اسم زيد يقع عليه باعتبار هذه الأعضاء فكان متناولا مجموع هذه الأعضاء فإذا قيل يد زيد غيره كانت اليد غير نفسها، وربما يشكل هذا في يد الآدمي لبقاء الاسم بعد فوات اليد وزواله عند فوات الواحد من العشرة، إلا أن بقاء الاسم في الآدمي كان لأن علة استحقاق الاسم هو التركيب المخصوص والصورة المخصوصة، وبفوات اليد بقى أكثر ذلك فبقى الاسم لبقاء علة الاستحقاق، وبهذا لا يثبت أن عند قيام اليد لم يكن اسم الآدمي متناولا إياها، وعند ثبوت التناول لو أثبت المغايرة لصارت اليد غير نفسها كما في الواحد مع العشرة. وفي العشرة بفوات الواحد زال الاسم، لأنها علة استحقاق القدر المخصوص إذ هي من أسماء الأقدار، وقد زال ذلك القدر وثبت قدر آخر هو علة استحقاق اسم آخر فزال الاسم، وثبوت الفرق من هذه الجهة بين اليد من الآدمي والواحد من العشرة لا يوجب التفرقة بينهما في كون الاسم متناولا لجميع الأفراد في كل واحد منهما، واستحالة كون الفرد غير الجميع لتناول اسم الجميع إياه. ووراء هذا في تقرير استحالة جعل اليد غير الآدمي كلام ذكرته في بعض المواضع أعرضت عنه هاهنا لئلا يفوت الإيجاز المشروط في أول الكتاب والله الموفق.
And some of the Mu'tazila said: 'The two distinct things (al-ghayran) are those that differ in description.' And this is invalid according to their own principle, for among their doctrines is that the existence of two blacknesses at one time in one locus is permissible, and they are two distinct things while they do not differ in any description. Then it is invalid by universal consensus by the example of the one from ten and the hand from a human: for the one and the ten differ in description, and similarly the hand and the human, yet there is no distinctness. And by this is invalidated the statement of whoever says that what is not the thing is other than it: for the one is not the ten and is not other than it, and similarly the hand from the human. Then if one of them follows the opinion of Ja'far ibn Harb and claims that the one from ten is other than the ten and the hand from the human is other than it, it is said: This is invalid; none of the theologians said it except Ja'far ibn Harb, and his fellow Mu'tazilites opposed him in it and considered this among his ignorances. And this is because the name 'ten' applies to the totality of these individuals, so it encompasses each individual together with its others. If the one were other than the ten, it would become other than itself because it is with the ten, and the ten would not exist without it. Similarly, the name 'Zayd' applies to him in consideration of these members, so it encompasses the totality of these members. If it is said 'Zayd's hand is other than him,' the hand would be other than itself. And perhaps this is problematic in the case of the human's hand due to the survival of the name after the loss of the hand, and its removal at the loss of the one from ten, except that the survival of the name in the human is because the cause of deserving the name is the specific composition and specific form, and with the loss of the hand most of that remains, so the name remains due to the survival of the cause of deserving. And by this it is not established that at the existence of the hand the name 'human' did not encompass it, and when encompassment is established, if distinctness were affirmed, the hand would become other than itself as in the case of the one with the ten. And in the ten, with the loss of the one, the name is removed, because it is the cause of deserving a specific quantity, for it is among the names of quantities, and that quantity is removed and another quantity is established that is the cause of deserving another name, so the name is removed. And the establishment of the difference in this respect between the hand from the human and the one from ten does not necessitate differentiation between them in the fact that the name encompasses all individuals in each one of them, and the impossibility of the individual being other than the whole due to the name of the whole encompassing it. And beyond this in establishing the impossibility of making the hand other than the human, there is discourse I mentioned in some places; I refrained from it here lest the conciseness stipulated at the beginning of the book be lost. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition of 'two distinct things' (al-ghayran) as 'those that differ in description.' The author first shows it is invalid even on Mu'tazilite grounds: they allow two blacknesses in one locus, which are distinct but do not differ in description. Then he argues universally: the one from ten and the hand from a human differ in description yet are not distinct. He then refutes a corollary that 'what is not the thing is other than it' by showing the one is not the ten but also not other than it. He addresses Ja'far ibn Harb's contrary view that the one from ten is other than the ten, noting it is rejected by all theologians. The core argument is that if a name (e.g., 'ten') applies to a whole, each part is included; if the part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself. Similarly for 'Zayd' and his hand. He resolves an objection about the hand: the name 'human' persists after hand loss because the cause (form) mostly remains, unlike the number 'ten' which changes upon loss of a unit. Thus, the hand is not other than the human. The author concludes by noting he has more on this but omits for brevity.

Key Terms
غيران
وصف
واحد من العشرة
يد الآدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That distinctness requires a difference in description.
Correction: Two things can be distinct while sharing the same description, as shown by two blacknesses.

Checkpoint
Which of the following does the author use as a counterexample to the Mu'tazilite definition?
A) Two blacknesses in one locus
B) The one from ten
C) The hand from a human
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Difference in description is neither necessary nor sufficient for two things to be distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilites assumed difference in description means distinctness. In your own life, have you ever treated someone as 'other' simply because they described things differently, even though you both shared the same faith? How can this lesson reshape your view of those who differ from you in practice or opinion?
?
Consider the example of the hand and the human: the hand is not the human, yet it is not other than the human. Are there parts of your own identity (e.g., your emotions, your past mistakes) that you consider 'other' to your true self? How does this passage challenge you to integrate rather than reject them?
?
The lesson shows that two things can be described identically yet remain distinct. Can you think of a relationship (with a spouse, friend, or Allah) where you expected uniformity of description as proof of unity? What must change in your expectations?

The Name-Encompassing Argument and the Hand Objection
Refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb's view and resolution of the hand objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the name of a whole encompassing its parts proves that the parts are not other than the whole, and how the objection regarding the hand's name survival is resolved.

Core Idea
A part is not other than the whole because the name of the whole includes it.

Imam al-Nasafi deepens the refutation by addressing a specific Mu'tazilite view attributed to Ja'far ibn Harb, who claimed that the one from ten is other than the ten and the hand from a human is other than the human. The imam argues that if a name applies to a collection, each individual is included; therefore, if a part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself. He then resolves an objection concerning the hand because the name 'human' persists after hand loss, showing that the cause of the name remains.

Guided Path
1
Read Ja'far ibn Harb's claim and its refutation
Read the passage and identify the claim that the one from ten is other than the ten and the hand from the human is other than the human. Note that this view is rejected by all except Ja'far.
Ja'far's view is considered an ignorance.
2
Understand the name-encompassing argument
The author argues that the name 'ten' applies to the totality of individuals, so if the one were other than the ten, it would be other than itself. Similarly for 'Zayd' and his hand.
This shows the impossibility of the part being other than the whole.
3
Understand the hand objection and its resolution
An objection is raised: the name 'human' persists after hand loss, unlike the name 'ten' which disappears when a unit is lost. The author resolves by explaining the cause of the name: the human form mostly remains, so the name persists, but that does not negate the name's encompassment when the hand exists.
The resolution shows the hand is not other than the human.

وقال بعض المعتزلة : الغيران هما المختلفان في الوصف. وهذا باطل على أصلهم فإن من مذهبهم أن قيام سوداين في وقت واحد في محل واحد جائز وهما غيران ولا يختلفان بوصف ما ثم هو باطل عند الجميع بالواحد من العشرة واليد من الآدمي فإن الواحد مع العشرة يختلفان في الوصف، وكذا اليد مع الآدمي ولا تغاير وبهذا يبطل قول من يقول : إن ما ليس بالشيء فهو غيره فإن الواحد ليس بالعشرة وليس بغيرها وكذا اليد من الآدمي فإن اتبع واحد منهم رأي جعفر بن حرب وزعم أن الواحد من العشرة غير العشرة واليد من الآدمي غيره، قيل: هذا فاسد لم يقل به أحد من المتكلمين إلا جعفر بن حرب وخالفه فيه أخوانه من أهل الاعتزال وعد هذا من جهالاته، وهذا لأن العشرة اسم يقع على مجموع هؤلاء الأفراد فكان متناولا كل فرد مع أغياره فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه لأنه مع العشرة ولن تكون العشرة بدونه. وكذا اسم زيد يقع عليه باعتبار هذه الأعضاء فكان متناولا مجموع هذه الأعضاء فإذا قيل يد زيد غيره كانت اليد غير نفسها، وربما يشكل هذا في يد الآدمي لبقاء الاسم بعد فوات اليد وزواله عند فوات الواحد من العشرة، إلا أن بقاء الاسم في الآدمي كان لأن علة استحقاق الاسم هو التركيب المخصوص والصورة المخصوصة، وبفوات اليد بقى أكثر ذلك فبقى الاسم لبقاء علة الاستحقاق، وبهذا لا يثبت أن عند قيام اليد لم يكن اسم الآدمي متناولا إياها، وعند ثبوت التناول لو أثبت المغايرة لصارت اليد غير نفسها كما في الواحد مع العشرة. وفي العشرة بفوات الواحد زال الاسم، لأنها علة استحقاق القدر المخصوص إذ هي من أسماء الأقدار، وقد زال ذلك القدر وثبت قدر آخر هو علة استحقاق اسم آخر فزال الاسم، وثبوت الفرق من هذه الجهة بين اليد من الآدمي والواحد من العشرة لا يوجب التفرقة بينهما في كون الاسم متناولا لجميع الأفراد في كل واحد منهما، واستحالة كون الفرد غير الجميع لتناول اسم الجميع إياه. ووراء هذا في تقرير استحالة جعل اليد غير الآدمي كلام ذكرته في بعض المواضع أعرضت عنه هاهنا لئلا يفوت الإيجاز المشروط في أول الكتاب والله الموفق.
And some of the Mu'tazila said: 'The two distinct things (al-ghayran) are those that differ in description.' And this is invalid according to their own principle, for among their doctrines is that the existence of two blacknesses at one time in one locus is permissible, and they are two distinct things while they do not differ in any description. Then it is invalid by universal consensus by the example of the one from ten and the hand from a human: for the one and the ten differ in description, and similarly the hand and the human, yet there is no distinctness. And by this is invalidated the statement of whoever says that what is not the thing is other than it: for the one is not the ten and is not other than it, and similarly the hand from the human. Then if one of them follows the opinion of Ja'far ibn Harb and claims that the one from ten is other than the ten and the hand from the human is other than it, it is said: This is invalid; none of the theologians said it except Ja'far ibn Harb, and his fellow Mu'tazilites opposed him in it and considered this among his ignorances. And this is because the name 'ten' applies to the totality of these individuals, so it encompasses each individual together with its others. If the one were other than the ten, it would become other than itself because it is with the ten, and the ten would not exist without it. Similarly, the name 'Zayd' applies to him in consideration of these members, so it encompasses the totality of these members. If it is said 'Zayd's hand is other than him,' the hand would be other than itself. And perhaps this is problematic in the case of the human's hand due to the survival of the name after the loss of the hand, and its removal at the loss of the one from ten, except that the survival of the name in the human is because the cause of deserving the name is the specific composition and specific form, and with the loss of the hand most of that remains, so the name remains due to the survival of the cause of deserving. And by this it is not established that at the existence of the hand the name 'human' did not encompass it, and when encompassment is established, if distinctness were affirmed, the hand would become other than itself as in the case of the one with the ten. And in the ten, with the loss of the one, the name is removed, because it is the cause of deserving a specific quantity, for it is among the names of quantities, and that quantity is removed and another quantity is established that is the cause of deserving another name, so the name is removed. And the establishment of the difference in this respect between the hand from the human and the one from ten does not necessitate differentiation between them in the fact that the name encompasses all individuals in each one of them, and the impossibility of the individual being other than the whole due to the name of the whole encompassing it. And beyond this in establishing the impossibility of making the hand other than the human, there is discourse I mentioned in some places; I refrained from it here lest the conciseness stipulated at the beginning of the book be lost. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition of 'two distinct things' (al-ghayran) as 'those that differ in description.' The author first shows it is invalid even on Mu'tazilite grounds: they allow two blacknesses in one locus, which are distinct but do not differ in description. Then he argues universally: the one from ten and the hand from a human differ in description yet are not distinct. He then refutes a corollary that 'what is not the thing is other than it' by showing the one is not the ten but also not other than it. He addresses Ja'far ibn Harb's contrary view that the one from ten is other than the ten, noting it is rejected by all theologians. The core argument is that if a name (e.g., 'ten') applies to a whole, each part is included; if the part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself. Similarly for 'Zayd' and his hand. He resolves an objection about the hand: the name 'human' persists after hand loss because the cause (form) mostly remains, unlike the number 'ten' which changes upon loss of a unit. Thus, the hand is not other than the human. The author concludes by noting he has more on this but omits for brevity.

Key Terms
جعفر بن حرب
اسم
تناول
علة الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hand from a human is other than the human.
Correction: The hand is part of the human and not ontologically separate; the name 'human' encompasses it.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, why is Ja'far ibn Harb's view considered invalid by most theologians?
A) Because it contradicts the definition of al-ghayran
B) Because if the one were other than the ten, the one would become other than itself
C) Because the hand is not part of the human
D) Because the name 'ten' does not include the one

Key Takeaway
The name of a whole encompasses its parts, so the part cannot be other than the whole without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim that seems to make sense but contradicts a clear rational principle (like part and whole), do you accept the rational principle or cling to the claim? Which is your default? This passage demonstrates that even a respected scholar's view can be refuted by simple logic. Will you allow your own opinions—or those of people you admire—to be corrected by sound reasoning, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The author notes that Ja'far ibn Harb's fellow Mu'tazilites rejected his view. Are you part of a community where you feel safe being corrected? Or do you surround yourself with people who only affirm what you already believe? This lesson calls us to value truth over group loyalty. Whose opinion would you challenge if it contradicted clear proof?
?
Consider the relationship between your actions and your identity. Are your sins 'other than' you, or do they in some way become part of who you are until you repent? The logic here shows that what is encompassed by a name cannot be completely separate. How does this affect your view of personal accountability and the need for constant self-examination?

Refutation of a Mu'tazilite Definition of Distinct Things
Imam al-Nasafi refutes a Mu'tazilite definition of distinct things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining distinct things based solely on separate knowability fails.

Core Idea
A single thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another.

This lesson examines a Mu'tazilite definition of two distinct things as those where one can be known and the other unknown. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that a single entity can be known in one respect and unknown in another, using the example of blackness. The argument highlights a subtle flaw in equating epistemic access with ontological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim followed by a refutation with an example.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite Claim
Identify the Mu'tazilite definition of 'two distinct things' (al-ghayran) as those where one can be known and the other unknown. This definition is based on epistemic accessibility.
3
Analyze the Refutation
See how the author rejects this definition by pointing out that a single thing (e.g., blackness) can be known in one respect (as a color) and unknown in another (as impermanent). This shows that separate knowability does not imply separate entities.
The passage is cut off, but the argument continues by noting that if every respect were considered a separate distinct thing, it would lead to an infinite regress.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض المعتزلة : الغيران هما اللذان يصح أن يعلم أحدهما ويجهل الآخر. وهذا باطل، لأن الشيء يعلم بجهة ويجهل بجهة كمن يعرف السواد أنه لون يعرف أنه مستحيل البقاء، فإن جعلوا كل جهة غير صاحبتها فقد جعلوا...

And this is invalid, because a thing is known in one respect and unknown in another, like someone who knows that blackness is a color, he knows that it is impossible to persist. For if they make every respect other than its companion, then they have made...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of another Mu'tazilite definition of 'two distinct things' based on the possibility of knowing one and being ignorant of the other. The author rejects this by pointing out that a single thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another. He gives the example of blackness: one may know it is a color but be ignorant that it cannot persist (i.e., accidents perish). If the Mu'tazilites then claim that each respect is distinct from the other, they would multiply entities arbitrarily. The passage is cut off, but the argument is that the definition fails because knowledge and ignorance of the same thing in different respects does not imply two distinct things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are separately knowable, they are necessarily distinct.
Correction: A single entity can be known in one respect and unknown in another, so separate knowability does not guarantee distinctness.

Epistemic accessibility does not determine ontological distinctness; a single thing can be known and unknown in different respects.

?

?
This lesson shows that a single thing can be both known and unknown depending on the perspective. How does that affect your confidence in your religious knowledge? Are you willing to be uncertain in some respects while certain in others, and how does that shape your humility before God?

Refuting That a Part Is Other Than the Whole
Refutation of the claim that a part is 'other' than the whole, using examples from numbers and human anatomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming a part is 'other' (ghayr) than the whole leads to a logical contradiction, based on the inclusion of the part in the name of the whole.

Core Idea
Claiming a part is other than the whole makes the part other than itself.

In this passage, the author refutes an isolated opinion that a part of a whole is 'other' (ghayr) than the whole. He uses concrete examples—the number ten and the human body—to show that if a part were truly 'other', it would have to be other than itself, which is impossible. An objection regarding the survival of the name after removal of a part is then resolved, reinforcing the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Notice the author's style and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: غير (ghayr), العشرة (ten), الآدمي (human).
2
Identify the claim and the two examples
Identify the claim being refuted: that a part is other than the whole. Then locate the two examples: the number ten with its units, and the human body with its hand. Understand how the reasoning applies in each case.
The author argues that if the part were 'other', it would be other than itself because the name of the whole includes it.
3
Understand the objection
The objection points out that when a hand is removed, the name 'human' remains, but when one unit is removed from ten, the name 'ten' disappears. Reflect on why this difference might seem to challenge the argument.
The author responds that the survival of the name is due to the persistence of the specific form and composition, not because the hand was not included.
4
See the resolution and conclusion
The author explains that the difference in naming does not affect the fact that when the hand was present, the name 'human' included it. Therefore, asserting difference would still make the hand other than itself. Conclude that the attack is invalid.
The author also mentions a longer discussion he set aside for brevity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيره، قيل: هذا فاسد لم يقل به أحد من المتكلمين إلا جعفر بن حرب وخالفه فيه أخوانه من أهل الاعتزال وعد هذا من جهالاته، وهذا لأن العشرة اسم يقع على مجموع هؤلاء الأفراد فكان متناولا كل فرد مع أغياره فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه لأنه مع العشرة ولن تكون العشرة بدونه. وكذا اسم زيد يقع عليه باعتبار هذه الأعضاء فكان متناولا مجموع هذه الأعضاء فإذا قيل يد زيد غيره كانت اليد غير نفسها، وربما يشكل هذا في يد الآدمي لبقاء الاسم بعد فوات اليد وزواله عند فوات الواحد من العشرة، إلا أن بقاء الاسم في الآدمي كان لأن علة استحقاق الاسم هو التركيب المخصوص والصورة المخصوصة، وبفوات اليد بقى أكثر ذلك فبقى الاسم لبقاء علة الاستحقاق، وبهذا لا يثبت أن عند قيام اليد لم يكن اسم الآدمي متناولا إياها، وعند ثبوت التناول لو أثبت المغايرة لصارت اليد غير نفسها كما في الواحد مع العشرة. وفي العشرة بفوات الواحد زال الاسم، لأنها علة استحقاق القدر المخصوص إذ هي من أسماء الأقدار، وقد زال ذلك القدر وثبت قدر آخر هو علة استحقاق اسم آخر فزال الاسم، وثبوت الفرق من هذه الجهة بين اليد من الآدمي والواحد من العشرة لا يوجب التفرقة بينهما في كون الاسم متناولا لجميع الأفراد في كل واحد منهما، واستحالة كون الفرد غير الجميع لتناول اسم الجميع إياه. ووراء هذا في تقرير استحالة جعل اليد غير الآدمي كلام ذكرته في بعض المواضع أعرضت عنه هاهنا لئلا يفوت الإيجاز المشروط في أول الكتاب والله الموفق.
Other than it. It was said: This is corrupt; none of the mutakallimun said it except Ja‘far ibn Harb, and his brothers among the Mu‘tazila opposed him in it, and counted this among his follies. This is because the name ‘ten’ applies to the aggregate of these individuals, so it includes each individual along with its others. Thus if the one were other than the ten, it would become other than itself because it is (part of) the ten, and the ten would not be without it. Likewise, the name ‘Zayd’ applies to him by virtue of these members, so it includes the aggregate of these members. Thus if it is said ‘Zayd’s hand is other than him’, the hand would be other than itself. And perhaps this is problematic in the case of the human hand because the name remains after the hand is lost, whereas it disappears when one of the ten is lost. However, the survival of the name in the human is because the cause for deserving the name is the specific composition and specific form, and with the loss of the hand most of that remains, so the name remains because the cause of entitlement remains. By this it is not established that when the hand existed, the name ‘human’ did not include it. And when inclusion is established, if one affirms difference, the hand would become other than itself, as in the case of the one with respect to the ten. In the case of ten, with the loss of the one the name disappears, because it (the ten) is a cause of entitlement for a specific quantity, since it is among names of quantities, and that quantity has ceased, and another quantity has become established which is the cause of entitlement for another name, so the name disappears. The establishment of a difference in this respect between the hand from the human and the one from the ten does not necessitate a distinction between them regarding the fact that the name includes all individuals in each of them, and the impossibility of the individual being other than the whole because the name of the whole includes it. Beyond this, concerning the elucidation of the impossibility of making the hand other than the human, there is a discussion I mentioned in some places, which I have set aside here lest it lose the brevity stipulated at the beginning of the book. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a part is 'other' (ghayr) than the whole. The author begins by stating that this claim is corrupt and only Ja‘far ibn Harb among the mutakallimun held it, and his Mu‘tazilite colleagues opposed him. Then he explains the reasoning: the name of the whole (like 'ten') includes each individual part, so if you say the part is other than the whole, it becomes other than itself. Similarly for the human body: the name 'Zayd' includes all his members, so saying 'hand is other than Zayd' makes the hand other than itself. An objection is raised: if the hand is removed, 'Zayd' still applies, but if one unit is removed from ten, 'ten' no longer applies. The author answers that the survival of the name 'Zayd' is due to the persistence of the specific form and composition; most of it remains. This difference in the cause of naming does not affect the fact that when the hand was present, the name 'Zayd' included it. In both cases, if we assert that the part is other than the whole, we end up with self-contradiction. Thus the argument holds.

Key Terms
غير
العشرة
الآدمي
الجزء والكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A part can be 'other' (ghayr) than the whole because it can be removed without the whole's name always disappearing.
Correction: The name of the whole includes the part when present; asserting 'otherness' makes the part other than itself, which is impossible. The persistence of the name after removal is due to other factors.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot the hand be considered 'other' than the human?
A) Because the hand is an essential part of the human.
B) Because the name 'human' includes the hand, so it would be other than itself.
C) Because the hand cannot survive independently.
D) Because the mutakallimun agreed on this.

Key Takeaway
The name of the whole includes its parts, so asserting a part is 'other' than the whole leads to self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you point to a habit or flaw in your life and say, 'That’s not the real me,' you are making the same logical error as Ja‘far ibn Harb. What part of your character have you been treating as other than yourself, unwilling to accept as part of the whole of who you are before Allah? Will you now repent and reintegrate it?
?
The name 'Zayd' remains after the hand is lost because most of the composition remains. What does this teach you about what truly defines your identity as a believer? If your health, wealth, or abilities were taken, would your core identity as a servant of Allah remain? How can you live today so that your identity is anchored in what cannot be removed?
?
The whole cannot exist without its part. In your relationship with Allah, are there parts—like your prayers, charity, or honesty—that you treat as optional extras rather than essential to the whole of your faith? How would your life change if you saw every action as integral to the whole of your submission?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Definition of Ghayr
The author rejects a Mu'tazilite definition of 'ghayr' based on knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite definition of 'ghayr' based on epistemic distinguishability is flawed, and how the author refutes it using the example of blackness and the absurdity of multiplying entities.

Core Idea
A thing can be known in one aspect and unknown in another—that doesn't make it two things.

This passage tackles a key debate in kalam about the nature of 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya). Some Mu'tazila proposed a definition based on epistemic distinguishability: two things are 'other' if one can be known while the other is unknown. The author argues this definition fails because a single thing can be known and unknown in different respects. As you read, notice how the author uses a concrete example to expose the problem and then pushes the argument to its logical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite definition
Start by reading the definition as presented: 'al-ghayrān are the two that it is valid to know one and be ignorant of the other.' Understand what the Mu'tazila are claiming as the criterion for two things being 'other'.
This definition relies on the possibility of distinct knowledge states.
2
Analyze the counterexample
The author gives an example: blackness (sawād) is known to be a color but unknown (by some) regarding its persistence. Notice that this is a single thing with two epistemic aspects. Ask yourself: does this satisfy the definition of two 'other' things?
The example shows that a single entity can be both known and unknown in different respects.
3
Examine the dilemma
The passage presents a conditional argument. If the Mu'tazila treat each aspect as a distinct entity, then they would split a simple accident and an indivisible atom into multiple things—absurd. If they do not, then the definition fails. Grasp both horns.
The author forces the opponent into an impossible choice.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the definition is invalid (bāṭil). Reflect on why this matters: defining 'otherness' correctly is essential for theological discussions about attributes, essence, and distinct entities like the world and the Creator.
This argument shows the danger of defining metaphysical concepts through human epistemic limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض المعتزلة : الغيران هما اللذان يصح أن يعلم أحدهما ويجهل الآخر. وهذا باطل، لأن الشيء يعلم بجهة ويجهل بجهة كمن يعرف السواد أنه لون ويعرف أنه مستحيل البقاء، فإن جعلوا كل جهة غير صاحبتها فقد جعلوا العرض والواحد الذي هو غير متجزئ شيئين متغايرين والقول بتعدد الواحد وتغيره محال، وإن لم يجعلوا كل جهة غير الجهة الأخرى أبطلوا التحديد.
And some Mu‘tazila said: The two things that are other (al-ghayrān) are the two that it is valid to know one of them and be ignorant of the other. This is false, because a thing is known in one aspect and unknown in another aspect, like one who knows blackness that it is a color and knows that it is impossible to persist. If they make each aspect other than its companion, then they have made the accident and the indivisible one two mutually distinct things, and the statement that the one is multiple and changes is impossible. And if they do not make each aspect other than the other aspect, they have invalidated the definition.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage refutes a Mu'tazilite definition of 'two other things' (al-ghayrān). Some Mu'tazila defined them as two things such that one can be known while the other is unknown. The author argues this is false because a single thing can be known in one aspect and unknown in another. For example, blackness is known to be a color but unknown (to some) in terms of its persistence. If the Mu'tazila take each aspect as a distinct thing, then they would arbitrarily split an accident and an indivisible atom into multiple distinct entities, which is impossible. If they do not treat each aspect as distinct, then the definition fails. Thus the definition is invalid.

Key Terms
الغيران
السواد
العرض
الواحد الذي هو غير متجزئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that different aspects of knowledge of a single thing imply multiple distinct entities.
Correction: A single entity can have multiple epistemic aspects without becoming multiple entities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the Mu'tazilite definition of 'ghayr' invalid?
A) Because it is impossible to know one thing and be ignorant of another.
B) Because a single thing can be known in one aspect and unknown in another, so the definition does not guarantee two distinct things.
C) Because all things are equally known.
D) Because the Mu'tazilite definition is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite definition of 'ghayr' fails because a single thing can be known and unknown in different aspects, and treating aspects as separate entities leads to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you judged something to be two separate things simply because you knew it in two different ways? How does this lesson expose that mistake in your own thinking? Will you commit to pausing before dividing what may actually be one?
?
The Mu'tazilites built a definition on an assumption about knowledge and ignorance. What assumptions are you building your life on that might collapse under the weight of a single counterexample? Are you willing to test them today?
?
If a thing can be known in one aspect and unknown in another, what does that say about your certainty in your judgments about others, about God, or about yourself? Will you now hold your opinions with more humility?

The Grammar of Otherness: 'Ali ibn 'Isa on Ghayr
'Ali ibn 'Isa defines 'ghayr' grammatically by analyzing the two types of idafa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical definition of 'ghayr' (other) based on dualization in Arabic construct phrases and its theological application.

Core Idea
Two things are truly 'other' only when they can be dualized in a possessive construct.

'Ali ibn 'Isa al-Nahwi, a Mu'tazilite grammarian, offers a precise definition of 'ghayr' rooted in Arabic syntax. His analysis of the two types of idafa (construct) reveals when two entities are genuinely distinct and when they are not. This grammatical insight has direct implications for theology, particularly regarding God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice the example 'al-rajulu ghayru Zayd' (the man is other than Zayd) and the exceptions mentioned.
The passage uses technical grammatical terms.
2
Understand the definition of 'ghayr'
Identify the defining criterion: the possibility of dualizing the construct term (mudaf) with its governed genitive (mudaf ilayhi). Ask why 'one of ten' fails this test.
3
Examine the two types of idafa
Learn the partitive (min-type) and possessive (lam-type) constructs. See how the relationship between mudaf and mudaf ilayhi differs in each.
The partitive type implies inclusion; the possessive implies ownership.
4
Apply the distinction to 'ilm Allah
Consider why the construct 'knowledge of God' must be possessive, not partitive, ensuring that knowledge and God are distinct.
This has theological significance for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال على بن عيسى النحوي: حقيقة "غير" ما صح أن يثنى مع المضاف إليه نحو: الرجل غير زيد فهما اثنان، وقد احترز عن الواحد من العشرة واليد من الآدمي. وبيان ذلك أن الإضافة تنقسم إلى قسمين: إضافة بتقدير كلمة "من" وهي إضافة البعض إلى الكل. وإضافة بتقدير اللام وهي المسماة عند النحويين إضافة تمليك. وفي الإضافة بتقدير "من" لا يصح أن يثنى المضاف المضاف إليه، لأن اسم المضاف إليه يتناول المضاف كما يقال: خاتم ذهب وسوار فضة، فلا يكون بين المضاف والمضاف إليه تغاير، وكذا هذا في قولك: يد زيد وواحد من العشرة، لأن اسم المضاف إليه يتناول المضاف من أشياء أخر. فأما بالإضافة بتقدير اللام فاسم المضاف إليه لا يتناول المضاف كما في قول الرجل دار زيد و غلام عمرو، فيتصور ضم أحدهما إلى صاحبه فكانت تثنيته. وأما في الفصل الأول فلا يتصور الضم، لأن المضاف داخل تحت المضاف فلو ضم إليه لضم إلى نفسه وهذا محال. ثم إضافة العلم إلى الله تعالى لا تكون بتقدير كلمة من بل تكون بتقدير اللام فيكون المضاف مع المضاف إليه متغايرين هذا هو تقرير تحديده والكشف عن معناه وصار الحاصل أن كل اثنين متغايران أو كل مسميين أو مذكورين ليس
And ‘Ali ibn ‘Isa al-Nahwi said: The reality of 'other' (ghayr) is what is valid to be dualized with the governed genitive (al-mudaf ilayhi), such as 'the man is other than Zayd,' so they are two. And he avoided the one from ten and the hand from the human. The explanation of that is that the construct (idāfa) is divided into two types: a construct with the implication of the word 'min', which is the construct of the part to the whole; and a construct with the implication of the lam, which is called by grammarians the construct of ownership (idāfat tamlīk). In the construct with 'min', it is not valid to dualize the construct term (mudāf) with the governed genitive (mudāf ilayhi), because the name of the mudāf ilayhi includes the mudāf, as it is said: 'a ring of gold' and 'a bracelet of silver', so there is no otherness (taghāyur) between the mudāf and the mudāf ilayhi. Similarly, this is in your saying: 'the hand of Zayd' and 'one of the ten', because the name of the mudāf ilayhi includes the mudāf from among other things. As for the construct with the lam, the name of the mudāf ilayhi does not include the mudāf, as in the saying: 'the man's house' and 'Amr's slave', so it is conceivable to join one of them to its companion, so the dualization is possible. As for the first type, joining is not conceivable, because the mudāf is included within the mudāf ilayhi, so if one were to join it to it, it would be joining it to itself, and that is impossible. Then, the addition (idāfa) of knowledge to God Almighty is not with the implication of the word 'min', but rather with the implication of the lam, so the mudāf and the mudāf ilayhi are mutually distinct. This is the exposition of his definition and the uncovering of its meaning. And the conclusion is that every two things that are other, or every two named things or mentioned things, are not...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents the grammatical definition of 'ghayr' (other) by the Mu'tazilite grammarian 'Ali ibn 'Isa. He defines 'ghayr' as what can be dualized with its governed genitive (mudaf ilayhi), using the example 'the man is other than Zayd', which is a dual noun. He excludes cases like 'one of ten' and 'hand of human' because they cannot be dualized. He explains by dividing idafa (construct) into two types: (1) idafa implying 'min' (partitive), where the mudaf ilayhi's name includes the mudaf (e.g., 'ring of gold' – gold includes the ring in a sense; 'hand of Zayd' – Zayd includes the hand; 'one of ten' – ten includes the one). In such cases, the two cannot be considered distinct (ghayr) because the second includes the first; dualization would be joining something to itself, which is impossible. (2) idafa implying 'lam' (possessive/ownership), where the mudaf ilayhi does not include the mudaf (e.g., 'house of the man' – the man does not include the house; 'slave of Amr' – Amr does not include the slave). Here the two are distinct and can be dualized. Applying this to theology: the idafa of knowledge to God (e.g., 'ilm Allah) is of the lam-type, not min-type, so knowledge and God are distinct (ghayr). This clarifies the definition and its meaning, concluding that every two distinct things, or every two named/mentioned things, are not [the same] (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
غير
الإضافة
تقدير من
تقدير اللام
إضافة تمليك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that any construct phrase implies a relationship of distinction (ghayr) between the two terms.
Correction: Only possessive constructs (with implied 'lam') allow genuine otherness; partitive constructs (with implied 'min') indicate inclusion, not distinction.

Checkpoint
According to 'Ali ibn 'Isa, why cannot 'one of the ten' be considered 'other' (ghayr) than 'ten'?
A) Because both are parts of a whole.
B) Because the name 'ten' includes the one, so they cannot be dualized.
C) Because 'ten' is a larger number.
D) Because 'one' is not a part of 'ten'.

Key Takeaway
'Ghayr' is defined by the possibility of dualization in possessive constructs, distinguishing things that are truly other from those that are included.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that when something is included in another (partitive idafa), it cannot be considered 'other'. Consider your relationship with God: are there aspects of your life—your health, your wealth, your very existence—that you treat as 'other' than Him, as if they were independent? In what specific way will you begin to see them as included in His sovereignty, not separate from it?
?
This passage uses the test of dualization to define true otherness. Think of a relationship in your life (with a spouse, a friend, or even your own abilities) where you have assumed separateness. This week, identify one such relationship and examine whether it is a possessive or partitive connection. How does recognizing the correct type change how you should relate to that person or thing?
?
The passage concludes that knowledge is other than God because the idafa is possessive. Reflect: Do you treat God's knowledge as something distinct from His essence, or do you assume it is identical? How does your answer affect your trust in His omniscience? Commit to one practical step to align your heart with the correct theological distinction.

The Analogy of Scent: Proving Speech as an Attribute of the Locus
Nasafi poses a dilemma about the scent of camphor to analogically prove that speech is an attribute of the locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the analogy of the scent of camphor to demonstrate that speech is an attribute of the locus, not of the originator.

Core Idea
Speech, like scent, belongs to its locus, not to its originator.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a clever argument by analogy to clarify the nature of divine speech. He uses the example of the scent of camphor to pose a dilemma: either the scent is an attribute of the camphor itself or of its Creator. The only reasonable conclusion is that it belongs to the camphor. By analogy, speech must belong to its locus, not directly to God. This argument reinforces the Maturidi position that speech is an attribute that inheres in a created locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the dilemma structure.
2
Identify the dilemma
The scent of camphor: is it an attribute of the camphor or of God? Follow the two horns.
3
Follow the reductio
If attributed to God, the consequences are impossible (kufr and denial of self-evident truths). So it must be attributed to the camphor.
4
Apply the analogy
By analogy, speech must be an attribute of its locus (e.g., the tongue), not of God. Consider the supporting examples (blackness of crow, etc.).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the author's confident closing: 'bi-hamd Allah wadih'. What does this say about the strength of the argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودة فيه اسم فالرائحة صفة للكافور أم صفة لموجدها وهو الله تعالى؟ فإن قال: صفة موجدها فكانت هي رائحة الله تعالى لا رائحة الكافور وأحد شطري هذا الكلام كفر والثاني جحد الضروريات، وإن قال: هي صفة الكافور فنقول: فالكلام أيضا ينبغي أن يكون صفة المحل، فيكون كلام المحل لا كلام الله تعالى كرائحة الكافور، وسواد الغراب وبياض الثلج ونتن الجيفة ومرارة الخمر، وهذا بحمد الله واضح.
The name existing in it: is the scent an attribute of the camphor or an attribute of its originator, who is God? If he says: attribute of its originator, then it would be the scent of God, not the scent of the camphor, and one of the two sides of this speech is unbelief, and the second is denial of necessities. If he says: it is an attribute of the camphor, then we say: speech also should be an attribute of the locus, so it would be speech of the locus, not speech of God, like the scent of camphor, and the blackness of the crow, the whiteness of snow, the stench of a corpse, and the bitterness of wine. And this, by the praise of God, is clear.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents a clear argument by analogy to prove that speech (al-kalam) is an attribute of the locus (al-mahall) in which it occurs, not of the agent who originates it. The author poses a dilemma concerning the scent (al-ra'iha) of camphor (al-kafur): either it is an attribute of the camphor itself or of its Creator, God. If attributed to God, that would be blasphemy (kufr) and a denial of self-evident truths (jadd al-daruriyyat). If attributed to the camphor, then by analogy, speech should be attributed to the locus in which it appears (e.g., the tongue or the body), not to God. The author supports this with other natural examples: the blackness of the crow, the whiteness of snow, the stench of a corpse, and the bitterness of wine—all are attributes of their respective substances. He concludes that this reasoning is clearly sound (bi-hamd Allah wadih). The argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum against the opponent who might attribute speech to God while denying that it inheres in a created locus.

Key Terms
رائحة
صفة
محل
كافور
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this passage denies that God is the originator of speech or that speech is not an attribute of God at all.
Correction: The passage does not deny that God creates speech; it argues that speech as an attribute inheres in a locus, consistent with the Maturidi view that God's eternal speech manifests in created loci like the tongue or the preserved tablet.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main purpose in using the example of the scent of camphor?
A) To prove that scent is an attribute of the Creator.
B) To argue by analogy that speech should be attributed to the locus, not the agent.
C) To deny the existence of divine attributes altogether.
D) To show that camphor has miraculous properties.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's analogy establishes that speech, like scent, is an attribute that belongs to its locus, not directly to the agent who originates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that speech, like scent, belongs to its locus, do you ever pause to reflect on whose words you are truly hearing when you listen to a lecture or a sermon? Are you content to accept speech without verifying its source and locus? This lesson challenges you to ask: whose locus does your speech serve? Are your words an attribute of your own character, or do they reflect something higher? Tonight, before you speak, ask yourself: is the locus of my speech my ego or my faith?
?
The analogy forces you to attribute effects to their proper loci. Where in your life have you mistakenly attributed something to God that actually belongs to a created locus? How will you correct that attribution today?
?
If scent belongs to camphor and speech belongs to the locus, then what does your speech reveal about you? Do your words carry the fragrance of faith or the stench of arrogance? Commit to one concrete change in how you speak this week.

Refutation of Speech Residing in the Tongue
Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that kalam is located in a part of the tongue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that divine speech consists of letters subsisting in the tongue is rationally untenable, and how Imam Nasafi refutes it by deconstructing its assumptions about locus and composition.

Core Idea
Speech is not made of letters, so it cannot reside in any tongue part.

Imam Nasafi presents a sophisticated refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of speech. The opponent argues that speech, being composed of letters, must subsist in a specific part of the tongue. Nasafi first entertains their reasoning, then shows two possible internal responses from other schools, only to demolish the entire foundation: speech is not letters at all. This passage sharpens our understanding of the divine attribute of speech as eternal and not composed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is dense and argumentative.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The Mu'tazilite view is that speech is ordered letters (al-huruf al-munazzama) and that it subsists in the tongue. Find the lines where this is stated.
The opponent also discusses the part of the tongue as locus.
3
Trace the two internal responses
Nasafi mentions two schools of thought: one treats the whole body as a single indivisible entity, the other assigns the derived name to the specific locus. See how they answer the objection about which part is the speaker.
These are presented as possible rejoinders from those who accept composition.
4
Grasp the final refutation
Nasafi rejects the entire framework: 'speech does not subsist in the tongue, as has been clarified.' This is the decisive point.
The argument about each letter having a distinct articulation point further undercuts the opponent.
5
Reflect on the absurd consequence
If each letter is from a different place, then no part of the tongue can be called 'speaker' since a single letter is not speech. Consider why this undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتأخرين الموصوف بالصفة الجزء الذي قامت به الصفة إلا أنهم يضمرون ذكر ذلك الجزء عند وضوح المعنى، بأن كانت العادة جارية أن تلك الصفة لا توجد إلا بجزء مخصوص، ولو كانت توجد بكل جزء لم يكن بد من ذكر ذلك الجزء على ما قررنا هناك كذا هذا، ثم ما ذكر بعد هذا كله بناء على مذهبه أن الكلام هو الحروف المنظومة وأنه قائم باللسان، وما ذكر أن الكلام لو وجد بجزء من أجزاء اللسان ينبغي أن يكون المتكلم ذلك الجزء من اللسان أو اللسان كله، لأنه أخص به منه بجميع البدن، وقد مر جوابه أن على أحد المذهبين كل البدن جعل كشيء واحد لا يتجزأ، وجعل كل معنى ترجع منفعته إلى كل البدن قائما كله بكله، وعلى المذهب الثاني اختص الجزء الذي هو محل الصفة بالاسم المشتق من الصفة، على أن هذا ممنوع فإن الكلام ليس بقائم باللسان على ما تبين وبهذا يعرف بطلان ما ذكر أنه إذا كان كل حرف يختص بمخرج على حدة ومحل مخصوص ينبغي أن لا يستقر كون كل جزء من ذلك متكلما، لأن الحرف الواحد لا يكون كلاما، فإنا بينا أن الكلام ليس هو الحروف وليس بقائم باللسان ليمكن التعلق بهذا.
The later ones (al-muta'akhkhirun) described by the attribute: the part in which the attribute subsists, except that they imply mention of that part when meaning is clear—habit dictating that that attribute is not found except in a specific part; if it were found in every part, mention of that part would be necessary, as we established there so also this. Then what he mentioned after all this, based on his doctrine that speech (al-kalam) is ordered letters (al-huruf al-munazzama) and that it subsists in the tongue—and what he mentioned that if speech were found in a part of the parts of the tongue, the speaker should be that part of the tongue or the whole tongue, because it is more specific to it than to the whole body—its answer has already passed: that according to one of the two schools, the whole body is made as a single indivisible thing, and every meaning whose benefit returns to the whole body is made to subsist in its entirety. According to the second school, the part which is the locus of the attribute is exclusively given the name derived from the attribute. But this is prevented, because speech does not subsist in the tongue, as has been clarified, and by this the falsehood of what he mentioned is known: that if every letter is specific to a distinct point of articulation and a specific place, it should not be established that every part of that is speaking, because a single letter is not speech. For we have shown that speech is not the letters and does not subsist in the tongue, so that one could cling to this.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument that speech is composed of letters and subsists in the tongue. The author first presents the opponent's position: they claim that an attribute subsists in a specific part of the body, but they sometimes omit explicit mention of that part when the meaning is clear by custom. Then he recounts the opponent's reasoning: if speech were in a part of the tongue, then that part—or the whole tongue—would be called 'speaker' because the speech is more specific to the tongue than to the rest of the body. The author replies with two possible explanations from two schools: one treats the whole body as a single indivisible entity such that a benefit returns to the whole, and the other assigns the derived name to the specific locus of the attribute. However, the author ultimately rejects this entire framework because speech does not subsist in the tongue at all, as he has already shown. He then points out the absurdity that if each letter had a distinct articulation point, no part of the tongue could be called 'speaker' since a single letter is not speech. Thus, the entire argument is invalid because it is based on a false premise.

Key Terms
كلام
حروف منظومة
لسان
صفة
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is composed of letters and exists in a physical part of the tongue or body.
Correction: Speech (kalam) is not composed of letters and does not subsist in any physical locus. Arguments based on such composition are flawed from the start.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that speech subsists in the tongue?
A) Because the tongue is not a single part.
B) Because speech is not composed of letters and does not subsist in the tongue.
C) Because the opponent's reasoning is logically sound but theologically forbidden.
D) Because the part of the tongue cannot be specified.

Key Takeaway
Speech (kalam) is not composed of letters, so arguments about its locus in a physical organ are invalid from the start.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever feel that you are uttering something that is ultimately from Allah's eternal speech, or do you think of it merely as human words? How does that distinction affect your reverence for each verse?
?
If Allah's speech is not like human speech, then how should your attitude change when you hear or read the Quran? Are you truly listening as if the Creator Himself is speaking to you right now?
?
The Mu'tazilites tried to measure the divine by human physics. Where in your own life do you subtly limit Allah by your own understanding? Will you consciously surrender that limitation today and accept His transcendence?
