Refuting Suspension on the Qur'an's Createdness
Nasafi refutes the position of tawaqquf regarding the createdness of the Qur'an and limits its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects blanket suspension of judgment on the Qur'an's createdness, and when suspension is actually permissible.

Core Idea
Suspension of judgment is only valid when the question is ambiguous, not when proof exists.

Nasafi addresses a position that tries to avoid the debate by simply saying 'The Qur'an is Allah's speech' and stopping there. He argues that this is not a valid theological stance because clear proofs exist. However, he acknowledges a legitimate form of suspension—when the questioner's intention is unclear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Nasafi first states the invalid position, then explains the exception.
The passage has two main parts: refutation and valid exception.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Find where Nasafi distinguishes between blameworthy suspension (due to ignorance or ignoring proofs) and permissible suspension (due to ambiguous questioning).
The key phrase is 'إلا أن يتوقف لاشتباه مراد السائل'.
3
Reflect on the proof
Note Nasafi's argument that one must know whether speech is an attribute (uncreated) or other (created); ignorance is not a valid reason for suspension.
This shows that suspension is not a neutral state but a failure to seek knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على هذا أيضا عرف بطلان قول من يتوقف في القرآن فيقول: القرآن كلام الله ولا يزيد على هذا، فلا يقول: إنه مخلوق أو غير مخلوق (وهذا القول منسوب إلى عبد الله الثلجي) ويقول: أقول بالمتفق عليه، وهو أن القرآن كلام الله، وأتوقف عند الاختلاف فلا أقول مخلوق أو غير مخلوق، وذلك لأنا أقمنا الدلائل الموجبة للعلم بصحة قولنا وإبطال قول خصومنا، والتوقف عند قيام الدليل خطأ، وإنما ذلك من موجبات تعارض الأدلة، ولا تعارض بحمد الله لما بينا من صحة دلائلنا، وبطلان دلائل خصومنا، إلا أن يتوقف لاشتباه مراد السائل، أنه يريد به الصفة القائمة بالذات أم هذه العبارات المؤدية، فحينئذ يكون التوقف صحيحا إذا لحق على كل أحد أن لا يجيب لأحد يسأله عن كلام متوجه إلى جهتين إلا بعد التأمل والتفحص أن مراد السائل أيهما فيخبره عنه. والذي يدل على بطلان القول بالتوقف أنه لابد من أن يعلم أنه متكلم بكلام قائم به هو صفته لا هو ولا غيره ولا يكون مخلوقا أو هو متكلم بكلام هو غيره فيكون مخلوقا. ولو قال: أنا لا أعلم بذلك. فيكون وقفا عن الجهل، وحق مثله التعلم لأنه لا دليل دفعه إلى ذلك ليتكلم فيه إنما هو الجهل. والله الموفق.
And by stopping at this also, the invalidity of the saying of one who suspends judgment regarding the Qur'an is known. He says: The Qur'an is the speech of Allah and does not add to this, so he does not say it is created or uncreated (and this saying is attributed to 'Abdullah al-Thalji). And he says: I say what is agreed upon, that the Qur'an is the speech of Allah, and I suspend judgment at the point of disagreement, so I do not say created or uncreated. This is because we have established the proofs that necessitate knowledge of the correctness of our statement and the invalidity of the statement of our opponents. And suspension of judgment when proof exists is error, and that is only among the consequences of conflicting evidences, and there is no conflict, praise be to Allah, due to what we have shown of the validity of our proofs and the invalidity of the proofs of our opponents. Except if one suspends judgment because of ambiguity in the intention of the questioner, whether he intends by it the attribute subsisting in the Essence or these conveying expressions; then suspension is correct when it is incumbent upon everyone not to answer someone who asks about speech directed to two sides except after reflection and scrutiny as to which of the two the questioner intends, and then inform him of it. And what indicates the invalidity of the statement of suspension is that it is necessary to know that He is speaking with speech subsisting in Himself, which is His attribute, not He nor other than He, and it is not created, or He is speaking with speech that is other than Him, then it is created. If he says: I do not know that, then his suspension is due to ignorance, and the right of one like him is to learn, because there is no proof that drove him to that to speak about it; it is only ignorance. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the position of tawaqquf (suspending judgment) regarding the createdness of the Qur'an. Nasafi first states that this position is known to be invalid because clear proofs support the Maturidi view. The suspender claims to only accept the agreed-upon phrase 'Qur'an is Allah's speech' and refuses to say created or uncreated. Nasafi argues that this is wrong because demonstrative proofs exist; suspension is only legitimate when evidences conflict, but here they do not. However, he allows a valid kind of suspension: if the questioner's intention is ambiguous — whether he means the eternal attribute or the recited expressions — then one may pause to clarify. This is proper caution. Then he gives a further proof: one must know that if God speaks with an attribute subsisting in Himself, that speech is uncreated; if it is other than Him, it is created. Ignorance of this fact is not an excuse for suspension; it requires learning.

Key Terms
توقف
الجهل
دليل
تعارض الأدلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All suspension of judgment about theological matters is permissible if one does not want to commit to a position.
Correction: Nasafi shows that suspension is only valid when the questioner's intention is ambiguous; otherwise, it is ignorance in the face of clear proof.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when is it permissible to suspend judgment about the nature of the Qur'an?
A) When one is ignorant of the proofs.
B) When the questioner's intention is ambiguous.
C) When the opponent is from the Mu'tazila.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Suspension of judgment is only valid when the questioner's intention is ambiguous; otherwise, it is ignorance in the face of clear proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When someone asks you a question about a religious matter, do you pause to understand their intention before answering, or do you rush to give a response? How can you apply Nasafi's advice about 'reflection and scrutiny' in your daily conversations? Write one practical change you will make.

The Term 'Hikaya' for Qur'anic Expressions
Nasafi reports the early Maturidi allowance and Ash'ari refusal of the term 'hikaya' for Qur'anic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Maturidi-Ash'ari disagreement over applying the term 'hikaya' to recited Qur'anic expressions and the reason behind the Ash'ari refusal.

Core Idea
The term 'hikaya' was avoided to prevent implying similarity between recited words and God's eternal speech.

Imam Nasafi reports that after establishing God's speech as an eternal attribute and the recited letters as expressions of it, the early scholars differed on whether these expressions can be called a 'hikaya' (narration) of God's speech. The early Maturidis permitted it, while al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi refrained due to concerns about suggesting similarity. Let's explore this nuanced terminological debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall the established distinction: God's speech is an eternal attribute, and the recited letters are only expressions of that attribute. This is the foundation of the disagreement.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that the early Maturidis allowed calling the expressions 'hikaya', while al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi refrained.
3
Grasp the reason
Understand why al-Ash'ari avoided 'hikaya': it could imply that the recited expressions are a copy or imitation of God's eternal speech, leading to similarity (tashbih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت أن كلام الله تعالى صفته القائمة به وأن هذه الحروف عبارات عنها، فبعد ذلك اختلفوا أن إطلاق لفظة الحكاية هل يجوز عليها فيقال: هذه العبارات حكاية عن كلام الله تعالى. فكان الأوائل من أصحابنا يذهبون إلى القول بجواز ذلك، ويقولون: إن لفظة الحكاية ولفظة العبارة سواء، وامتنع الأشعري وأبو العباس القلانسي عن إطلاق لفظة الحكاية، لما فيها من إيهام المشابهة.
Then when it is established that the speech of Allah, the Exalted, is His attribute subsisting in Him, and that these letters are expressions about it, after that they disagreed: is the application of the word 'hikaya' (narration) permissible to them, so that one says: these expressions are a narration of the speech of Allah, the Exalted? The early ones among our companions (the Maturidis) held the opinion of the permissibility of that, and they said: the word 'hikaya' and the word 'ibara' are the same. And al-Ash'ari and Abu al-'Abbas al-Qalanisi refrained from applying the word 'hikaya', because of what is in it of suggesting similarity.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two positions among the Ahl al-Sunna regarding the term 'hikaya' (narration) for the recited expressions of the Qur'an. After establishing that God's speech is an eternal attribute, and that the written/recited expressions are only signs pointing to that attribute, there was disagreement on whether we can call these expressions 'hikaya' (narration) of God's speech. The early Maturidis (called 'our companions' by Nasafi) permitted it, considering 'hikaya' and 'ibara' (expression) synonymous. However, al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi avoided 'hikaya' because it could imply that the recited Qur'an is a copy or imitation of God's eternal speech, which might suggest similarity (tashbih) or corporeality. Thus they preferred to avoid the term to prevent misunderstanding.

قلانسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions are identical on this issue.
Correction: This passage shows a genuine difference: Maturidis allowed 'hikaya', Ash'aris avoided it due to fear of suggesting similarity.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi avoid the term 'hikaya' for the recited expressions of the Qur'an?
A) Because they considered it blasphemy.
B) Because it might imply similarity (tashbih) to God's eternal speech.
C) Because they believed the recited Qur'an is also eternal.
D) Because they thought the early Maturidis were wrong.

Key Takeaway
The early Maturidis allowed calling recited expressions 'hikaya' while Ash'aris avoided it due to concern over implying similarity to God's eternal attribute.

Refutation of the Repetition Objection and Argument from Frequent Usage
Refutation of repetition objection and argument from frequent usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that metaphors cannot be repeated and argues that frequent usage can transform a metaphorical usage into a de facto reality, as illustrated by words like 'ḍa‘īf' and '‘adl'.

Core Idea
Frequent usage can turn a metaphor into a virtual reality, making repetition natural.

The opponent claims that if 'khalq' were a metaphor, it couldn't be repeated in the Qur'an. The author refutes this by showing that Arabic routinely repeats metaphors, giving examples like 'ḍa‘īf' (weak) and '‘adl' (justice). He further argues that such extensive usage can make a word so clear that it effectively becomes a reality, no longer felt as metaphorical. This passage illustrates a key principle in ʿilm al-kalām: the power of ʿurf (custom) and frequent usage in language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the flow of the argument.
The text ends abruptly; focus on the completed reasoning.
2
Identify the examples
Note how the author uses 'ḍa‘īf' and '‘adl' as examples of words originally masdar but used metaphorically for the agent.
These examples are key to the refutation.
3
Understand the repetition point
See how the author counters the objection by showing that these metaphorical words are repeated in Arabic without issue.
The opponent's claim contradicts actual Arab usage.
4
Grasp the frequent usage argument
The author argues that when a metaphorical usage becomes very frequent, it effectively becomes a reality, so no problem in repeating it.
This elevates the argument from mere refutation to a positive principle.
5
Apply to 'khalq'
Understand that 'khalq' for the created thing is similar to '‘adl' for the just person; frequent usage makes it like a reality.
Thus repetition is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر أن الله تعالى كرر لفظة الخلق وأراد بها المخلوق، ولو كان هذا مجازا لما كرر، فليس من شأن العرب التكرير في المجازات كلام واه وادعاء على العرب ما هو خلاف عادتهم، أليس أن الضعيف مصدر وهو في الشاهد معنى وراء الفاعل يذكر ويراد به الفاعل مجازا، وكذا العدل والزور في نظائر كثيرة لا تحصى، ثم إنهم يكررون ذلك في مفاوضاتهم ومحاوراتهم ويريدون به الفاعل دون المصدر فكذا هذا، والتعليل بمثل هذه الكلمات إما غفلة وإما وقاحة يقع فيها الإنسان لإفلاسه. على أنا نقول : إن غرضنا من هذا أن نبين أن إطلاق اسم الخلق على المخلوق مغير عن أصله إذ هو اسم المصدر دون المفعول ثم في الجملة من الكلام المغير عن أصله ما لا يحسن أن يقال إنه مجاز لأنه قد كثر استعماله فظهر معناه كظهوره بالأصل أو أكد كلفظة العدل، وإن كانت في الأصل موضوعة للمصدر ولما كثر استعماله في الفاعل التحق بالحقيقة لظهور المراد به عند كثرة الاستعمال حتى إن من قال : إن الله ليس بعدل لسارع الناس إلى إنكاره وتخطئته. وإن كان الله تعالى في الحقيقة عادلا لا [؟] عدلا بل العدل صفته، والخلق مع المخلوق من هذا القبيل فيصير لغلبة الاستعمال كأنه حقيقة فيجوز تكريره كما
And what he mentioned that God Most High repeated the word 'khalq' and intended by it the created thing, and if this were a metaphor, He would not repeat it — 'It is not the habit of the Arabs to repeat metaphors' — is a flimsy statement and a claim against the Arabs that contradicts their custom. Is not 'al-ḍa‘īf' (the weak) a masdar, yet in actual usage it is a meaning beyond the agent, and it is mentioned intending by it the agent metaphorically? Likewise 'al-‘adl' (justice) and 'al-zūr' (falsehood) in countless similar instances. Then they repeat that in their discussions and conversations, intending by it the agent, not the masdar. So is this case. And explaining by such words is either heedlessness or insolence into which a person falls due to his bankruptcy. But we say: Indeed our purpose in this is to show that the application of the name 'khalq' to the created thing is changed from its original, since it is the name of the masdar, not the maf‘ul. Then, in general, some speech changed from its original is not proper to be called metaphor (majāz) because its usage has become frequent, so its meaning has become as clear as its original meaning, or even stronger, like the word 'al-‘adl'. Even though in origin it was placed for the masdar, when its usage for the agent became frequent, it joined reality due to the clarity of the intended meaning through frequent usage, such that if someone said 'God is not 'adl (just)', people would hasten to deny him and declare him mistaken. And God Most High is in reality 'ādil (just), not [؟] 'adl (justice), rather 'adl is His attribute. And 'khalq' with the created thing is of this category, so due to the preponderance of usage it becomes as if it were a reality, so its repetition is permissible, just as...
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's objection that if using 'khalq' for the created thing were metaphorical, it would not be repeated in the Quran. The author dismisses this as a weak claim against Arab custom. He gives examples: the word 'ḍa‘īf' (weak) is originally a masdar but is used metaphorically for the agent, and it is repeated. Similarly, '‘adl' (justice) is a masdar but is used for the just person, and it is repeated. Thus, repetition does not preclude metaphor. Then the author extends the argument: sometimes speech changed from its original meaning is no longer considered metaphor because of frequent usage. The meaning becomes as clear as the literal meaning. He uses '‘adl' as an example: it originally means 'justice' (masdar) but due to frequent use for the just person, it becomes like a reality, so that denying it is commonly rejected. The same applies to 'khalq': through frequent usage, it becomes like a reality, so repetition is permissible. The text ends abruptly with 'just as...' (incomplete).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A word used metaphorically cannot be repeated without causing confusion or being incorrect.
Correction: The author shows that Arabic speakers regularly repeat metaphorical expressions like 'ḍa‘īf' and '‘adl', and that frequent usage can even make them effectively literal.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that metaphors can be repeated in Arabic?
A) The word 'khalq' itself.
B) The word 'ḍa‘īf' used for a weak person.
C) The word 'makhluq'.
D) The word 'wajh'.

Key Takeaway
Repetition does not preclude metaphorical usage; frequent usage can elevate a metaphor to the status of a reality in linguistic practice.

Disagreement Over the True Maf'ul in Grammar
Nasafi refutes the opponent's grammatical claim by citing Basran-Kufan disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi undermines the opponent's argument by showing that the Basran view of the maf'ul is disputed by al-Farra.

Core Idea
Grammatical authority is not monolithic; disagreement weakens theological proofs based on grammar.

Nasafi begins by challenging the opponent's reliance on a specific grammatical claim, namely that the true object (maf'ul) is the masdar (infinitive). He points out that this is not a universally accepted position: the Basrans hold it, but the Kufan grammarian al-Farra disagrees. Since al-Farra is a major authority, the opponent cannot simply assume the Basran view as definitive. This lesson examines the first part of Nasafi's refutation, focusing on the grammatical disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Nasafi quotes the opponent's statement and then critiques it.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find where Nasafi states the Basran view and then al-Farra's opposing view. What is the key difference?
Basrans say the true maf'ul is the masdar; al-Farra says the masdar is a verb, not an object.
3
Understand the rhetorical move
Why does Nasafi mention al-Farra? How does citing a dissenting authority weaken the opponent's claim?
It shows the claim is not universally accepted, so the opponent cannot treat it as a given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قوله في آخر هذا الفصل : إن عندهم المفعول في الحقيقة هو المصدر، ثم :يقولون مفعول به و مفعول له ومفعول فيه ومفعول معه ولا يزيدون على ذلك، وما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول من أوله إلى آخره كلام فاسد متناقض وقع فيه لجهله بمواصفات أهل اللغة ونبين ذلك فنقول : قوله إن عندهم المفعول في الحقيقة هو المصدر، هذا شيء يقوله البصريون، وخالفهم فيه الفراء، فقال: إن المصدر ليس بمفعول بل هو فعل، والمفعول ما تعلق به الفعل لا نفس الفعل فلم يكن هذا مقبولا من البصريين مع مخالفة الفراء إياهم وهو من رؤساء أهل اللغة، ثم نقول : المفعول عندهم ليس ما هو المفعول حقيقة ، وكذا الفاعل وكذا الفعل. بل الفعل عندهم ما مر والفاعل عندهم ما أسند إليه ما هو الفعل عندهم وهو المحدث عنه لا من فعل حقيقة، والمفعول ما لم يسند إليه ما هو الفعل عندهم وخرج
Then his statement at the end of this chapter: 'According to them, the object (maf'ul) in reality is the infinitive (masdar).' Then [they] say: 'object with', 'object for', 'object in', 'object with'—and they do not go beyond that. And what he took from them concerning the distinction between the verb and the object from beginning to end is corrupt and contradictory speech into which he fell due to his ignorance of the specifications of the linguists. We explain that and say: his statement that according to them the object in reality is the masdar—this is something the Basrians say, but al-Farra disagreed with them, saying: the masdar is not an object, rather it is a verb, and the object is that to which the verb is attached, not the verb itself. So this [view] is not accepted from the Basrians given al-Farra's disagreement with them, and he is one of the foremost linguists. Then we say: the object according to them is not what is the object in reality, and likewise the subject (fa'il) and likewise the verb. Rather, the verb according to them is what has passed, and the subject according to them is that to which what is the verb according to them is predicated, that is the thing spoken about, not the one who actually acts, and the object is that to which what is the verb according to them is not predicated, and [the text ends].
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage critiques the opponent's claim that, according to grammarians, the true 'maf'ul' (object) is the masdar (infinitive). Nasafi points out that this is not a universally accepted position. The Basran grammarians hold this view, but al-Farra (a leading Kufan grammarian) disagreed, asserting that the masdar is not an object but a verb, and that the object is that which receives the action of the verb. Since al-Farra is a major authority, the Basran view is not definitive. Beyond this, Nasafi explains that grammatical terms like 'fa'il' (subject), 'fi'l' (verb), and 'maf'ul' (object) do not correspond to the real-world categories of agent, act, and patient as understood in theology. In grammar, 'fi'l' simply denotes a tense-marked word, 'fa'il' is the subject of predication (the thing spoken about), not necessarily the actual doer, and 'maf'ul' is whatever is not the subject. Therefore, the opponent's attempt to derive theological conclusions from these grammatical definitions is invalid because the linguistic definitions do not reflect ontological reality. The passage ends abruptly with 'wa kharaja' (and [the object] exits), likely continuing to explain that the object is excluded from being the subject.

Key Terms
مفعول
مصدر
بصريون
فراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all grammarians agree that the true maf'ul is the masdar.
Correction: Nasafi shows that al-Farra, a prominent Kufan grammarian, holds a different view, so the claim is disputed.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, who disagreed with the Basran view that the masdar is the true object (maf'ul)?
A) Sibawayh.
B) Al-Mubarrad.
C) Al-Farra.
D) Al-Ash'ari.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement among grammarians shows that the opponent's reliance on the Basran view is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Grammatical vs. Theological Categories of Fa'il, Fi'l, and Maf'ul
Nasafi explains that grammatical categories are based on predication, not reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the grammatical definitions of fa'il, fi'l, and maf'ul do not correspond to the real-world categories of agent, act, and patient used in theology.

Core Idea
Grammar and theology speak different languages; linguistic terms do not map to ontological reality.

In the second part of the passage, Nasafi deepens his refutation by explaining that grammatical terms like 'fa'il' (subject), 'fi'l' (verb), and 'maf'ul' (object) are defined purely in terms of syntactic predication (isnad), not in terms of actual agency or action. This means the opponent's attempt to draw theological conclusions from grammatical definitions is fundamentally misguided. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second part of the Arabic passage. Notice how Nasafi begins with 'Then we say' to introduce a new point.
2
Identify the three grammatical definitions
Find how Nasafi defines fa'il, fi'l, and maf'ul according to grammarians. What is the key factor that distinguishes them?
The key factor is 'isnad' (predication).
3
Contrast with theological meanings
Reflect on the theological concepts of agent (fa'il), act (fi'l), and object (maf'ul). How do they differ from the grammatical definitions?
In theology, a fa'il is one who actually performs an act; in grammar, it is merely the subject of a verb.

D) The masdar of the verb.

Key Takeaway
Grammatical definitions are based on predication, not reality, so they cannot ground theological arguments about agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear phrases in religious discussions like 'Allah does not do X' based on grammar, do you stop to ask whether that category is linguistic or real? Are you willing to re-examine your assumptions about Allah's attributes after learning that grammar does not equate to reality?
?
If linguistic categories are not a reliable guide to theological truth, then what is the proper grounding? Have you been relying on language more than revelation or reason in your understanding of God? How will you check yourself next time you are tempted to argue from grammar?
?
Think of one specific theological argument you have heard that depends on a grammatical point (e.g., about the Qur'an being created or about God's attributes). After this lesson, do you now see a weakness in that argument? What will you do differently when you encounter such arguments in the future?

Distinguishing Linguistic and Theological Definitions of Act and Object
The author distinguishes linguistic and theological definitions of 'act' and 'object' by showing their different markers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theological term 'act' (fi'l) is classified as a noun by linguists and why this difference invalidates arguments that conflate the two disciplines.

Core Idea
Theological 'act' is linguistically a noun—categories do not align.

A common mistake in theological debates is to assume that grammatical terms like 'act' and 'object' refer to the same realities in theology. This passage shows how linguists define these terms differently, and why forcing theological conclusions from grammar is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on markers
Read the Arabic passage about noun markers (alif lam, tanwin, idafa) versus verb markers (qad, sawfa). Notice the author's precise use of linguistic terminology.
The author is quoting the opponent's own claim about the verbal noun being an object.
2
Understand the linguistic definitions
The passage defines a noun as indicating a meaning not coupled with a determined time, while a verb does the opposite. Grasp how these definitions classify the theological 'act' as a noun.
This is crucial for seeing the disconnection between the two fields.
3
Recognize the author's challenge
The author asks the opponent: since the theological act is not a verb in language, how can you force your opponents to accept linguistic categories as binding? This is the central refutation.
The opponent's argument is based on a false equivalence.

يعدون ذلك فعلا بل يعدونه اسما، ولهذا اختص بعلامات الأسماء وهي دخول لام التعريف أو التنوين أو الإضافة دون علامات الأفعال وهي قد وسوف، ودخل فيما هو من حد الاسم عندهم، وهو ما دل على معنى غير مقترن بزمان محصل أو كلمة تدل على المعنى دلالة الإشارة دون الإفادة، وخرج من حد الأفعال عندهم وهو ما دل على خلاف، وما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام ليس بفعل عند أهل اللغة. فكيف تلزم خصومك الأخذ بقولهم. ثم إنه سمى المصادر أحداث الأسماء، فكان ضرب زيد وأكل خالد وشرب محمد أحداثا لهؤلاء، أتقول أنت: إن هذه المعاني حدثت عنهم؟ فإن قلت : نعم، فقد جعلت للعباد أحداثا وإن قلت: لا، فقد خالفت أهل اللغة . ثم قوله في آخر هذا الفصل : إن عندهم المفعول في الحقيقة هو المصدر، ثم يقولون مفعول به و مفعول له ومفعول فيه ومفعول معه ولا يزيدون على ذلك، وما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول من أوله إلى آخره كلام فاسد متناقض وقع فيه لجهله بمواصفات أهل اللغة ونبين ذلك فنقول : قوله إن عندهم المفعول في الحقيقة هو المصدر، هذا شيء يقوله البصريون، وخالفهم فيه الفراء، فقال: إن المصدر ليس بمفعول بل هو فعل، والمفعول ما تعلق به الفعل لا نفس الفعل فلم يكن هذا مقبولا من البصريين مع مخالفة الفراء إياهم وهو من رؤساء أهل اللغة، ثم نقول : المفعول عندهم ليس ما هو المفعول حقيقة ، وكذا الفاعل وكذا الفعل. بل الفعل عندهم ما مر والفاعل عندهم ما أسند إليه ما هو الفعل عندهم وهو المحدث عنه لا من فعل حقيقة، والمفعول ما لم يسند إليه ما هو الفعل عندهم وخرج فاعلا من أن يكون محدثا عنه، فإنك إذا قلت: لم يضرب زيد عمرا، كان زيد عندهم وعمرو مفعولا ولو قلت مات خالد وطال الغلام وأسود الشعر وأبيض الثلج، كان ارتفاع كل اسم من ذلك لكونه فاعلا ، ولا شك أن الفعل لم يوجد من أحد هؤلاء، ولكنه لما حدث عنه وأسند إليه ما هو الفعل في صناعتهم عد فاعلا. وما عندهم من خرج عن الحديث ولم يسند إليه كان مفعولا وإن لم يقع عليه فعل ما كان المصدر عند البصريين مفعولا مطلقا لأن ما هو الفعل لم يسند إليه ولم يحدث عنه فكان عندهم على مقتضى صناعتهم مفعولا لا أن يكون مفعولا حقيقة كما لو قلت: عبدت الله تعالى كان انتصاب قولك : الله ، لكونه مفعولا، وكذا لو قلت: لم يعبد فلان الله وليست حقيقة الفعل واقعة على الله تعالى - تعالى الله عن ذلك - فإذا ما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام كان مفعولا عند البصريين من أهل اللغة، فأما ما هو الفعل عند أهل اللغة فليس بمفعول عندهم، وأما ما هو مفعول عندهم ليس بفعل البتة. وشيء من هذا لا يتصل بما نحن فيه، وبهذا يبطل قوله: ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فإن أهل اللغة جعلوا الفعل الأمثلة التي بينا وهي مأخوذة عندهم من المصدر وهو المفعول المطلق عندهم ، وهو غير تلك الأمثلة المأخوذة عنه، فكيف يستقيم قوله : ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فصار مؤخر كلامه مناقضا أول كلامه، فإنه حكى عنهم في أول كلامه الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، ثم قال في آخر كلامه ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الأمرين، وهذا هو التناقض الظاهر، وإنما ثبت الاتحاد بين ما هو حقيقة الفعل عند أهل الكلام وهو المصدر لا عندهم وبين ما هو مفعول في صناعتهم دون أهل الكلام، وما هو المفعول عندهم ليس بمفعول حقيقة عند أهل الكلام، فإذا لم يثبت الاتحاد بهذا بين ما
They count that as a verb, rather they count it as a noun, and for this it is distinguished by the markers of nouns, namely the entry of the definite article (lam al-ta'rif), or nunation (tanwin), or construct state (idafa), without the markers of verbs which are 'qad' and 'sawfa', and it entered into what is from the definition of the noun according to them, which is that which indicates a meaning not coupled with a determined time, or a word indicating a meaning by reference (dalalat al-ishara) without predication (ifada), and it exited from the definition of verbs according to them, which is that which indicates something else. And what is the act (al-fi'l) according to the people of kalam is not an act according to the people of language. So how can you oblige your opponents to accept their statement? Then he called the verbal nouns (masadir) 'events of the nouns' (ahdath al-asma'), so 'Zayd's hitting', 'Khalid's eating', and 'Muhammad's drinking' became events belonging to them. Do you say that these meanings occurred from them? If you say yes, then you have made events for the servants. If you say no, then you have contradicted the people of language. Then his statement at the end of this chapter: 'Verily, according to them the object (al-maf'ul) in reality is the verbal noun (al-masdar).' Then they say: 'object with the verb' (maf'ul bihi), 'object of purpose' (maf'ul lahu), 'object of place/time' (maf'ul fihi), and 'object of accompaniment' (maf'ul ma'ahu) and they do not exceed that. And his saying: 'What has been taken from them of the distinction between the verb and the object, from its beginning to its end, is corrupt, contradictory speech in which he fell due to his ignorance of the specifications of the people of language, and we will explain that.' So we say: His statement that according to them the object in reality is the verbal noun — this is something the Basrans say, and al-Farra' disagreed with them in it, saying: 'The verbal noun is not an object; rather it is a verb, and the object is that which the verb is attached to, not the verb itself.' So this [Basran position] is not accepted because of al-Farra's opposition to them, and he is one of the leaders of the people of language. Then we say: The object according to them is not what is the object in reality; likewise the subject (al-fa'il) and likewise the verb. Rather, the verb according to them is what has passed, and the subject according to them is that to which is attributed what is the verb according to them, which is the spoken-of thing (al-muhaddath 'anhu), not the one who truly acted. And the object is that to which what is the verb according to them is not attributed, and it emerges as a subject from being spoken-of. For if you say: 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr', Zayd according to them and 'Amr are both objects. And if you say: 'Khalid died', 'the boy grew tall', 'the hair turned black', and 'the snow turned white', the elevated status of each noun in that is because it is a subject, and there is no doubt that the verb did not occur from any one of them, but because it was spoken-of and attributed to it what is the verb in their craft, it is counted as a subject. And what according to them exits from being spoken-of and is not attributed to it is an object, even if no verb occurs upon it. The verbal noun according to the Basrans was an absolute object (maf'ul mutlaq) because what is the verb was not attributed to it and it was not spoken-of about it, so it became according to them, by the requirement of their craft, an object, not that it is an object in reality — as if you say: 'I worshipped God Almighty,' the vertical form (intisab) of your saying 'God' is because it is an object, and likewise if you say: 'So-and-so did not worship God,' and the reality of the verb does not occur upon God Almighty — exalted is God above that. So when what is the verb according to the people of kalam was an object according to the Basran people of language — as for what is the verb according to the people of language, it is not an object according to them, and as for what is an object according to them, it is not a verb at all. And none of this relates to what we are in, and by this his statement is invalidated: 'What has been taken from them of the distinction between the verb and the object.' For the people of language made the verb to be the paradigms (amthila) which we mentioned, and they are taken according to them from the verbal noun, which is the absolute object according to them, and it is other than those paradigms taken from it. So how can his statement 'what has been taken from them of the distinction between the verb and the object' be sound? So the later part of his speech became contradictory to the beginning of his speech, for he narrated from them at the beginning of his speech the distinction between the verb and the object, then he said at the end of his speech what has been taken from them of the distinction between the two matters, and this is clear contradiction. And only unity is established between what is the reality of the verb according to the people of kalam — which is the verbal noun, not according to them — and between what is an object in their craft, not according to the people of kalam. And what is the object according to them is not an object in reality according to the people of kalam. So when the unity is not established by this between what

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who conflates linguistic (grammatical) and theological definitions of 'act' (fi'l) and 'object/patient' (maf'ul). The author identifies the opponent's mistake and demonstrates its self-contradiction.

The passage begins by noting that what theologians call an act (fi'l) is actually classified as a noun (ism) by grammarians, because it takes the markers of nouns (definite article, nunation, construct state) and not the markers of verbs (qad, sawfa). Thus, the theological definition of 'act' differs completely from the grammatical one. The opponent, however, tries to force his theological opponents to accept linguistic categories, which is fallacious.

The author then cites the opponent's claim that the 'true object' (al-maf'ul fi al-haqiqa) is the verbal noun (masdar). The author notes that this is a Basran grammatical position, but it was opposed by al-Farra', a leading Kufan grammarian, who argued that the masdar is a verb, not an object. So even within grammar, the matter is not settled.

Next, the author explains that grammarians do not define 'subject' (fa'il), 'object' (maf'ul), and 'verb' (fi'l) according to real-world action and reception. Instead, the 'subject' is simply the word to which the 'verb' (in their conventional sense) is attributed, regardless of whether any real action occurred. Examples illustrate this: in 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr', both Zayd and 'Amr are called 'objects' grammatically, even though the hitting did not happen. In 'Khalid died', 'the boy grew tall', etc., the nouns are called 'subjects' (fa'il) even though no action issued from them. Thus, grammatical categories do not reflect theological reality.

The author then applies this to the opponent's claim: since the masdar is not attributed to any subject in the grammatical sense, it is conventionally called an 'object' (maf'ul mutlaq) by Basrans. But this does not make it a true object in theological terms. The author gives the example of 'I worshipped God': even if one says 'So-and-so did not worship God', the word 'God' is still grammatically an object, yet no act actually occurs upon God. So the theological 'act' (what really occurs) is grammatically an 'object', not an 'act'. This shows that the opponent's reliance on grammatical distinctions is invalid.

Finally, the author points out the opponent's self-contradiction: he first claimed that grammarians distinguish between verb and object, but then claimed that nothing was taken from them on this distinction. His own words are inconsistent. The attempted unity between the grammatical object and the theological act fails, because the grammatical object is not a real object in theology. Thus, the opponent's argument is refuted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That grammatical categories directly correspond to theological realities.
Correction: The passage shows that what theologians call an act is linguistically a noun, and linguistic verbs are different.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say 'How can you oblige your opponents to accept their statement?'
A) Because the opponent's own definition is wrong.
B) Because the theological definition of act differs from the linguistic one.
C) Because the opponent is not a linguist.
D) Because the opponent uses Basran grammar.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic and theological definitions of 'act' are distinct; one cannot impose linguistic categories on theological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you used a common Arabic word (like 'iman', 'kufr', or 'amal') in a theological discussion. Did you assume its linguistic meaning was the same as its theological meaning? How can this lesson's distinction help you avoid confusion in the future? Commit to checking the technical definition before using such terms.
?
When you debate theological matters with others, do you ever feel pressured to accept someone's argument because it sounds linguistically correct? How will you train yourself to identify when a category difference (like between linguistic and theological 'act') is being ignored? Write down one concrete step you will take.
?
The author rebukes the opponent: 'So how can you oblige your opponents to accept their statement?' Have you ever insisted on a linguistic point as if it were theologically binding? Examine your own tendency to impose categories. What will you do when you catch yourself doing that next time?
